# 道德真经藏室纂微篇

**Daoist Classic *Zhenjing Cangshu Zuanwei* (Selections from the Treasury of the Daoist *Zhenjing*)
Chapter Title: *Zhenjing Cangshu Mùwéi* (Selections from the Treasury of the Daoist *Zhenjing* on Reverence for the Subtle)
Compiled by **Bìxū Zǐ Chén Jǐngyuán** of the **Song** dynasty. Ten volumes.

According to **Yáng Zhònggēng’s preface**, this work was published in the year **Bǎobǎo 6** of the **Dàodé Zhōng** school (1258 CE). The original source is the *Zhèngtǒng Dàozàng* (Orthodox Daoist Canon), under the *Dòngshén* (Cavernous Deities) section, in the **Jùjié** (Jade Precepts) category.

---

**Volume One**
Compiled by **Bìxū Zǐ Chén Jǐngyuán**

**Daoist Scripture (Upper Section)**

The **Dao/Way** is expounded in terms of **Chángdào** (the Eternal Dao). The **Chángdào** is the **void-hearted** (emptiness of the mind) that awaits all things.

The Dao, when it can be spoken of, is **not** the **Chángdào**. The Dao that can be articulated is subject to change, to transmigration, to speech and explanation—this is called **teachings and precepts**. How is it different from dross? Let us examine it by elucidating:

The Dao is **the Way of universal passage** (通). All things obtain it, and thus nothing is impassable.
It is also called **the Way of traversal** (蹈), taking the path as its metaphor.
The *Shuōwén* (Explanations of Script and Characters) states: **"One path reaching through is called Dao."** The ancient sages commanded that it be embodied and practiced, hence it is called **Dao**.

As for **virtue/power** (德) such as *rén* (benevolence), *yì* (righteousness), *lǐ* (propriety), *zhì* (wisdom), and *xìn* (faithfulness), these are all **applications of the Dao**. When applied, it is called **the Dao that can be spoken of**.

Once the **Dao that can be spoken of** is made manifest, it is **no longer the Chángdào**. The **Chángdào** is the spontaneous and natural, responding to sensation and transforming without end. It cannot be transmitted by words, nor can it be sought by intellect—only by embodying and merging with the **transformative power** (造化), containing the luminous light and hiding its radiance, acting without action yet accomplishing all things, silently attaining its utmost.

**Yán Jùnpíng** (严君平) said: The **Dao that can be spoken of** is the **Dao of virtue/power** made manifest, yet it is **not** the natural Dao.

Those who practice today do not carry torches by day, for the sun is bright. The sun’s brightness is the **Dao that is not spoken of**, eternal in its constancy. Carrying torches is the **Dao that can be spoken of**, made manifest.

What is inscribed on bamboo and silk, carved into metal and stone, and can be passed on to others—this is the **Dao that can be spoken of**. If it can be passed on yet cannot be received, if it can be obtained yet not seen, if it is the **self-originating root** (自本自根), before Heaven and Earth existed, since ancient times yet eternally preserved—then the **Dao of the Cavernous Deities** (神鬼、神帝), the **Dao that generates Heaven and Earth**, is the **Chángdào**.

The **five-thousand-word essence** (五千文) is unfolded from these few words, truly the **mysterious within the mysterious**, the **divine within the divine**.

**Naming and the Dao/Way**:
The Dao, when it can be named, is **not** the **Chángdào**.
The Dao is **embodiment** (体). Naming is **application** (用). Application arises from embodiment, and naming comes from the Dao. Once its name is marked, it can be applied. The application being established, it is called **the Dao that can be named**.

Once the **Dao that can be named** is applied, it is **no longer the Chángdào**. The **Chángdào** is the **name of application without bounds**, fragmenting its virtue/power.

**Yán Jùnpíng** said: The **Dao that can be named** is the **Dao of virtue/power** made manifest, yet it is **not** the innate truth.

**Lì Zǐ** (列子) dwelled in the **Jìng** (郑) garden for forty years, and no one recognized him. The king and ministers treated him like the common folk—this is truly the **Dao of guarding the Chángdào**.

The **beginning of Heaven and Earth** is **nameless** (无名). The **nameless** refers to the Dao. Before all transformations, there was no way to force a name upon it. When the **marvelous origin** (妙本) first descended, the **primordial simplicity** (浑沦之朴) was about to depart, and then the **Great Polarity** (太极) of the *Yì* (易) emerged, giving rise to the **Two Principles** (两仪).

**Yán Jùnpíng** said: The **nameless** is **without traces** (无朕), merging with the divine embodiment, the world relying upon it yet unaware of its transformation from **voidness** (虚无), making it the **beginning of Heaven and Earth**—this is the **embodiment of the Dao**.

The **named** (有名) is the **mother of all things**. The **named** refers to **Heaven and Earth**. Heaven’s bestowal and Earth’s transformation nurture all things. They shelter and harm them like a mother nurturing her children. Hence it is called the **named**, the **mother of all things**.

The **Great Dao** is mysterious and remote—how could it be confined to the **named** or the **nameless**? The sage **simplifies application to establish teachings**, thereby clarifying the **similarities and differences** of the **origin and traces**.

All **Dao that is renewed daily** is said to be **nameless**. Hence, the **beginning of all things** is the **nameless**. The **completion of all things** is the **named**.

**Yán Jùnpíng** said: The **named** is the **Dao of transformation**. Revering the Dao and valuing the divine, following the **Great Harmony** (太和), then Heaven and Earth become the **mother of all things**—this is the **application of the Dao**.

**Always without desire**, one may observe its **mystery** (妙);
**Always with desire**, one may observe its **boundaries** (徼).

**Desire** (欲) is the **pursuit of sensory realms** that generates the mind.
**Mystery** (妙) is the **essence of the utmost subtlety**.
**Boundary** (徼) is the **edge and corner**.

The **Great Dao** has small paths at its boundaries—these are called **boundaries**. It is also **return**.
The **Dao of voidness** (虚无) is **still and unmoving**—this is called **without desire**.
When it **senses and gives birth to all things**, it is called **with desire**.
**Without desire** is observing the **mystery** while guarding the **voidness**.
**With desire** is observing the **boundaries**—this is called **retaining thought** (存思).

It has been said that **true constancy** (真常) is the **Great Dao** itself.
**Without desire** and **with desire** are the **Dao’s applications**.
The Dao originally has **no form**—**form** arises when the Dao is sensed and generated.
What is **above form** is called the Dao.
What is **below form** is called **instrumentality** (器).

The **superior person** (上士) knows the **subtle** and the **manifest**, seeing what is **not yet formed** and already exhausting its **mystery**—hence, **always without desire**, they observe its **mystery**.
The **middle person** (中士) follows and adapts to things, seeing their **clusters and masses** (群村), and thus obtains their **application**—hence, though there are **ten thousand differences**, the **true principle** (真理) is unfathomable, but they merely **glimpse its boundaries**.

Another elucidation states:
If a person **always embodies the subtle essence of the Great Dao**, guards the **key to stillness** (清静之要), and returns to the **extreme of life and vitality** (性命之极), not being seduced by external things, then their **intentions and will** (志意) become **pure and vast** (虚澹), able to observe the **Dao’s utmost mystery** and the **Dao’s ultimate boundaries**.

**Yán Jùnpíng** said:
When the **mind is like metal or stone** (金石), the **body is like the wood of the *jú* tree** (桔木), and one **silently dwells in the corners** (隅隅), with **intentions as gentle as a colt** (驹犊)—this is the **person without desire**, returning to the **root of life and vitality**.

The person with desire consumes the boundaries of things and loses the path of ease and openness.
They see only the petty edges of the net, lost and unable to return, forfeiting the true origin.

Some ancient sages said that those without desire embody the Dao inwardly, observing transformation without attachment to any form or trace, forgetting their own origins.

Those with desire, however, arise from the root and apply its use to what can be spoken of—establishing teachings to respond to things, accomplishing the salvation of myriad affairs. They perceive the end of things and know their return, first speaking of restraining oneself, then of transforming the people. Cultivating oneself and governing the state—these two are the same.

In principle, nothing is lacking. These two are the same—what can be spoken of and what can be named, the unnamed and the named, desirelessness and desire. All are contained within the still, motionless, and profound source, where essence and function remain indistinct, and good and evil are both extinguished. Hence, they are said to be the same.

Yet when they manifest, they take on different names. By "manifest," it means descending from the root to expound what can be spoken of in gradual stages. Though originating from one mind, the applications of the Five Constants differ, and the wise and foolish vary and transform. Lifespans, fortune, and existence all differ—each takes on its own name.

The same is called the Mysterious. The Mysterious again, the Mysterious—this is profound and subtle, obscure and natural. What is called "natural" is the Way.

It is said that both the unnamed and the named, desirelessness and desire, all receive *qi* from Heaven and examine nature’s essence. Pure and impure, honored and humiliated, forms take on myriad shapes—this arises from the Mysterious again, the Mysterious, the Divine again, the Divine. This is what is called "naturally so."

If one penetrates and comprehends this profound subtlety, piercing the silent and obscure, this is called possessing *Xuándé* (玄德). It is the gateway to all marvels—meaning the realm of the Dao.

The Great Dao is vast and unbounded, with no barriers or confines, no doors or walls—it is boundless and resplendent. When speaking of self-cultivation, outwardly it aligns with the common people, inwardly it becomes the wondrous root. When teaching, the myriad truths emerge from subtlety, and the sages contain subtlety within. Transformation and guidance have no fixed method; profound and singular, it stands alone and unchanging—this is called *Zhīcháng* (知常).

Since there are ways of emergence and return, it is called the *Gate*. Zhuangzi said:
*"There is birth and there is death,
There is exit and there is entry.
Exit and entry without seeing its form—
This is called the Heavenly Gate."*

The Heavenly Gate is the state of *wu* (無有, non-being).
All things emerge from non-being—
This is also called the gateway to all marvels.

Only the Marvelous Dao gives rise to all things.
What gives rise to all things is surely the Marvelous Dao.
Those who use the Dao are surely the sages.
The sage’s Dao renews itself daily, its applications boundless—
Hence, it is called *Cháng* (常, the Constant).

Emergence and retreat have boundaries;
Desire for difference leads to action—
Hence, it is called *kě* (可, permissible).

What can be spoken of must be named;
What is named must be known—
Hence, it follows with *"All under Heaven know."*

All under Heaven know beauty as beauty—
Then ugliness already exists.
All know goodness as goodness—
Then ungoodness already exists.

Beauty and goodness arise from deluded emotions.
What one’s emotions favor becomes beauty;
What one’s emotions reject becomes ugliness.
If one indulges deluded emotions, what is not ugliness?

What one takes as right becomes goodness;
What one takes as wrong becomes ungoodness.
If one indulges in one’s own right and wrong,
How can there be true beauty?

Zhuangzi said:
*"Is and is-not—this is what I call emotions.
What I call without-emotion is:
People do not harm themselves with their likes and dislikes,
Always following nature without artificial enhancement.
This is called stopping at what is truly fitting."*

The sage is not without beauty and goodness—
Rather, they possess it without boasting,
Thus aligning with non-being.
Not boasting preserves virtue intact;
Aligning with non-being keeps harm at bay.
Virtue preserved, harm distant—
Then beauty and goodness are complete.

If one uses beauty and goodness to transform the world,
Making them know beauty as beauty—
This is not yet perfect goodness.

The *Classic* says:
*"The highest virtue does not cling to virtue;
The lower virtue does not lose virtue."*
This is correct.

Moreover, good people are few under Heaven,
While ungood people are many.
If one corrects their governance and secretly mocks them,
This becomes evil.

Yán Jùnpíng said:
*"The bright does not always endure;
The obscure does not always vanish.
Honor and decay begin in late spring,
Wild plants and grains sprout at the autumn equinox.
On the winter solstice, all things flourish;
On the summer solstice, all things wither.
It is said that prosperity must yield to decline,
And beauty must yield to ugliness.
Even yin and yang follow this—
How much more so for humans?"*

This is a warning against those who boast in beauty and goodness.

Thus, being and non-being give rise to each other,
Difficulty and ease complete each other,
Length and shortness define each other,
High and low incline against each other,
Sounds harmonize with each other,
Front and back follow each other.

These six principles, when one boasts in beauty and goodness, lead to action.
Once action is manifest, partial fixation arises,
And mutual destruction and harm emerge—
Things lose their nature.
Hence, it concludes with the sage’s *wuwei*,
And *Xuándé* does not depart.

The nature of being and non-being
Originally does not give rise to each other.
Yet it is said that being must emerge from non-being,
And non-being must arise from being—
Hence, *"being and non-being give rise to each other."*

The principle of difficulty and ease
Originally does not complete each other.
For instance, the potter finds clay easy to shape
But wood difficult to work.
The carpenter finds wood easy to shape
But altars difficult to construct.
If one tries to make the easy difficult,
It will not succeed.
If each sticks to their craft,
It will be accomplished—
Hence, *"difficulty and ease complete each other."*

Length and shortness
Originally do not define each other.
Seeing a crane, one knows the hare’s legs are short;
Observing a hare, one knows the crane’s legs are long.
Things follow this pattern—
Hence, *"length and shortness define each other."*

High and low
Originally do not incline against each other.
If one lacks sufficient rank,
Then the desire for usurpation arises.
If one is high but not arrogant,
Then none will seek to humble them.
If one is low but not abased,
Then none will seek to exalt them.
If one aspires and envies, striving to surpass others,
Then all will topple each other—
Hence, *"high and low incline against each other."*

The resonance of sounds,
Originally does not harmonize.
Like the natural music of heaven,
It hums and ceases on its own.
Yet the world claims that tones and rhythms,
Qi and melodies combine to form songs,
To harmonize and hum together—
Hence, *"sounds harmonize."*

Before and after,
Originally do not follow in sequence.
Like day and night succeeding each other,
Yesterday is before today,
Today is after yesterday.
Again, like the path of forward and backward steps—
Lifting the foot, lowering the foot,
Which comes first, which comes after?
It shifts and changes.
Jun Ping said:
Without the void, there is no way to perceive existence;
With the void, form takes shape.
Difficulty cannot reveal ease,
Ease cannot reveal difficulty.
An inch shortens a foot,
A foot lengthens an inch.
Mountains collapse through valleys,
Valleys tilt through mountains.
Sounds distinguish tones,
Tones pause through sounds.
What comes first is seen through what follows,
What follows is understood through what precedes.
Thus, without the void, existence cannot be seen;
Without existence, the void cannot be known.
Without difficulty, ease cannot be known;
Without ease, difficulty cannot be known.
Without length, shortness cannot be known;
Without shortness, length cannot be known.
Without mountains, valleys cannot be known;
Without valleys, mountains cannot be known.
Without sounds, tones cannot be known;
Without tones, sounds cannot be known.
Without what comes first, what follows cannot be known;
Without what follows, what comes first cannot be known.

All these things—
The proofs of Heaven and Earth,
The symbols of Nature—
Are laid out, exposed, and slow to change.
Affirmation and negation follow each other,
Beginning and end reverse and repeat,
Unseparable, beyond evasion by the numinous,
Unopposed by yin and yang.

From this, it is seen:
The affairs of the Sage-King
Cannot be achieved through contrived action.

Thus, the Sage acts in *wuwei*,
Practices the teaching of *non-speech*.
This speaks of the ancient Sage-Kings,
Who did not govern the world through beauty and virtue,
Yet the world governed itself.
*Wuwei* is not idle inaction or silent withdrawal—
It is the forgetting of beauty and virtue,
The annihilation of emotion, the return to true nature,
Where the people act according to their abilities,
Things settle into their proper order,
And none disturb the harmony above or below.
Thus, the teaching of *non-speech* is carried out—
By leading with one’s own conduct,
Rectifying one’s bearing to enlighten things,
Acting in accordance with the times,
Utilizing resources to achieve merit,
And aligning with the principle of *forgetting speech*.

All things act without complaint.
*Action* is movement.
The Sage governs the world with *youshou* (nurturing),
Doing nothing, yet nothing is undone—
Thus the people till and eat,
Weave and clothe themselves,
Rest content in their homes,
And play with full bellies.
They take joy in their natural inclinations,
All drinking from the transformation of *wuwei*.
Thus the Sage entrusts them without complaint.
The people can be led along a path,
But cannot be made to know its purpose.
This is the wind of *upper virtue not claiming virtue*.

To give birth without possession,
To act without reliance,
To complete deeds without taking credit—
All things arise and transform on their own,
Standing alone and self-so,
Not claimed as one’s own.
All creatures pursue their nature,
Each adapting to its kind,
Not relying on their own virtue.
When deeds are accomplished and affairs settled,
The Way permeates all things,
The mind wanders in the Maoshu Mountains,
Not lording over the people.
This is the complete virtue of the Sage.

Only by not claiming
Does one not depart.
The Sage’s deeds are like those of Creation,
Allowing all things to reach their utmost,
Forgetting name and self.
Not claiming means not taking position as one’s own,
Thus the people are unaware, unknowing—
Hence, their Way does not perish,
Their virtue does not fade.

*The Honglie Explanation* says:
When the Chu did not appoint Zi Fa to attack Cai,
He was praised, and King Xuan of Chu welcomed him at the border,
Granting him a hundred *mu* of land and sealing him with a jade tablet.
Zi Fa declined, saying:
*"To govern a state and establish policies,
To receive vassal lords as guests—
This is the virtue of the ruler.
To issue commands and the enemy flees before battle—
This is the might of the general.
To array the troops and defeat the enemy—
This is the strength of the common people.
To ride upon the people’s labor and take their rank and rewards—
This is not the Way of ren and yi.*
Thus he declined and did not accept it.
This is *completing deeds without claiming them.*"

Knowledge brings the mutual birth of good and evil,
The incline of high and low cannot be escaped.
Difficulty and ease stand opposed,
The talents of long and short cannot be concealed.
When long and short are revealed, the worthy are praised—
Hence, it is followed by *not praising the worthy*.
Not praising the worthy prevents the people from contending.
If the ruler is humble, yielding, and tranquil,
Not boasting of his own virtue,
Then the people’s transformation follows like wind bending grass,
And who would dare to contend?
*The Classic* says:
*"I do nothing, yet the people transform themselves;
I love stillness, yet the people rectify themselves."*

Another explanation says:
If the ruler is tranquil,
The ministers are enlightened,
Punishments do not spare the noble,
Grace does not exclude the lowly,
The worthy and unworthy each fulfill their roles—
Then the scholars will not contend.
But if the ruler is indecisive,
The ministers are flattering,
Though they praise the worthy and seek talent,
Their outward loyalty hides inner deceit,
And those with poisonous hearts and harmonious words arrive—
Then deceit arises and contention spreads.

Jun Ping said:
The Sage is the master of great virtue,
The commoner is the servant of lesser virtue.
The worthy are not one in ten thousand,
The Sage does not appear in every age.
Heaven’s creation of the worthy and unworthy
Is not by praising them.

Again, it is said:
Like driving a poor horse and a fine steed together on a level road,
Or letting a wild goose and a quail fly side by side in the blue sky—
Then the worthy and unworthy become clear.
This is natural, not by praising them.

Not valuing rare treasures
Prevents the people from stealing.
Rare treasures are gold, jade, pearls, and rhinoceros horn.
If the ruler’s transformation is pure and tranquil,
The people focus on farming and learning,
Hiding gold in the mountains,
Discarding pearls in the depths,
Finding no profit in wealth,
No attraction in nobility—
Then the people will have no heart for theft.
Another explanation says:
The black dragon’s night-light pearls,
Gold, jade, brocade, and embroidery—
If the ruler does not value them,
The people will not covet them,
And where would thieves come from?
The saying goes:
*"If a person does not desire it,
Even if rewarded, they will not steal."*

*The Honglie Explanation* says:
Citing the laws of Shennong:
*"If a man is strong yet does not plow,
There will be those who starve in the world."*

When women reach adulthood yet remain uneducated,
The world will have those who suffer from cold.

Thus, the ruler himself plows,
His wife weaves,
Setting an example for all under heaven.
This is how he guides the people—
By not valuing rare treasures,
By making desirable things unseen,
So that the people’s hearts remain undisturbed.

Desirable things are those external objects
That bewilder the mind,
Giving rise to covetous and lustful desires.
Yet "not seeing" does not mean distant isolation—
It means not letting perception become perception,
But embracing *wuwei* instead.

If the ruler beholds external things
Yet harbors no covetous or lustful desires,
Then he is untouched by the defilements of form,
And his original nature returns to purity and tranquility—
To its true, singular essence.

When the ruler preserves this true singularity,
The people’s hearts remain undisturbed,
And the winds of simplicity and sincerity can flourish.

**Jun Ping** says:
If the world does not honor the worthy,
The people will not strive,
And without striving, there will be no contention,
And without contention, there will be no disorder.
If the world does not value wealth,
The people will not desire it,
And without desire, there will be no seeking,
And without seeking, there will be no theft.
If the world abandons the pleasures of music and dance,
The people will have no joy,
And without joy, there will be no licentiousness.
This is the natural order of things.

*The Honglie Explanation* says:
**Ordering Yin Zi Pei** to drink with King Zhuang of Chu,
The king agreed.
Zi Pei stood with loose sleeves, facing north in the hall,
Thinking: *"The king once granted this, yet now does not fulfill it—
Perhaps I have erred?"*

The king replied:
*"I have heard of your Strong Terrace.
That terrace overlooks the Liang Mountains,
Facing the vast expanse,
With the river to its left and the Huai to its right—
Its pleasures make one forget life and death.
A man of my meager virtue cannot bear such delights.
I fear if I linger, I may never return.
Thus, the saying: 'Make desirable things unseen,
So that the heart remains undisturbed.'"*

**Transmission of Yi’s *Yin Yi*** says:
The ancient text reads *"make the people’s hearts undisturbed,"*
But the He Shang Gong *Kaoyuan* edition reads *"make the heart undisturbed,"*
Which is also acceptable.

Thus, the Sage’s governance begins with emptying the heart—
First purifying oneself,
Then extending to family and state.
Emptying the heart means harboring no wicked thoughts.
Not forcing the mind to chase after darkness,
Yet inwardly quelling desires,
Dispelling all attachments,
So that the mind naturally settles—
This is emptiness.

As Zhuangzi says:
*"In an empty chamber, white purity arises—
A blessing of stillness.
Stillness means the heart is empty,
And pristine whiteness brings fortune to dwell."*

**Filling the belly.**
The Sage’s virtue and power internally suffuse,
The five spirits remain tranquil,
Contentment is self-sufficient,
And no attachment to wealth or love arises—
Thus, *"filling the belly."*

**Weakening the will.**
The will is the mind’s affair.
What the mind pursues is called will.
To act with the mind,
One must uphold humility, stillness, gentleness, and weakness—
Then the Dao is complete.

**Strengthening the bones.**
Bones are the body’s framework.
When purity and harmony are sufficient,
The body is nourished,
And when vital essence is robust,
The bones grow strong—
This too is natural law.

**Always keeping the people in ignorance and desirelessness.**
The Sage constantly cultivates himself, emptying his heart,
So that the people may return to simplicity and sincerity,
And transform naturally with joy.

The saying goes:
*"If one rectifies himself,
What need has he for governance?"*
Thus, even those who possess knowledge or cunning,
Or who adorn themselves with deceit,
Are already influenced by the winds of purity and sincerity,
Their hearts grow dull,
Their bodies wither—
They dare not stir,
And all desires for action fade.

**To act with *wuwei* is to govern perfectly.**
To act with *wuwei* means to follow the Way of non-action.
Non-action means not exceeding one’s natural endowment.
If one does not exceed nature,
Then natural order is preserved,
And all actions become non-action.

When all things act without contrivance,
The hearts of covetousness and seeking vanish entirely,
And the winds of simplicity prevail—
Who could say governance fails?

To honor one’s own worth and ability makes the people follow,
But to indulge in contention and theft stirs the desires that breed chaos.
Only the Sage governs through emptiness and openness,
Thus he follows with the *chong* of the Dao.

The *chong* of the Dao,
When applied, is never full.
*"Full"* in the ancient text means *"satisfied."*
*Chong* means emptiness.
It also means centrality—
An indefinite term,
And also constancy.
The Dao’s use lies in its emptiness—
Harmonious *qi* is empty and open,
Thus it flows universally.
The ruler who attains this emptiness
Loses his wealth in a thousand chariots,
While the common man, attaining it,
Forgets his poverty in a single room.
Though seated in the temple halls,
His heart is as still as in a mountain cave;
Though dressed in coarse rags,
He does not differ from the adorned in fine silks—
This is the profound attainment of emptiness,
And the absence of fullness.

**Jun Ping** says:
Those who *do* emptiness are not empty,
Those who *do* harmony are not harmonious.
Only by *not* doing emptiness and harmony
Does one truly attain emptiness and harmony.
Emptiness takes emptiness as its dwelling,
Harmony takes non-action as its home.
To be able to empty and to be non-action—
This is the excess of emptiness,
To be able to be non-action and to empty—
This is the constancy of harmony.
Thus, one truly attains the Great Dao,
The use of emptiness and harmony without fullness.

The central way lies in harmony.
Some dare not establish it—
This refers to the Dao itself.

Deep, like the source of all things.
*Deep* means profound stillness.
*Fu* is the rhythmic intonation of Daoist chant.
Understanding this,
The Dao of emptiness is neither deficient nor excessive,
Its nature is concentrated and serene—
Deep, unfathomable—
Thus it is called *deep*.

The logic of the unfathomable
Is neither being nor non-being,
Difficult to name,
Thus it is spoken of metaphorically.
Daily life flows naturally,
Like water in a vessel—
Serene and profound,
Encompassing all phenomena,
Preserving both good and evil,
Nurturing all movement and growth—
Thus it is the source of all things.

**Dulling the sharp, unraveling the tangled.**
*Dulling* means restraining.
It also means breaking the edge—
*Sharp* refers to keen ambition,
And also to forward momentum.
*Unraveling* means dispersing,
*Tangled* means excessive agitation.
Those who advance eagerly for fame and gain
Should reflect on the Dao’s emptiness,
Restrain their greed and delusion,
And not exalt themselves.

「Yet some rage with arrogance, galloping in pursuit of fame,
Their inner and outer selves in turmoil—
But those who grasp the Dao’s profound stillness
Can loosen bonds and disperse agitation,
Serene and clear, restoring their true nature.
*Agitation* (纷), Master He Shang of the Riverbank, renders as *resentment* (忿), meaning "a knot of grudges."
When one harbors resentment and hatred, bound like iron chains,
Yet holds the Dao’s emptiness and harmony in mind,
All dissolves—like ice melting in the sun.

*Harmonizing its light*, *blending with the dust*—
Serene, yet as if something remains.
The Dao of ultimate emptiness
Employs harmony and balance in its operations,
And its applications know no limits.

In light, it harmonizes—
Present yet without distinction.
In dust, it blends—
Coexisting yet without separation.

When responding to phenomena, it merges with light and dust,
When returning to its root, it remains untouched and serene—
Trace its wondrous origin, and it vanishes into emptiness,
Yet its actions are manifest and real—
Thus it is said: Serene, yet as if something remains.

I do not know whose child I am,
Yet I precede the likeness of the Emperor.
*I* refers to the Old Master’s self-designation.
*Likeness* means resemblance.
I observe the Dao’s unfathomable depths,
Its boundless, unmeasurable operations,
Crafting all things.

The Dao endures through ages past and present,
Having neither ancestor nor master—
Who dares claim descent or lineage?
It seems as though, through profound contemplation,
One might suppose I emerged before the Celestial Emperor.

If one employs the Dao’s emptiness and balance,
Then the *deep* (渊) is never full,
And blending with light and dust,
Thus it becomes the source of all things.

Seeking its source, it precedes Heaven and Earth—
Hence the mention of Heaven and Earth.

Heaven and Earth are not kind,
Yet they treat all things as fodder for sacrifice.
The Sage is not kind,
Yet he treats the people as fodder for sacrifice.
*Fodder* (刍) means grass—
Bound into bundles for ritual offerings.
In ancient times, it was used for sacrifices to avert misfortune,
Then discarded afterward.
When used, they did not cherish it greatly,
And when abandoned, they did not grieve its loss.
Thus, fodder for sacrifice arises from divine purpose,
Yet the divine has no need for the fodder.
No need means no partiality,
No partiality means no favoritism—
Hence, Heaven and Earth have no favoritism,
Yet great benevolence arises,
The Sage is not kind,
Yet Heaven’s kindness is fulfilled.

*Not kind* means lacking emotional attachment to benevolence,
Not neglecting what is evil.
Thus, Heaven and Earth, lacking sentiment,
View all things as fodder for sacrifice,
Demanding no gratitude in return.
The Sage, lacking sentiment,
Views the people as fodder for sacrifice,
Demanding no gratitude in return.
This is the natural way of Heaven and Earth.

Master Rong Ping said:
Heaven is high and clear, Earth is thick and yielding—
The interplay of Yin and Yang,
Balanced *qi* flowing,
Naturally effortless, yet all things arise.

Heaven and Earth do not strain their hearts or minds,
Nor exhaust their spirits in laborious deeds,
Nor weep in excessive affection,
Nor bestow favors with calculated gain,
Nor shower blessings to demand repayment—
Yet all things are nurtured and fulfilled.
This is what is meant by:
Heaven and Earth are not kind,
Yet they treat all things as fodder for sacrifice.

Enlightened kings and sagely rulers
Hold the Dao’s unity and harmony,
Their clarity and stillness unchanging,
Yet they adapt in all transformations—
Divine and effortless,
Their actions aligned with the Dao,
The world responds, all things naturally evolve.

The Sage does not exhaust his wisdom or abilities,
Nor disturb his heart with restless schemes,
Nor drain his vital essence in ceaseless toil,
Nor fixate on endless calculations,
Nor flaunt his benevolence in public displays,
Nor lead the people with contrived affection—
Yet virtue spreads universally,
And the realm unites in peace,
His kindness reaches to all corners,
His deeds are fully accomplished—
For what is the art of benevolence and kindness?

Things rely on their allotted measure for transformation,
Yet the Dao’s boundless operations
Grant measure to the boundless,
Endowing it with inexhaustible power—
If they do not sustain each other,
It is like a river drying up,
And the land withering in its wake.
*Again it is said:*
Even lice stirring on the hair
Cause sleep to be unsettled.
Mosquitoes settling on the skin
Distract the mind,
And obstruct clear thought.
External harm from venomous insects
Pierces the heart with pain,
Yet the damage ends at the ears and eyes,
And all limbs grow sluggish.
This shows that affection and gain
Stored in the chest
Cannot but bring strain, imbalance, and depletion of vital *qi*.

Excessive words lead to exhaustion,
Better to uphold the Center.
*Excessive words* refers to excessive talk of universal love—
Too much leads to difficulty in execution,
Thus, words run dry, bending under strain,
Yet if one follows Heaven and Earth’s emptiness and stillness,
And the *bell bellows* (橐钥)’s heartless nature,
Embracing the Center’s harmony,
All will naturally suffice.
It is also the teaching of *non-action* (不言之教).

The *Honglie Explanation* (《鸿烈解》) says:
Master Wang Shou carried his books while walking,
And encountered Xu Feng in Zhou.
Xu Feng said:
*Action* (事) arises from responding to change,
Change comes from the times,
Thus, those who know the times act without fixed patterns.
*Books* are the source of words.
Words come from those who know,
And those who know hide their books.
Thus, Master Wang Shou burned his books
And danced, saying:
Excessive words lead to exhaustion,
Better to uphold the Center.

Heaven and Earth move and rest without partiality,
Their essence containing emptiness yet unyielding—
This is due to their *spirit* (神).
Hence, it follows with *the spirit of the valley* (谷神).

The *spirit of the valley* does not die,
This is called the *mysterious female* (玄牝).
The Great Person regards the *Great Emptiness* (太虚) as the *valley’s spirit*,
And *creation* (造化) as the *ultimate spirit* (至神).
The *valley’s spirit* and *ultimate spirit*
Are the Dao’s essence and function—
How could they ever die?

「The principle of non-death is both profoundly obscure and capable of nurturing as a mother.
Some liken the *valley* to Heaven and Earth,
And *spirit* to the Great Dao.
Now, those who speak use the analogy of mountain valleys echoing in response—
Is this not a small comparison?

Moreover, if a person can embrace openness without limits,
Conform to the boundless scope of the Great Emptiness,
Preserve the true and genuine *spirit*,
And draw upon the vitality of *creation*,
Then naturally their form aligns with the Dao—
What is there of death to speak of?
Thus, in obscurity, they flourish and nurture,
And all things receive their blessing—
This is called the *mysterious female*.

River Above Master’s *Commentary* states:
The sound of the *valley* (谷) is *nurturing* (育).
The *mysterious female* is nurturance itself.
If a person can nurture their *spirit*, they will not die.
By *spirit*, they mean the *spirits* of the five *viscera* (五藏):
The liver harbors the *hun* (魂),
The lungs harbor the *po* (魄),
The heart harbors the *spirit* (神),
The kidneys harbor the *jing* (精),
The spleen harbors the *zhi* (志).
If a person can maintain purity, stillness, and emptiness,
To nurture their *spirit* without being stained by desires,
And keep their form intact and *spirit* complete—
Then they will not die.
If, however, they indulge in emotions and become entangled,
Disturbed by all phenomena,
Their form becomes impaired and their *spirit* departs—
How could the Dao persist?

The *gateway* of the *mysterious female*
Is called the *root of Heaven and Earth*.
For all things emerge and return through the Great Emptiness and *creation*.
Since there is the name of *emergence and return*,
It is thus called the *gateway*.
By *root*, they mean *origin*.
Though Heaven and Earth are vast,
They cannot evade having form—
All things of form rely on the Great Emptiness and harmony as their origin,
Hence, it is called the *root of Heaven and Earth*.
River Above Master says:
The way to non-death lies within the *mysterious female*.
*Xuan* (玄) is Heaven,
In humans, it is the *nose*.
*Pin* (牝) is Earth,
In humans, it is the *mouth*.
Heaven nourishes humans with the five *qi* (五气),
Entering through the nose and stored in the heart—
These five *qi*, pure and refined, become the *spirit* of intelligence,
The clarity of hearing and voice,
And the five *natures* (性),
Their *ghostly* aspect being the *hun*.
The *hun* is masculine.
It governs the entering and exiting of *qi* through the nose,
Connecting with Heaven—
Thus, the nose is called *xuan*.
Earth nourishes humans with the five *flavors* (五味),
Entering through the mouth and stored in the stomach—
These five flavors, coarse and impure, become the *form* of bones, flesh, and blood,
The six *emotions* (情),
Their *ghostly* aspect being the *po*.
The *po* is feminine.
It governs the entering and exiting of *qi* through the mouth,
Connecting with Earth—
Thus, the mouth is called *pin*.
By *root*, they mean *primordial* (元).
The gateway of the nose and mouth
Is where the primordial *qi* of Heaven and Earth
Flows in and out.

The earlier statement that the *spirit of the valley* does not die
Encourages people to understand the principle of nurturing the *spirit*.
This passage on the *gateway of the mysterious female*
Illustrates the further development of refining the form.
Thus, those who perfect both form and *spirit*
Are the ones who can align with the Dao.

When humans have a body and a *spirit*,
Then there is life and death.
Life and death cannot be spoken of as the Dao.
Flowing and changing without constancy—
How could it be called *stillness*?

If one empties their form and *spirit*,
Forgets distinctions of self and other,
Then emergence has no root—
Thus, *qi* gathers without being called life,
And return has no orifice—
Thus, *qi* disperses without being called death.
Neither life nor death—
This is the *spirit of the valley*.
Life and death without constancy—
This is the floating and changing of things.
Profound stillness, feminine tranquility,
Serene and unmoving—
This is the *mysterious female*.

Continuous as if ever-present,
Yet effortless in its application.
Continuous—
The appearance of ceaselessness—
This concludes the meaning of nurturing the *spirit* and refining the form.

To nurture the *spirit* is to reach profound mystery and obscurity,
Purity, stillness, and emptiness,
Continuous as if ever-present,
Responding to things without ceasing.
To refine the form is to breathe the Great Harmony,
Guide and connect the blood and *qi*,
Drink the *elixir* of the endless spring,
Chew the herbs of longevity,
And ensure the limbs and joints remain unobstructed without labor—
Only then can one align with the roots of Heaven and Earth,
And share the gateway with all marvels.

The *valley* is the Great Emptiness,
*Creation* is the *spirit*—
The *mysterious female* nurtures all things,
Continuous and unending—
Thus, it is followed by the saying *Heaven endures, Earth persists*.

*Heaven endures, Earth persists*—
A standard.
Heaven, through its vast *qi* and forms,
Has not tilted from ancient times to now,
Hence it is called *enduring*.
Earth, through its solid substance and capacity,
Remains eternally steadfast and unchanging,
Hence it is called *persisting*.
This concludes the analogy and establishes its meaning in the following text.

Heaven and Earth endure and persist
Because they do not generate themselves—
Thus, they can achieve eternal life.
This concludes the meaning.

Heaven endures in purity,
Earth persists in tranquility,
Because they cover and nourish all things,
And extend their nurturing to all beings—
Yet they all draw upon the wondrous origin,
Return to the boundless void,
And restore to the profound stillness,
Without boasting of their own creative power,
And thus they uphold their constant *virtue*—
Hence, they can endure and persist eternally.

Another interpretation says:
Heaven, Earth, and all things
Transform uniquely and naturally,
All discerning their own essence,
Yet none seeking to prolong or augment their life—
Thus, they can endure and persist eternally.
This is a solemn warning.

Hence, the Sage follows his own body while his body precedes others,
Sets aside his body while his body remains.
The Sage acts without action,
His body in the hall of state,
Yet his mind wanders in *Maoshu* (姑射).
He follows the pattern of Heaven and Earth’s covering and nurturing,
And distributes nourishment impartially.
He has greatness yet practices humility,
And does not dare to take precedence over the world.
His people delight in his guidance without wearying of its joy,
They are pushed forward without resentment—
Thus, his body precedes others.
He can also forget his merits and name,
Set aside his body and reduce his desires.
The world loves him as a father and mother,
The *spirit* and clarity of Heaven protect him as an innocent child—
Thus, his body remains.

Is it not because he has no self-interest?
Hence, he can achieve his own interests.
Heaven and Earth generate all things,
Yet the Sage, setting aside himself, benefits all people—
None rely on their own benevolence,
How could there be a heart of self-interest?
It is truly without self-interest.
Without self-interest, he can endure and persist,
And because he endures and persists,
He is like one who can achieve his own interests.

Master Li Yue says:
He who does not selfishly hoard for himself,
Yet gives generously to others—
Others’ selfishness may not succeed,
But his own will be fulfilled.

*The Profound and Distinct Interpretation* says:
When Gongyi Xiu governed Lu,
He loved fish,
And when the entire state offered fish to him,
He did not accept it.
His disciples remonstrated:
“Master, you love fish—
Why do you not accept it?”
He replied:
“It is precisely because I love fish,
That I do not accept it.”」

「Though he receives fish and is spared from serving as prime minister,
Even if he loves fish, he cannot provide fish for himself—
Without receiving fish, he is not spared from serving as prime minister,
Yet he can still long provide fish for himself.
This is the understanding of one who acts for others while benefiting himself.

Thus it is said:
It is not because he lacks self-interest that he fulfills his own.
The Riverbank Master (河上公) and Master Yan Junping (严君平) wrote: *"because he lacks self-interest."*
Wang Bi’s ancient text read: *"It is not because he lacks self-interest that..."*
The Kaiyuan Imperial Edition (开元御本) states: *"It is not because he lacks self-interest that..."*
Each has its own meaning—let the reader consider them carefully.

The Sage’s later body, having transcended the self,
Can be public to himself while private to others,
Soft and yielding yet beneficial to all things—
Thus he is ranked above as *the highest virtue is like water*.

*The Daoist Classic of Authentic Perfection* (*道德真经*), in the *Palace Treasuries* (*藏室*), *Mù Wēi* Chapter (*慕微篇*), Volume One concludes.
*The Daoist Classic of Authentic Perfection*, *Palace Treasuries* (*藏室*), *Zhuǎn Wēi* Chapter (*纂微篇*), Volume Two begins.
Compiled by Bìxū Zǐ (*碧虚子*) Chén Jǐngyuán (*陈景元*).

*The Daoist Classic* says:
The highest virtue is like water.
The *highest virtue* (上善) refers to the standard for humanity (标人).
*Like water* (若水) is a metaphorical illustration (举喻).
He whose aspirations are for others, whose heart is empty and desireless,
Who adapts to circumstances and follows the times,
Does not oppose the myriad things,
And in virtue forgets virtue—
Thus he is called *the highest virtue*.

Water can solidify or dissolve, conform to depths or shallows,
Flow or sink, follow the Dao and embrace emptiness—
All these have their principles.
Only the Sage comprehends and harmonizes them all,
Thus he is like water.

Water embodies the Dao’s three near-qualities (三能之近道)
And seven virtues of benefit to all (七善之利物),
As explained in the following passage.

Water benefits all things yet does not contend,
Dwelling where others abhor—
Thus it is near the Dao.
This is the first of the three near-qualities.

Water’s nature is calm and still,
Yet it spreads and nourishes all things.
Without water, the sky would be parched and dry,
And the earth would be dusty and turbulent.
It benefits and nourishes all things,
Thus it is called *virtuous in beneficence*—
This is the first virtue.

Water is the gentlest and weakest of all things,
Yet it yields to emptiness, rejecting solidity,
Avoiding heights and seeking depths—
Blocked, it stops; pressed, it flows.
It submits to all things,
Thus it is called *non-contending*—
This is the second virtue.

Though men’s nature is to abhor what is low and impure,
Water dwells in the depths and accepts filth—
It does not shun what others abhor,
Yet it cleanses all things, making them pure and white,
Bearing all insults alone,
Dwelling where others abhor—
This is the third virtue.

*Near* (几) means *approaching*.
Water benefits all things,
Thus its benevolence is vast and boundless—
It does not contend,
Thus its virtue is modest and radiant—
It dwells in what others abhor,
Thus its capacity endures defilement.
By illustrating water’s three near-qualities,
Only the Sage embodies them in a unified virtue and conduct—
Far from straying from the Dao,
Thus it is said: *near the Dao*.

Another explanation:
Water is *qi* (气),
The ruler of the North (毋王於北方),
Its number being six.
The North is the beginning of Yang virtue (阳德)
And the end of Yin *qi* (阴气).

The number of life is one, the same as the Dao—
The Dao is also called one.
The Dao’s one is *the one that transcends all ones* (无一之一),
Water’s one is *the one that contains all ones* (有一之一).
The one that transcends all ones is the essence of the Dao,
The one that contains all ones is the manifestation of the Dao—
Thus water, as the Dao’s manifestation,
Is said to be *near the Dao*.

Residence (居), virtue of the land (善地);
Heart (心), virtue of the depths (善渊);
Giving (与), virtue of benevolence (善仁);
Speech (言), virtue of trustworthiness (善信);
Governance (政), virtue of rectitude (善治);
Affairs (事), virtue of ability (善能);
Action (动), virtue of timeliness (善时).

The Sage’s residence is virtuous in holding low and modest,
Following things naturally—
His influence extends to his village,
Like water flowing beneath the earth,
Dwelling in the depths and low places,
Nourishing all things—
Thus it is said: *virtuous in residence*—
This is the first virtue.

The Sage’s heart is virtuous in preserving emptiness and stillness,
Piercing the profound and subtle,
Clear and penetrating—
Like the depths of water, tranquil and undisturbed,
Though nine changes occur in its waves,
Its clarity and stillness remain unaltered—
Thus it is said: *virtuous in heart*—
This is the second virtue.

The Sage in giving is virtuous in practicing benevolence,
His kindness extending to all under heaven,
Without favoring kin or loved ones—
Like water’s oiliness, nourishing all,
Rising and falling with ease,
Nothing escapes its reach—
Thus it is said: *virtuous in giving*—
This is the third virtue.

The Sage’s speech is virtuous in upholding sincerity,
Not relying on external agreements,
Yet naturally harmonizing—
Like water’s reflection, without discrimination of beauty or ugliness,
Filling to the brim before it moves—
Flowing through dangers without loss—
Thus it is said: *virtuous in speech*—
This is the fourth virtue.

The Sage in governance is virtuous in rectifying the people,
His bearing upright and understanding profound—
Things submit naturally—
Like water’s clarity, settling high and low,
Purifying and cleansing all things,
Removing all dust and filth—
Thus it is said: *virtuous in governance*—
This is the fifth virtue.

The Sage in affairs is virtuous in ability,
Assigning roles according to aptitude—
No one is left unplaced—
Like water’s gentleness, accommodating all shapes and vessels,
Nothing it handles fails—
Thus it is said: *virtuous in affairs*—
This is the sixth virtue.

The Sage in action is virtuous in timeliness,
Emerging and retiring as circumstances demand—
His conduct preserves the Dao—
Like water’s movement, adapting to the times—
In winter, it solidifies; in summer, it flows—
It never deviates from its proper course—
Thus it is said: *virtuous in action*—
This is the seventh virtue.

Only because it does not contend,
Is there no resentment against it.
*Only* (唯) means *alone* (独).
This concludes the discourse on the three near-qualities and seven virtues above.

The Sage’s thoughts, conduct, and words,
His emergence and retirement—
All are complete in the wondrous Dao,
Conforming to the needs of things,
Yet ever maintaining *feminine* (雌) stillness,
Not contending with all things—
If things do not contend with it,
How could there be grounds for blame or resentment?

Again, *resentment* (尤) means *grudge* (怨).
It is said that only the Sage, who is alone in not contending,
Has no one to blame or resent.

The Dao’s essence is emptiness and nothingness,
Yet water’s number is one—
Only when the Sage employs it,
Does it approach the Dao.
It is also good at holding yet not overflowing,
Thus it is ranked next with *holding yet overflowing* (持而盈之).

*Holding yet overflowing* (持而盈之) is not as good as *early cessation* (其已).
*Overflowing* (盈) means *full* (满),
*Cessation* (已) means *stop* (止).
He who clings to fullness and overflowing,
Yet does not know humility and moderation,
Will inevitably see his downfall.
To avoid such ruin,
It is better to seek early cessation—
This is *laying down arms* (垂械).

Master Yan Junping (严君平) wrote: *"accumulating yet overflowing"* (殖而盈之),
Referring to amassing wealth and treasures.
*Measuring yet sharpening* (揣而锐之) cannot be long preserved.
*Measuring* (揣) means *calculating* (度).」

「Again, this is about *governance* (治). *Truly sharp* (锐) means *keen and cutting* (铦利). It speaks of how people know only of their sharp, greedy desires, yet pursue wealth and honor with reckless abandon, yet fail to discern the calculated folly (揣度) of their passions, and to govern themselves with moderation (思治侨恣). When misfortune comes, it cannot be long preserved.

Master Yan Junping (君平) said: *"Wealth and honor to me are like ascending a mountain to gaze endlessly—*
*Power and status pass like a fleeting bolt of lightning.*
This is to say they cannot be long preserved."

*The *Honglie Jie* (《鸿烈解》) records: White Gongsheng (白公胜) seized a state’s treasury but could not distribute its wealth. After seven days, Shi Qi (石乞) entered and said: *"What is gained unjustly cannot be kept, and if you cannot share it, disaster will surely come."* Unable to give to others, he only brought harm to himself. White Gongsheng did not heed this warning. On the ninth day, Ye Gong (叶公) arrived and opened the treasury’s stores to distribute among the people, while also raising taxes to fund soldiers. White Gongsheng attacked him, but nineteen days later, he was captured.

To claim a state that is not rightfully yours,
Yet fail to share its wealth,
Is to be the most avaricious (至贪).
To take without giving to others,
Yet lack the wisdom to benefit oneself,
Is to be the most foolish (至愚).
Compare White Gongsheng’s fate to the *Ur* (臬) who loved his son—
How different is it?

Thus it is said:
*Holding yet overflowing* (持而盈之) is not as good as *early cessation* (其已),
*Measuring yet sharpening* (揣而锐之) cannot be long preserved.
Gold and jade fill the halls,
Yet none can keep them.
This illustrates the inevitable truth:
*Fullness cannot be long held.*

Gold and jade in a chamber
May only moisten the house,
Yet when a great thief arrives,
He will seize baskets and bags in haste,
Though wise men may dwell within,
None can guard them.
Thus even beasts know this—
The elephant fears the hunter’s blade and loses its tusks,
The rooster dreads sacrifice and sheds its tail.
Even beasts recognize this,
Yet how do humans fail to reflect?

Master Yan Junping (严君平) and Wang Bi (王弼) wrote: *"Gold and jade fill the chamber"* (金玉满室).
Wealth and honor, if indulged in excess (侨),
Bring ruin upon oneself.
*遗* (遗) means *to bestow* (与).
Wealth invites others to seek it,
So one easily deceives himself.
Honor makes others subservient,
So one easily oppresses others.
Deceit and oppression grow daily,
Disaster and blame will surely follow—
This ruin is self-inflicted, is it not?

Master Yan Junping (君平) further said:
*"Gold and jade harm the body,*
*Status and power corrupt the spirit—*
Like ice and fire,
They cannot coexist."
Thus, *status is the spirit’s impurity* (名者神之秽),
*wealth is the body’s harm* (利者身之害).
Nurturing the spirit’s impurity,
Accumulating the body’s harm,
Damages what is already achieved,
And aids what is already flawed.
Gaining them brings benefit,
Losing them brings ruin—
Then one’s thoughts are muddled,
And his discernment of gain and loss is perverse.

Again he said:
*"Those who increase my wealth*
*Diminish my spirit,*
*Those who bring me fame*
*Destroy my body.*
Misfortune arises from within myself,
Not from others.
Fortune arises from within myself,
Not from heaven."*

Master Lu Xisheng (陆希声) said:
*"To hold a great office yet overflow with arrogance (满盈),*
Though one fears it,
It is better to cease early.
To occupy a high position yet exceed its limits (亢极),*
Though one worries,
It is better to withdraw early.
To calculate power and wealth yet sharpen one’s ambitions (锐意),*
Though one gains them,
They cannot be preserved forever.
To hoard gold and jade while the halls overflow,*
Though one possesses them,
They cannot be kept long.
If one is honored yet extravagant (侨), he invites disaster;
If one is wealthy yet extravagant (侨), he multiplies his faults.
Hubris grows within the heart,
And its punishment comes from oneself—
Why blame heaven or reproach others?"*

*"Great deeds accomplished,*
*Fame secured,*
*Then step back—*
This is the Dao of heaven.
Whoever understands this,
Knowing when to advance and retreat,
To preserve or perish,
Is the Sage indeed.
External fullness injures life—
Rather than sharpen one’s focus on wealth and honor,
It is better to embrace simplicity (抱一) and avoid blame,
Thus follows the teaching: *Carry the vital essence (营魄), embrace unity (抱一).*

*Carrying the vital essence (载营魄), embracing unity (抱一),*
Can one truly remain undivided?

The Daoists say:
*The spirit of yang is called *hun* (魂),*
*The hun delights in life,*
*The shade of yin is called *po* (魄),*
*The po favors death.*
*The hun roams,*
*The po remains still.*

*The *Baihu Tong* (《白虎通》) states:*
*"The hun is the wandering essence,*
*Qi is the undetermined form,*
Thus the hun is called *ying* (营)."*
The old commentary says:
*载* (载) means *to bear* (乘).
*营* (营) refers to the *hun* (魂).
It is also said that *ying* (营) means *to protect the yang qi*.
The *hun* is the yang essence,
The *po* is the yin spirit.
The yang essence favors movement and wandering,
Thus Daoist scriptures speak of *restraining and retaining* (拘留).
The yin spirit favors agitation and deception,
Thus Daoist scriptures teach methods of *subduing and controlling* (制伏).
If one’s form and essence always bear the yang essence and yin spirit,
And embrace the pure, unified *tàihé* (太和) *qi*,
Keeping them from scattering,
Then one may forever preserve longevity.

The Dao’s embrace of unity (抱一)
Is like a *yì* (镒) containing its brilliance—
Can brilliance ever separate from the mirror?
This teaches how to nurture the spirit (养神).

Now I explain:
If one seeks to guard unity (抱一之卫),
One must keep the heart undivided.
If the heart remains undivided,
Then it is bearing the *hun* and *po*,
And embracing the pure, unified Dao.
Can one be like an infant (婴儿)?
The following passages are all interpreted in this way.

*Concentrating qi to achieve softness (专气致柔),*
Can one be like an infant?

The old commentary says:
*专* (专) means *unified* (一) and *to entrust* (任).
*Qi* (气) is the *chōnghé miàoqì* (冲和妙气) of harmony and subtlety,
And also the natural *qi* of the body.
When a person stands alone, distinct from all else,
He receives the natural, harmonious, and subtle *qi*.
As *qi* descends and form is born,
It remains untainted and pure.
If one concentrates and entrusts oneself to this harmonious, subtle *qi*,
Forgets all perceptions and knowledge,
Then *qi* will naturally remain pure and harmonious,
And the body will naturally be soft and weak,
Unharmed by the evils of others—
This is attaining the infant’s complete harmony.
This teaches how to nurture *qi* (养气).

Now I explain:
*"Can one be like an infant?"*
It means:
If one seeks to guard *qi* through concentration and softness (专气致柔之卫),
One must be like an infant in purity and harmony.
If one can be like an infant in purity and harmony,
Then one has attained the guarding of *qi* through concentration and softness.

*Washing away impurities, gazing into the profound (涤除玄览),*
Can one be without blemish?

The old commentary says:
*涤* (涤) means *to wash* (洗).
*除* (除) means *to dispel* (遣).
*玄览* (玄览) is *mind’s illumination* (心照).
*Blemishes and flaws* (疵瑕) are *diseases* (病).
One must wash away dust and filth,
Dispel the five passions (五情),
Keep inner and outer purity,
Illumine all things with the mind—
Then blemishes and flaws, like diseases,
Will vanish without trace.
This teaches how to cultivate the heart (修心).」

From here, the three matters above exhort one to cultivate the person;
From here, the three matters below urge one to govern the state.

The *Classic* says:
*"Cultivate it in the person, and its virtue becomes true;
Cultivate it in the world, and its virtue becomes universal."*

Now it is explained:
If one seeks to wash the heart, dispel filth, and fathom inner and outer affairs,
And can reflect upon oneself without blemish or disease,
Then this is the method of *washing away and profound illumination*.

Can one govern the people with love and the state without action?

The old commentary says:
*"To govern the state is to love the people as one’s own children.
In administering affairs, one must not be harsh or cruel;
Taxes and labor must not harm their nature.
One should focus on agriculture, simplify governance,
And let the people pursue their occupations in peace,
Thus ensuring their well-being—
This is the transformation of *wuwei*."*

Now it is explained:
If a ruler seeks to nurture the ten thousand people without harming their natural endowment,
And governs by fostering agriculture while keeping affairs clear and still,
Then this is the art of loving the people and governing the state.

Can one be receptive like the female principle?

*The Heavenly Gate* is the gate of Nature.
Nature gives birth to the *Taiji*;
The *Taiji* gives birth to Heaven and Earth;
Heaven and Earth give birth to yin and yang;
Yin and yang give birth to the ten thousand things;
The ten thousand things live and die through it, coming and going—
Thus it is called the *Heavenly Gate*.
*Opening* means to disperse and give;
*Closing* means to gather and withdraw.
When it opens, life flourishes;
When it closes, things decay and perish.
Though it gives birth to all things, one does not see its *generating*;
Though it brings death to all things, one does not see its *destroying*.
Life and death unfold unseen—
How could it not be called Nature?

Only the *Great Person* can embody Nature.
The *Great Person* encompasses the universe in measure,
And contains yin and yang in *qi*.

Through receptive stillness,
The transformations of life and death, rise and decline
Do not enter the mind.

*Receptive stillness* is the subtle function of Nature.
This warns those who cultivate the person and govern the state
To guard with receptive stillness.

The old commentary says:
*"The *Heavenly Gate* is the gate of the Northern Pole within the Purple Enclosure.
Heaven has the Northern Pole, with stars within the Purple Enclosure,
And within the enclosure are the Five Emperors,
Who take turns in their reigns—
Thus the gate opens and closes.
When it opens, it is *Tai* (harmony);
When it closes, it is *Pi* (blockage).
Thus, in spring, the Green Emperor’s gate opens,
While the others close—
And the seasons follow this pattern.
Moreover, the Five Phases cycle endlessly,
And the changes of time and fate are unceasing.
Only the sage knows Heaven and follows the Mandate,
Ever guarding receptive stillness,
Thus never swayed by change—
Hence eternal prosperity and great good fortune.

Some interpret this in terms of personal cultivation:
The *Heavenly Gate* refers to the nose and mouth;
*Opening and closing* refers to breathing and exhalation.
Speaking of one who is receptive, still, and yielding,
Their breath becomes deep and subtle,
Continuous and refined,
Nurturing the spirit in effortless ease."*

Now it is explained:
The opening and closing of the *Heavenly Gate* in its cycles
Is the natural order of time—
Only by being receptive and humble
Can one remain unmoved by yin and yang,
Unchanged by cold and heat.

Can one be clear and open in all directions?

Can one be without knowing?

The old saying speaks of *day’s clarity*—this is wisdom’s illumination.
Though one cultivating the self may possess this luminous wisdom,
And though their ears and eyes are sharp and their understanding vast,
If they lack perception and hearing,
Then the one governing the realm must cultivate virtue as bright as the sun and moon,
Illuminating the four quarters of the land—
Yet they must block their ears and veil their eyes,
And act as though they know nothing.

To make the people of the world live unaware in their daily lives—
This is called *the Dao*.

Now it is explained:
To attain wisdom and clarity in all directions,
One must first withdraw the senses and return to stillness,
Ever guarding *non-knowing*—
This is the root of clarity and boundless understanding.

To nurture and preserve,
To give birth yet not possess,
To act yet not rely upon it,
To grow yet not control—
This is called *Mysterious Virtue*.

Speaking of cultivating the self and governing the realm,
If one practices these six principles,
It is like the Dao’s generation—
Things are not obstructed at their source;
They are left to complete themselves naturally.

And in nourishing virtue,
Things are not constrained in their nature;
They are left to complete themselves naturally.

Thus, virtue shines forth alone,
And only the sage is born—
How could this be otherwise?

All creatures attend to their own affairs—
What does the sage rely upon?

Things grow and nurture themselves—
What does the sage control?

This is to forget merit, forget things,
Pierce the depths of obscurity—
This is called *Mysterious Virtue*.

Wang Bi says:
*Mysterious Virtue* is having virtue yet not knowing its master,
Emerging from the depths of darkness.

Embrace the One without departing from focused *qi*,
Achieve the harmony of an infant—
When harmony is attained, all things return as spokes converge to the hub,
Thus the thirty spokes unite in one hub.

The thirty spokes unite in one hub—
When the hub is empty, the wheel’s function is realized.
This illustrates how *having* and *non-having* support each other in utility.

The *Zhou Li: Kao Gong Ji* states:
The carriage’s canopy is round to symbolize Heaven’s vehicle,
Its square base follows Earth’s law,
And the thirty spokes symbolize the number of days in a month.

The moon hangs in Heaven, flowing endlessly,
The wheel rolls across Earth, turning without end.
Thus, the carriage symbolizes the moon—
Thirty days complete one month,
Just as thirty spokes unite in one hub.

When the hub’s center is empty, the wheel can turn.
The carriage’s emptiness allows it to bear its load.
Thus, the void’s emptiness holds the function of turning and bearing.

Jun Ping said:
In the age of ancient sages governing the people,
They followed what Heaven and Earth naturally did,
And did not contrive clever artifices.
They drank from gourds, ate with their hands,
And all things were equal, with no high or low.

When rulers began to act,
Taxes grew heavy, labor burdensome,
The people exhausted—
The upper classes no longer sang,
Tribute came from afar,
Men and women bore loads they could not bear.

Thus, the wise invented the *pushing-hub carriage*,
Hitching horses and oxen to bear heavy burdens over long distances,
Easing the people’s toil.

Later generations followed suit,
And clever creations multiplied—
Carved and painted hubs,
Gilded wheels adorned with silver and jade,
And emerald pendants—
The cost of one carriage could impoverish a family.
Thus, Laozi lamented how artificial contrivances harm morality,
And how doing good can breed chaos.

Hence, the three things—carriage, tools, and dwellings—
Illustrate how *having* and *non-having* mutually support utility,
And serve as a warning.

Mixing clay to make vessels—
When the center is empty, the vessel’s function is realized.
*Cao* means blending,
*Ni* means clay—
The artisan blends and molds clay,
Shaping it into vessels,
Taking the empty space within to hold all things.

Jun Ping said:
In times when morality decays,
Worries assail from within,
And yin and yang harm from without—
The people grow weak, emaciated, and sickly.
Thus, water and fire arise together,
The five flavors take form,
Raw and cooked are indistinguishable,
Drying and moistening are confused.
Hence, the wise mix clay to make vessels,
To cook sour and salty foods,
And eventually indulge in hunting, luxury, and licentiousness,
Slaughtering creatures and cracking eggshells to please the ruler,
Carving jade into bowls and plates,
Refining crude materials into tools—
Until it reaches this point.

Piercing doors and windows to make dwellings—
When the center is empty, the dwelling’s function is realized.
*Zao* means to pierce.

Half-door and daily door.
The window beside the door is called *yǒu* (牖).
Door, window, and *yǒu*—these are also general terms.

In ancient times, when dwelling in caves,
One would pierce and hollow out the earth within,
Covering it above to form doors and windows for dwellings.
Taking the empty space within the dwelling,
Humans found a place to rest, enter, and exit.

Jun Ping said:
Once human hearts change,
All things harbor resentment—
Worms and snakes rise,
Poisonous silkworms act,
Birds and beasts harm people.
Thus, even caves prove insufficient
To defend against calamities,
Preserve life,
Or complete one’s natural years.

Therefore, the wise constructed dwellings—
Roofs above, eaves below,
Windows pierced for observation,
Sturdy gates and firm closures,
Swiftly opening and shutting,
So that wasps and insects could not enter,
And beasts could not approach.
Only then did it lead to ornate pavilions,
Lofty towers,
The Alang Palace’s halls,
Great gates guarding strategic passes,
Fortified cities,
Soldiers exhausted and dying in countless numbers.

Yet what was considered governance in ancient times
Is seen as chaos in later ages—
This stems from the mutual drawbacks of *you* (有) and *wu* (无),
For it lies in human nature itself.

Thus, *you* is used for advantage,
And *wu* is used for function.
This resolves the three matters above,
Illustrating that *you* and *wu* mutually rely on each other
And neither can be discarded.
*You* is used for advantage—advantage lies in vessels.
*Wu* is used for function—function lies in emptiness.

The utility of vessels
Must exist externally,
Drawing on emptiness to fulfill their purpose.
The function of emptiness
Must be internally void,
Borrowing from vessels to achieve its use.

Thus, *wu* relies on *you* for advantage,
And *you* relies on *wu* for function.

*Wu* aligns with the Dao,
*You* manifests as vessels.
That which is above form is called the Dao.
The Dao is formless.
Though the Dao is formless,
It must rely on *you* to display its efficacy.
That which is below form is called vessels.
Vessels have substance.
Though vessels have substance,
They must rely on *wu* to fulfill their function.

Thus, vessels without the Dao cannot be applied,
And the Dao without vessels cannot display its function.
It is like the hub having spokes,
Vessels containing things,
Dwellings housing people—
All rely on *wu* to benefit *you*,
And on *you* to utilize *wu*.

As for cultivating the body,
The spirit is the advantage for sustaining life,
Emptiness is the function for attaining the spirit.
Thus, *wu* enables function,
And *you* benefits things.
The advantage of *you* lies in sustaining life,
While the function of *wu* lies in attaining the spirit.
*Wu* is the state of emptiness and tranquility,
*You* is the state of spiritual clarity.

Spiritual clarity is the subtle *you*,
Emptiness and tranquility is the ultimate *wu*.
The advantage of subtle *you* lies in sustaining life,
The function of ultimate *wu* lies in attaining the spirit.
The advantages and functions of sustaining life and attaining the spirit
Do not exceed subtle *you* and ultimate *wu*.

The establishment of chariots and tools
Uses *wu* to benefit *you*—
*Wu* is emptiness,
*You* is form,
Thus, it follows with the five colors.

The five colors blind the eyes,
The five tones deafen the ears,
The five flavors dull the palate.

The five colors were originally established
To manifest the symbols of the Five Phases,
With *fǔfù* (黼黻) patterns and decorations
Distinguishing the adornments of rank—
This is the proper use of the five colors.
Yet later generations indulged in brocades and silks,
With green and yellow pleasing the eye,
Chasing external objects,
Draining vitality and impairing vision,
Unable to perceive the color beyond color—
What else but blindness?

The five tones were originally established
To manifest the sounds of the Five Phases,
With metal, stone, silk, and bamboo
Harmonizing the *qi* of Heaven and Earth—
This is the proper use of the five tones.
Yet later generations composed *Zheng* and *Wei* music,
With licentious melodies pleasing the ear,
Indulging without restraint,
Shaking the spirit and blocking hearing,
Unable to listen to the sound beyond sound—
What else but deafness?

The five flavors were originally established
To manifest the harmony of the Five Phases,
With salt and vinegar moderating,
Nourishing human relationships—
This is the proper use of the five flavors.
Yet later generations craved bear’s paw,
With coarse meats delighting the palate,
Gluttony without end,
Corrupting the spirit and defiling purity,
Unable to taste the flavor beyond flavor—
What else but dullness? *Cuan* (爽) means loss,
*Cha* (差) means error.

Galloping in hunts drives the mind to madness.
Hunting is the state’s customary ritual,
To practice martial affairs
And demonstrate the seasons to the people.
The Son of Heaven and feudal lords
Each year held three hunts:
One for *gān dòu* (乾豆) offerings,
To sacrifice at ancestral temples.
One for guests,
To foster goodwill between states.
One to replenish the ruler’s kitchen stores in season.
If hunting is not done in season,
It is called disrespectful.
Hunting out of season
Is called ravaging nature.
Thus, spring searches, summer inspects seedlings,
Autumn fills stores, winter hunts.
If one does not follow these laws,
Galloping recklessly across borders,
Exhausting wildlands without restraint,
Harming crops and farmers,
Climbing cliffs and crossing dangers,
The mind and spirit grow disordered—
What else but madness?

Valuable goods cause one to act recklessly.
Gold, silver, jade, and pearls—
These are rare treasures.
If one greedily pursues them without measure,
Or ceaselessly seeks them,
The Dao becomes obstructed and injured,
And the body suffers labor and humiliation.

Jun Ping said:
When the five colors are excessive, the world grows blind;
When the five tones are harmonized, the world grows deaf;
When the five flavors are balanced, the world grows dull;
When hunting flourishes, the world grows mad;
When jade and pearls are prized, the world grows weary;
When currency and silk circulate, the world grows impoverished.

Thus, the five colors are like daggers piercing the eyes,
The five tones are like mallets blocking the ears,
The five flavors are like blades severing the tongue,
And hunting is the leader of madness.

Pursuing distant goods brings ruin,
And valuing difficult-to-obtain things brings scarcity.
All these things
Transform and generate one another,
Unfathomable and endless,
Difficult to clarify yet easy to extinguish,
Difficult to obtain yet easy to lose.

Calamities and disasters
Are the chambers of peril and annihilation.
Seeking them brings self-harm,
Dwelling in them brings self-destruction.

This is both a warning to the sovereign
And instruction to the common people.

Thus, the Sage prioritizes the belly over the eyes,
And so discards the former to take the latter.

The Sage refers to the ruler who follows the Dao.

The ruler who follows the Dao allows his mind to wander beyond sound and color, while nurturing the boundless inner radiance. The belly—when it receives nourishment and fills with substance—does not chase after things, thus preserving its wholeness and attaining spiritual sovereignty. Therefore, the Sage follows this principle and prioritizes the belly.

The eyes—when they fixate on colors and cause disorder—lead one to chase after things, thus blinding the outer senses and dissipating vital essence. Therefore, the Sage warns against this and does not indulge the eyes.

To "discard the former and take the latter" means to abandon the eyes' pursuit of things and embrace the belly's inner completeness.

Indulging in the pleasures of sound and color leads to slaughter and disgrace, while not valuing hard-to-obtain goods brings honor and prosperity. Thus, honor and disgrace follow.

Honor and disgrace are like sudden alarms.
Honor refers to wealth, nobility, celebrations, and rewards—all that is fortunate.
Disgrace refers to poverty, humiliation, punishment, and misfortune—all that is calamitous.

Those who grasp the Dao regard the body as a temporary lodging, and life and death as burdensome afflictions. They neither rejoice in prosperity nor fear adversity, knowing that official rank and external possessions are mere transient dependencies. How could honor and disgrace bind their hearts and cause constant alarm?

This speaks of honor and disgrace as sudden alarms—words meant for the average person.
The moderately wise, dwelling in safety yet mindful of peril, fearing disgrace even in honor, thus react as if alarmed.
Common folk flee toward trivial pursuits, alarmed by disgrace, while the moderately wise, observing the fundamental Dao, are alarmed by honor—hence, "honor and disgrace are like sudden alarms."

To hold great afflictions as one’s own body.
"Great afflictions" refers to official rank, treasures, and external possessions that sustain life.
The perfected one knows the body is not truly theirs, yet they still cling to externals—how much more so do they cling to what they revere?

Now, people of the world claim that official rank and treasures can sustain life, thus valuing them as their own body, unaware that both the body and external things are the roots of great sighing and are unworthy of reverence.

What is meant by "honor and disgrace"? Honor is superior; disgrace is inferior.
*(Original note: The *Kaoyuan* text reads "honor is inferior, disgrace is superior.")*
It means that when one attains wealth and nobility, relying on honor leads to arrogance and ruin. If ruin comes from honor, then honor is the root of disgrace—thus, "honor is inferior."
*(Original note: The *Heshang Gong* and *Huangfu Mi* texts also read "honor is superior, disgrace is inferior," meaning gain is superior, loss is inferior.)*
To gain is like being alarmed; to lose is like being alarmed—this is called "honor and disgrace are like sudden alarms," concluding the meaning.

The vulgar cling to their wealth and rank—holding them brings trembling, losing them brings sorrow. They do not understand that gain and loss are not truly theirs, so they are all seized by alarm.
The moderately wise know that fortune and misfortune cycle like a knot. If one enjoys honor and prosperity, decay and disgrace will inevitably follow, so they guard against overreaching, fearing loss as they would alarm.

*The Liezi* says: Zhao Xiangzi sent envoys to attack Di, capturing two cities yet wearing a worried expression. He said, "I have great virtue and heavy deeds; how can I not be vigilant?" Confucius heard this and said, "The Zhao clan will surely flourish—this is gaining as if alarmed."

What is meant by "to hold great afflictions as one’s own body"? My greatest affliction is that I have a body. When I have no body, what affliction do I have?
*(The repeated "what is meant by" marks a question-and-answer structure to introduce the following explanation.)*
The reason one has great affliction is that they have a body.
The human body, lacking fur or feathers to resist cold and heat, must rely on external things to nourish life and preserve existence. Yet those who cling excessively to life often court death, and thus great sighing follows.
This arises from being ensnared by worldly attachments, clinging to the idea of having a body.

If one transcends the body, no longer treating it as their own, forgetting the mind and no longer treating it as their own, merging with the natural transformations and aligning with all things—moving as if dragging dead wood, sitting as if gathering cold ashes—then the former afflictions of honor, disgrace, and great peril, what harm can they bring?
Thus it is said: *When I have no body, what affliction do I have?*

In *"On Equality of All Things,"* Zi Cha said to Yan Yan: "Now I have lost myself—do you know this?"
*(Original note: "Lost" here means "forgotten" or "transcended.")*
To abandon illumination and attain seated forgetfulness is to still greater afflictions—how much more so for those who naturally merge with existence?
*(Original note: Some interpret "no body" as annihilation or emptiness, but this misses the essence of Laozi’s teaching.)*

Thus, to hold the body as precious for the sake of the world is to be able to entrust the world to it.
To love the body as precious for the sake of the world is to be able to deposit the world in it.
From the perspective of transcending the body upward, this broadly addresses how common people and scholars are seized by honor and disgrace, leading to great affliction.
