From the perspective of valuing and loving downward, it specifically speaks of rulers who have not yet forgotten the world, hence the language of entrustment.

Yet the idea of entrustment is not the virtue of the highest Sage. If one’s heart wanders in tranquility, blends with the boundless, follows nature without artifice, then one may restore the winds of antiquity.
Lu Xi-sheng said: If one’s heart is disturbed by gain and loss, clinging to external things, then honor and disgrace never bring lasting peace, and fortune and misfortune never cease. How can such a one govern the world or hold the great position within the realm?

Only those who hold the body as precious for the world’s sake, loving the body as precious for the world’s sake, truly value and love the world, not their own bodies.
Thus, gain and loss lie beyond them, and worries and afflictions do not cling to their person. Even so, they dare not claim dominion over the world—how much less would they seize it as their own?

This is the Way of Great Dao, the intent of the public realm.
*(Original note: The *Kaoyuan* text reads: "Thus, to hold the body as precious for the world is to be able to entrust the world; to love the body as precious for the world is to be able to deposit the world." The commentary says: This chapter begins with a warning against honor and disgrace, concluding with the idea of entrustment. If one is alarmed by honor and disgrace, they have not forgotten them; if they value and love [the body], they have not forgotten [their attachments].")*

Thus, comparing disgrace to honor, disgrace is not as worthy as honor;
comparing virtue to love, virtue is not as profound as love.

Those alarmed by honor and disgrace still cling to their hearts;
those who cherish virtue and love have not yet forgotten the world entirely.

Hence, first, one must make the alarm of honor akin to disgrace,
then seek to forget both honor and disgrace entirely.
Using the near-name of entrustment, one argues for the ultimate of forgetting both—
forgetting honor and disgrace means nothing remains to alarm,
forgetting the body means no root for affliction,
forgetting the world means no need for the near-name of entrustment.

The *Wang Bi* text reads:
*"Thus, if one values the body for the sake of the world, then one can entrust the world.
If one loves the body for the sake of the world, then one can deposit the world."*
Wang Bi’s commentary states:
*"There is nothing to exchange for the body, hence ‘virtue’—
thus one can entrust the world.
There is nothing to diminish the body, hence ‘love’—
thus one can deposit the world.
Only when honor and disgrace, glory and calamity,
do not diminish or exchange the body,
can the world then truly be entrusted."*

The *Zhuangzi: "Zai You"* chapter’s citation is taken as definitive,
with Wang Bi’s text ranked secondary.
The commentary aids in elucidation, though its phrasing is inferior.
It is suspected that the *Kaoyuan* text’s collation may have added or omitted passages,
thus losing the original intent.

When both honor and disgrace alarm, the mind is not yet free—
only those who conform to the Way are effortless and formless,
hence the progression to *"what cannot be seen."*

The *Zhuangzi* states: "Thus one can entrust the world."
Since there is nothing to diminish the body, it is called **"Yī"**—
thus one can deposit the world.
When honor and disgrace, glory and calamity,
do not diminish or exchange the body,
only then can the world truly be entrusted.

**Yī** (原本作 *jī*), in ancient texts, refers to the subtle and formless.
The *Yìjīng* says: "Subtle (*jī*) is the faintest stir of movement."
Though this meaning exists, it is now preserved but not discussed.

That which cannot be heard is called **"Xī"** (希).
That which cannot be grasped is called **"Wēi"** (微).
These three—**Yī**, **Xī**, and **Wēi**—
cannot be summoned or invoked,
so they are merged into one.
The Dao is difficult to describe and define,
thus the Sage names it with reluctant necessity.

The Dao is not a visible thing,
yet cannot be seen with the eyes—
thus, within the formless,
it manifests all forms,
and is therefore called **Yī** (夷).
**Yī** means "level"—
it describes the boundless, tranquil state
where, though present in form,
there is no form at all.

The Dao is not a sound,
yet cannot be heard with the ears—
thus, within the silent,
it gives rise to all sounds,
and is therefore named **Xī** (希).
**Xī** means "sparse"—
like objects scattered thinly,
without striking or touching,
it is sound yet not sound.

The Dao is not a shape,
yet cannot be grasped by the hands—
thus, within the shapeless,
it takes on all shapes,
and is therefore called **Wēi** (微).
**Wēi** means "wondrous"—
it is subtle and profound,
without substance or boundary,
yet is shape without shape.

Only the **Perfect Sage** (至人) perceives with the spirit
the formless form of the Dao,
and emerges beyond all forms.
With **qi** attuned, he hears the soundless sound,
and emerges beyond all sounds.
With the mind discerning, he grasps the shapeless shape,
and emerges beyond all shapes.

These three—**Xī**, **Yī**, and **Wēi**—
are forced names for the Dao’s operation,
not its true, inherent existence,
so they cannot be examined or questioned.
Thus, they should be merged and understood as the supreme unity.

Jun Ping says:
*"Before the great wild goose (*hóng*) is fully formed,
cut open its egg and look inside—
it is not yet the goose,
yet its shape, sound, head, and tail
are all already present.
This is the formless goose within the egg.
How much more so,
when even the egg of the goose does not yet exist,
and yet the Transforming Power (*zàohuà*) creates it!"*

From this, it is clear:
The origin of the **Supreme Ultimate** (*Tàijí*),
before Heaven and Earth,
already contains subtle traces of shape and sound,
though unseen and unheard.
Thus, it is evident:

Those who do not see with the eyes can see,
those who do not hear with the ears can hear,
those who do not follow with the hands can grasp,
those who do not speak with words can explain.
Therefore, the shapeless shape,
from which Heaven and Earth arise,
is called **Yī** (夷).
The soundless sound,
from which the five tones begin,
is called **Xī** (希).
The formless trace,
from which myriad things emerge,
is called **Wēi** (微).

These are the paths the ancient sages pointed toward.
If one forgets seeing and hearing,
truly follows and grasps,
and merges all into boundless unity,
then the Dao is fully attained and exhausted—
nothing more can be added.

Its upper reaches are not bright,
its lower depths are not dark.
Endless and unbroken, beyond naming,
it returns to the formless.
**Yī** (绳绳) means endless continuity,
without bounds.

This is called the **indescribable state** (*wúzhuàng zhī zhuàng*),
the **formless image** (*wúwù zhī xiàng*),
or **dim and vague** (惚怳).

To return to the formless is not to speak of emptiness—
it means that within the stateless state,
all forms are created,
and within the imageless image,
all things are transformed.
To say "it exists" would be wrong,
yet things arise from it—
this is **having without having**.
To say "it does not exist" would also be wrong,
yet things arise through it—
this is **having without having**.
Existence and nonexistence are indeterminate—
this is called **dim and vague** (惚怳).
**Hū** (惚) is "nonexistence,"
yet speaks of nonexistence as if it were existence.
**Huàng** (怳) is "vague,"
yet speaks of existence as if it were nonexistence.
Thus, it is called **dim and vague**.

Approach it, yet you cannot see its head.
Follow it, yet you cannot see its tail.
The Dao precedes Heaven and Earth,
and endures beyond the ancient past—
how did it come into being?
No one knows its origin,
thus you cannot see its head.
Yet it persists through all ages,
and extends beyond countless eons—
how does it end?
No one knows its conclusion,
thus you cannot see its tail.

This is what prompts one
to clear their **spiritual platform** (*língtái*)
and model themselves after the Dao’s essence.

To hold the ancient Dao
and govern the present is to know
the origin of antiquity—
this is called the **principle of the Dao** (*dàojì*).
The ancient Dao is formless and nameless,
the root of Heaven and Earth,
the ancestor of all things—
the Dao that cannot be seen or heard.

The *Lǎozǐ*’s method for cultivating the self and the world
is to uphold the ancient, natural Way of nonaction (*wúwéi zìrán*),
to restrain the present’s artificial, vexatious customs (*yǒuwéi fánnǎo*),
and to return to simplicity,
recovering the **Supreme Beginning** (*Tàishǐ*),
so that each may rectify their nature and life—
not shifting their virtue—
this is called the **framework of the Dao’s principle**.

Seeing and hearing cannot be summoned,
dim and vague beyond form—
only the **good scholar** (*shànshì*)
can hold the ancient Dao
and govern the present’s artificiality.

Thus, it follows with the **good scholar of antiquity** (古之善为士者).
The *True Classic of the Dao and Virtue* concludes here.

**The True Classic of the Dao and Virtue: *Zàngshì Zuǎnwēi* (Volume Three)**
*Compiled by Bìxū Zǐ Chén Jǐngyuán*

**The Dao Classic**

The **good scholar of antiquity**—
subtle, profound, and ineffable,
deep beyond recognition—
was one who, in the **Supreme Beginning**,
governed self and realm
through the natural Way,
his virtue vast yet hidden,
his spirit bright yet distant,
his wisdom piercing the void,
his sincerity unobstructed.
Thus his bearing was profound and thick—
who could truly know the *Lǎozǐ*?

For the Dao seems great yet appears unworthy,
as when **Liezi** dwelt in Zheng
yet none could recognize him—
this was the **good scholar of antiquity**.

Since he could not be known,
he feigned outward form.
The **good scholar of the Way**,
his virtue deep and boundless,
difficult to discern,
fearing later ages might lack his model,
thus he spoke of his bearing and demeanor,
pointing out his conduct and posture—
this is the **lower text**.

**Like winter crossing a river,**
*Yù*—hesitant, as if—
the **good scholar of the Way**,
following nature without resistance,
neither hastening fortune nor provoking calamity,
yet in all actions, cautious and reserved,
though walking smooth paths,
ever wary of drowning,
as in bitter winter’s month,
venturing across a long river,
his heart hesitant yet doubtful,
fearing to sink into unfathomable depths.

**Like fearing four neighbors,**
the **good scholar of the Way**,
ever treading the void yet daring no action,
in retreat or engagement, deeply contemplative,
fearing judgment in the realms of light and dark,
as common folk avoid taboos,
dreading neighbors’ secret knowledge—
this is the depth of his caution.

**Solemn as a guest,**
the **good scholar of the Way**,
upright and composed,
his mind undistracted,
like a guest before his host,
how dare he act hastily?
His words: *"No action, no doing."*

As **Shùngé Shùnzǐ** bound his belt and straightened his bearing,
his presence dissolved all intent—
thus **Tiánzǐ Fāng** revered him as teacher.
**Lǐshè Guāng** dwelt in darkness,
as if before sovereign and parent—
thus **Sīmǎ Zǐwēi** praised him deeply.
This is called **solemn as a guest**.

**Dissolved like ice about to melt,**
the **good scholar of the Way**,
though outwardly stern,
his mind at ease, his wisdom unfettered—
like spring ice thawing,
dissolving and dispersing,
all rigidity gone.

**Sincere as uncarved wood,**
*Sincere*—in its pure thickness.
*Uncarved*—in its undivided simplicity.
*Uncarved*—before form’s division.
The **good scholar of the Way**,
naturally thick and pure,
speech and silence both unadorned.

**Vast as a valley,**
*Vast*—in its breadth.
*Valley*—in its hollow void.
The **good scholar of the Way**,
his virtue thick yet unseen,
his capacity wide yet encompassing,
bearing good and ill alike,
like an empty valley’s echo—
always void.

**Mixed as muddy water.**
*Mixed*—as waves churn.
*Muddy*—when clarity is lost.
The **good scholar of the Way**,
his heart serene within,
yet outward currents swirl—
like turbid water’s obscurity,
how could beauty or ugliness be distinguished?

The seven matters above:
if applied to ruling a realm,
the people know not, understand not,
returning to the **Supreme Beginning**;
if applied to cultivating the self,
harmonizing with dust and light,
obscured in the **Ultimate Way**—
who could muddy then clarify?
Stillness brings gradual clarity.

The **good scholar of the Way**,
his heart like a deep spring—
muddied yet settling,
stillness brings its clarity.

Who could remain steadfast yet endure?
Movement brings gradual life.
The **good scholar of the Way**,
his virtue fragmented—
when steadfast, enduring,
movement brings life’s fullness.

An old saying claims:
The **Perfect One** outwardly appears mixed and indistinct,
yet within, pure and clear—
aligned with the Dao’s essence,
gradually ascending,
borrowing from clarity’s truth.

Again, for the scholar’s sake,
fearing to dwell in this gradual clarity,
long yet unchanging,
lodged in all phenomena,
thus urged to move, seek superior methods,
until life’s gradual unfolding,
no longer clinging to forms,
reaching unbroken generation.

*One variant reads:*
Who could muddy then still—gradual clarity?
Who could remain steadfast yet move—gradual life?

**Preserve this Way without excess.**
Only by not being full
can one endure decay without renewal.
He who guards this gradual clarity and life,
well knows humility in self-cultivation,
dwelling in emptiness and stillness.

Only by not being full—
again, the **Perfect One** alone,
not boasting of completeness,
thus ever clinging to decay’s humility,
though new achievements arise,
he holds victory unmoved,
seeks further progress,
pursues higher virtue,
not resting in small gains—
this is the **Sage’s** profound path.

The **good scholar** follows antiquity,
his insight deep and subtle,
sincere, uncarved, vast and hollow—
attaining the **Way of the Hollow Valley’s Extremes**,
thus it follows with **attaining the Extremes of Emptiness**.

**Attaining the Extremes of Emptiness,**
**Guarding Stillness Firmly.**
*Attaining*—obtaining.
He who, desireless,
attains the **Void’s Way**,
reaches the **Obscure’s Extremes**,
yet steadfast in stillness,
his virtue becomes pure and thick.

*The *Lièzǐ* says:*
None surpasses stillness,
none surpasses emptiness—
stillness, emptiness,
obtaining their dwelling.

*The *Xīshēng Jīng* says:*
If one empties and does nothing,
not desiring the Way,
the Way itself returns.

*Yán Jūnping said:*
Dao and virtue are empty and void,
thus able to receive and bestow.
Heaven and Earth are clear and still,
thus able to transform.
Yin and Yang alternate,
thus able to give and take.
Sun and Moon advance and retreat,
thus able to shine.
The four seasons begin and end,
thus able to nurture and complete.

Remove emptiness and void—
Dao and virtue cannot endure.
Remove clarity and stillness—
Heaven and Earth cannot exist.
Advance without return—
Yin and Yang cannot circulate.
Move without retreat—
Sun and Moon cannot illuminate.
End without beginning—
All things cannot live.

Thus:
Having yet returning to nonhaving,
full yet returning to emptiness,
the heart bearing nothing,
the will displaying nothing,
doing nothing as if blocked,
unworried as if mad,
embracing truth, treading simplicity,
abandoning cleverness,
not knowing what is first,
emptiness as constant—
then spirit and clarity reach their peak,
naturally exhausted.

The movement of action returns to the self,
while thought and contemplation restore the spirit.
Hide me within the heart of no-mind,
carry the form within the body of no-self.
What does not benefit life,
do not use it to enslave the will.
What does not accord with Heaven,
do not use it to scatter the spirit.

When matters are simple yet the spirit remains unshaken,
when inner flow does not alter the outer,
when vision is inverted and hearing is reversed,
and the spirit moves in harmony—
above aligning with the Dao of Heaven,
below engaging with the world—
the spirit remains undisturbed,
vital qi unstrained,
naturally embracing contraction and extension,
thus attaining the Middle Way.

When all things act in concert,
I observe their return.
Human beings are born in stillness—
this is the nature of Heaven.
Now, those who speak of attaining ultimate emptiness
and steadfastly guarding stillness
are guiding people to return to their wondrous origin.
This is not merely for humanity.
Truly, when all things move together,
their origin has never failed to begin in profound tranquility,
emerging from formlessness,
born of harmonious qi,
responding to transformation.
When observed in their return,
they exhaustively revert to the boundless void,
returning to the trackless,
thus preserving their true form.

The *Classic of Changes* says:
*"Return—does it not reveal the heart of Heaven and Earth?"*
The heart of Heaven and Earth is profound stillness unto nothingness.

Jun Ping said:
*"Heaven and Earth return and invert,
thus able to endure eternally.
Humans return to sleep,
thus able to attain clarity.
Flying birds return to gather,
thus able to soar high.
Running beasts return to rest,
thus able to leap far.
Dragons and serpents return to their coils,
thus able to shine resplendent.
Grasses and trees return to their roots,
thus able to remain verdant.
When transformation returns,
spirit and clarity attain their proper place,
communing with the void and nothingness,
the soul resting, the vital breath at ease,
each finding its place—
the will tranquil, the qi harmonious,
the blood and vital essence in balance.
Even this temporary return to stillness
preserves the spirit—
how much more so for those who endure in the ultimate Dao!"*

All things, teeming and flourishing,
each return to their root.
Returning to the root is stillness;
stillness is returning to the vital command;
returning to the vital command is constancy.
Teeming—luxuriant in growth—
refers to plants and grasses.
Some say *yún yún*—
moving and acting, teeming and numerous—
refers to flying and walking creatures.
By analogy, though movement and stillness differ,
all return to their root.
The void is the boundless and formless;
stillness is profound and silent.
The boundless and silent—
this is the root of movement and stillness.

Moreover, the nonbeing is the origin of being,
stillness is the sovereign of agitation—
when movement reaches its peak,
it must return to stillness;
when being reaches its limit,
it must return to nonbeing.
The roots of grasses and trees are heavy,
resting low in stillness,
thus they thrive.
Flowers and leaves are light,
residing above in movement,
thus they wither and fall.
If even things abide thus,
how much more so for humanity?

Therefore, the Sage offers analogies
to make the people abandon their love of desires,
returning to the root of emptiness and stillness,
thus restoring the origin of their nature and vital command.
The origin of nature and vital command—
this is the boundless and obscure,
where sight does not perceive and hearing does not hear.
This is only for the wise to awaken to themselves.

Those who awaken to it
walk, stand, sit, and lie outside of emptiness and stillness,
yet respond to change without deviation—
these are those who attain constancy
and return to the vital command.

To know constancy is clarity.
To not know constancy is reckless action—disaster.
This speaks to the difficulty of the constant Dao,
yet even understanding it is enlightenment.

Those who awaken to the constant Dao
possess spirit that transforms without bounds,
nature that communicates with all,
qi that harmonizes with all—
they do not know of all things as "me,"
nor of "me" as all things,
thus able to tread upon water and fire,
pierce metal and stone,
reverse mountains and rivers,
shift cities and towns,
ride the void without falling,
touch the solid without breaking—
thousand transformations, ten thousand changes,
inexhaustible—
this is the spirit aligned with the constant Dao.

The next level:
their essence and vitality unwaning,
their visage like a maiden’s,
dwelling in the world for millennia,
borne aloft to immortality—
this is the form aligned with the constant Dao.

The next level:
speech and silence follow principle,
conduct and retreat accord with the times,
action aligns with the Yang’s rhythms,
shining yet not dazzling,
stillness aligns with the Yin’s virtue,
using obscurity to attain clarity—
worldly burdens cannot harm them,
their bodies free from blame—
this is the ability to wield the constant Dao.

If governing the body is the foundation,
then wielding the constant Dao is supreme.
Thus, it is said: *"To know constancy is clarity."*
Or, *"To grow ever clearer"*—
this is the doctrine of gradual enlightenment.
Having awakened to the constant Dao,
one must follow as described above.
Those who fail to awaken to the constant Dao
instead take spiritual transformation for monstrosity,
long life for falsehood,
ultimate emptiness and steadfast stillness for hollow retreat,
injuring vitality as annihilation—
they do not know strong knowing,
do not recognize strong discernment,
every thought false and deceptive,
every action disastrous and easy—
this is *"not maintaining virtue"*—
or bearing its shame.
Thus, it is said: *"Reckless action brings disaster."*

To know constancy is tolerance;
tolerance is impartiality;
impartiality is sovereignty;
sovereignty is the Way of Heaven;
the Way of Heaven is the Dao;
the Dao is enduring—
living without peril.

Those who know the constant Dao
respond to all things with benevolence,
saving without abandoning,
embracing without exclusion.
Embracing movement and stillness without selfishness,
their bosom opens boundlessly,
achieving true impartiality.
True impartiality without selfishness
spreads virtue universally across the world,
with none failing to return.
*"Sovereign"* means "to go forth."
When people return,
Heaven will aid them—
by nature, they oppose nothing,
thus called *True Persons*.
They emerge from being, enter nonbeing,
obscurely vast and all-pervading,
enduring with the Dao,
inexhaustible—
water and fire cannot harm them,
metal and stone cannot maim them,
worldly calamities cannot assail them—
what peril could there be?

Attaining emptiness, guarding stillness,
returning to the root, restoring the vital command—
this is to know constancy.
Those who know constancy walk the path of the Great Above,
thus ranked beneath the *Supreme Above*.
The *Supreme Above*—
the sovereign of primordial antiquity,
unnamed and unmarked—
the *"Highest Virtue that is not virtue."*
Its virtue cannot be surpassed,
thus called *Supreme Above*—
though it holds sovereign rank,
it does not claim exaltation,
letting all things act naturally,
each rectifying their nature and vital command.
Thus its teaching is effortless,
its governance untraceable,
actions arise with the times,
resources are leveraged for achievement—
the people use it daily without knowing its Dao,
only knowing of their sovereign above.

The sovereign—what power does he hold over me?

Zhuangzi said: In the age of supreme virtue, the worthy are not exalted, the capable are not employed. The ruler stands like a pole marking a branch, the people move like wild deer—upright and proper without knowing it as *righteousness*, loving one another without knowing it as *benevolence*. They act without leaving traces, perform deeds without leaving records. This is the age of the *Supreme Above*.

The next stage: they are cherished, they are praised. As supreme virtue declines and *benevolence* and *righteousness* become prominent, those who benefit from *benevolence* grow close and attach themselves, those who embrace *righteousness* praise and elevate them. Zhuangzi said: When the sage arrives, he bends and stretches to create *benevolence*, he stands on tiptoe to establish *righteousness*, and thus the world begins to doubt. Doubt gives rise to cherishing and praise.

Again he said: Shun had his natural conduct, and the people delighted in him, so he moved his capital three times to Chengdu. Yao, hearing of his virtue, raised him to high office and then abdicated in his favor—this is the stage of cherishing and praising.

The next stage: they are feared, they are scorned. When *benevolence* and *righteousness* fade and penal laws are established, when penal laws are strict, the people fear them. Though laws are strict, those who rely on cunning and deceit will transform the people, leading them to fraud and deception—this is when the people’s scorn of their ruler runs deepest.

*The Profound and Mysterious* says: In the age of the *Supreme Above*, virtue spreads naturally—this is when the people merely know of their sovereign above. The next stage is to prevent them from doing wrong—this is the stage of cherishing and praising. The next stage is to reward the virtuous and punish the violent—this is the stage of fear and scorn.

Trust is insufficient, so distrust arises. Sincerity is when the heart loves truly; faithfulness is when words can be relied upon. When trust is complete, the world is secure; when trust is lost, the world is in peril. Now that cunning and deceit have flourished and fraud is rampant, the ruler’s trust is lacking among the people, so distrust answers in kind.

The ruler’s influence over the people is like the reflection of a bright mirror—its form is clear, its logic unerring. Wang Bi said: When the body’s nature is lost, disease arises; when the essence of things is distorted, flaws and conflicts emerge. When trust is insufficient, distrust follows—this is the natural order.

Yet even this is better than reckless speech. From the stage of cherishing downward, the way of the *Supreme Above*’s effortless transformation is already lost, unable to restore the purity of antiquity. Even so, it still values speech and teaching, clinging to old methods as the means of transformation. Though far from the Dao, it is still better than reckless speech leading to distrust and rebellion.

When deeds are accomplished and affairs settled, the people say, *"This is natural."* The ruler of the Dao governs with passive ease, so achievements are completed without claim, his silence and stillness profound—thus affairs are settled and forgotten. The people are pure and unadorned, doing nothing in vain, saying, *"This is simply natural."* Where then do the hearts of cherishing, praising, fearing, and scorn arise?

The old saying: *"Trust is insufficient, so distrust arises"*—this explains *fearing* and *scorning*. *"Yet even this is better"*—this explains *cherishing* and *praising*. *"When deeds are accomplished..."*—this again echoes the stage where the people merely know of their sovereign above. To rigidly adhere to old interpretations is not followed here.

The *Supreme Above*’s effortless virtue is not manifest. When *benevolence* and cherishing arise, the *Great Dao* is abandoned, so it is ranked below. When the *Great Dao* is abandoned, *benevolence* and *righteousness* emerge. The *Great Dao* is the ancient way of effortless transformation. *"Abandoned"* means both hidden and decayed, no longer practiced. When the people’s hearts are not pure, the *Great Dao* is hidden and decayed; supreme virtue is not practiced.

Then the seven forms of artificiality gradually arise, and *righteousness* and *profit* grow excessive. Thus, the pure is shaped into vessels—this is when the *Great Dao* is abandoned, giving rise to the seven forms of artificiality.

Zhuangzi said: Fish forget one another in the rivers and lakes; people forget one another in the states of Dao and Wei—this illustrates the age of the *Great Dao*. When *comprehensive love* becomes *benevolence* and *judging wrong* becomes *righteousness*, then *benevolence* and *righteousness* arise from insufficiency. This is like fish stranded on dry land, splashing each other with muddy water, moistening one another with saliva—not as good as forgetting one another in the rivers and lakes. This explains how the *Great Dao*’s abandonment gives rise to the seven forms of artificiality.

Wisdom emerges, and great deceit follows. When purity is lost and the *Great Dao* is abandoned, wisdom arises, and with it, great deceit—this is the natural order. Zhuangzi said: The true nature of the horse is to graze, drink, and lift its legs as it walks. When happy, it nuzzles and rubs necks; when angry, it rears and kicks. This is all the horse’s wisdom. But when the master of horses tames it—with bit and bridle before, whip and spur behind, the yoke and reins to guide it, and the monthly inspections to refine it—the horse becomes skittish, resistant, fierce, and deceitful, and the cunning of stolen reins is born.

In the age of antiquity, the people lived without knowing what to do, walked without knowing where to go. They dwelled in contentment and joy, drumming their bellies as they wandered. The people could do no more than this. But when the sage arrived, he bent and shaped *rites* and *music* to regulate the world’s form, he stretched and adjusted *benevolence* and *righteousness* to soothe the world’s heart—and thus the people’s cunning and deceit grew, their sophistry and hair-splitting flourished. This is what is meant by *"wisdom emerges, and great deceit follows."*

The five relationships are disrupted, and *filial piety* and *affection* arise. The state falls into chaos, and loyal ministers emerge. The five relationships are father, son, elder and younger brothers, husband and wife. The *Rites* say: When the *Great Dao* is practiced, one does not love only one’s own kin, nor cherish only one’s own children—true *benevolence* forgets kinship. Those who cherish their kin treat nephews as their own children and serve uncles as their own fathers. When the five relationships are harmonious, where then does *filial piety* and *affection* become prominent? Thus, the people’s reverence for my kin is as it should be for all kin. Therefore, above and below live in harmony, and the bonds of kinship are forgotten.

But when the teachings of balance are lost and the young harbor superiority, they defy the natural order, so the five relationships are disrupted, and the traces of *filial piety* and affection appear.

「Loyal ministers,
　　The six kinships—father and son, elder and younger brothers, husband and wife—are thus unified. The *Rites* state: When the *Great Dao* is practiced, one does not favor only one’s own kin nor cherish only one’s own children; true *virtue* forgets kinship. Those who cherish their kin treat nephews as their own children and serve uncles as their own fathers. When the six kinships are harmonious, where then does *filial piety* and *affection* become manifest? Thus, all revere my kin as they should revere all kin. Therefore, above and below dwell in harmony, and the bonds of kinship are forgotten.

But when the young lack balance and harbor arrogance, they transgress the natural order, disrupting the six kinships, and the traces of *filial piety* and affection then appear.

Moreover, the *Sage* maintains balance, and thus the Four Seas become one family. Wandering in spirit to the *Maoshan* (Maoshan Mountains), one fades away and forgets all of one’s worldly concerns. Then, the ruler above does nothing, yet the people transform effortlessly—where then would there be ministers who pierce their eyes and split their hearts to serve?

Thus, the blind and the late-born, the obstinate, are praised as *Shun* is called *Great Filial Piety*, the wise yet austere of *Lu* is praised as *Shen* is called *Able to Nurture*, the upright *Jie* of *Xia* saw *Longfeng* rise, and the perverse *Zhou* saw *Bigan* shine—these are acts of necessity, not of joy.

When the *Dao* is abandoned, *virtue* and *righteousness* become prominent; when *wisdom* emerges, great deceit arises. To restore the ancient simplicity, one must extinguish the traces of *Sageliness* and discard cunning *wisdom*—thus comes the teaching of *Discarding Sageliness and Wisdom*.

Discarding Sageliness and Wisdom, the people’s benefit increases a hundredfold.
*Sageliness* refers to the institutions, laws, symbols, and functional relics of the *Sages*.
To discard it is to restore their original simplicity.
*River Upper Master* (Huangshang Gong) said: The Five Emperors’ painted symbols and *Cangjie*’s creation of writing are inferior to the Three Sovereigns’ simple knotted cords without script.

*Wisdom* refers to strategic scheming, calculation, and the manipulative arts of *halong* (纵横).
To discard it is to return to *non-action*.
The *Classics* say: To rule a state with *wisdom*, it is the state’s ruin.
If one does not display the functional relics of *Sageliness* nor employ the manipulative arts of *wisdom*, then craftiness and deceit will not arise, calamities and chaos will not occur, and the people will be like innocent children, devoted solely to agriculture—then their benefit will far exceed a hundredfold.

*Zhuangzi* said: “Abandon minor *wisdom*, and major *wisdom* will become clear.”
He also said: “If the *good man* is not found, the *Dao* of the *Sage* cannot stand.
If *Zhi* the robber is not thwarted, the *Dao* of the *Sage* cannot prevail.”
Thus, one must discard and abandon these things for the people to truly benefit.

Discarding *virtue* and *righteousness*, the people return to *filial piety* and *affection*.
*Virtue* is the love of things, so people grow attached to it.
*Righteousness* is the proper arrangement of things, so people praise it.
Yet the harm of *virtue* and *righteousness* lies in the attachment and praise they inspire—once attachment and praise are established, the hearts of the people become restless, and the harmony of life and nature is lost.

When the harmony of life and nature is lost, how then can *filial piety* and *affection* persist?
Now, if one discards and abandons them, it is to restore the wholeness of life and nature and revive *filial piety* and *affection*.

Discarding cleverness and material gain, thieves and robbers will cease to exist.
*Cleverness* refers to intricate carving, fine craftsmanship, and mechanical devices like locks and keys.
*Material gain* refers to precious gems, jade, and measuring tools—weights, scales, seals, and tokens.
Though these mechanical devices and tokens may serve to guard against minor threats, in the grand scheme, they cannot repel great calamities.
Today, to discard them is like casting aside jade, destroying pearls, burning seals, and breaking tokens—it is to make the people simple and unadorned, so that theft naturally stops.
These three teachings—*Discarding Sageliness and Wisdom*, *Discarding Virtue and Righteousness*, and *Discarding Cleverness and Material Gain*—are said to be insufficient as *culture* (文), so they are followed by *Associated Teachings*.
*Associated* means to follow or continue.
The three teachings refer to the aforementioned steps.
Though one may wish to abandon these three, to restore the ancient simplicity, yet as *culture* and *teachings* handed down, they remain unclear in principle—thus, many find them perplexing.

Separately, there are the *Associated Teachings* to follow, which will clarify the mind like ice—this is explained below.
*Seeing Simplicity and Embracing Purity* (见素抱朴), with *few private interests and little desire*.
*Seeing Simplicity* means preserving one’s natural purity, without mixing in artificial adornments—thus, the traces of *Sageliness* and *wisdom* naturally vanish.
*Embracing Purity* means returning to one’s original simplicity, without indulging in ostentation—thus, the sentiments of *virtue* and *righteousness* naturally fade.
*Few private interests* means adhering to one’s proper portion with utmost fairness, not coveting external beauty—thus, cleverness and artifices naturally cease.
*Little desire* means moderating one’s emotions and living frugally, calm and tranquil—thus, superficial material gain naturally disappears.

The traces of *Sageliness* and *wisdom* can rescue the near but fail to know the harm they cause to the far; the sentiments of *virtue* and *righteousness* can aid the urgent but fail to recognize their deviation from true nature; the tools of *cleverness* and *material gain* can assist the small but fail to see their danger to the great. Therefore, the *Sage* is bright yet does not display, wise yet does not act, holding only to *Seeing Simplicity and Embracing Purity* as their aspiration, and *few private interests and little desire* as their concern. Thus, ruling a state in this manner, the people attain wealth and longevity, returning to the ancient simplicity, and cultivating oneself in this way, one’s body withers and mind grows empty, fading away like a lost pair.

When the traces of *Sageliness* are discarded, cleverness and artifice naturally fade; when *virtue* and *righteousness* are discarded, *affection* and *filial piety* naturally flourish, and kinship grows harmonious. This arises from the harm caused by *scholarship*—thus, it is followed by the teaching of *Discarding Scholarship and Freedom from Worries*.

*Discarding Scholarship and Freedom from Worries*.
The *Dao* is vast and difficult to speak of—how can *scholarship* grasp and advance it? Thus, it can be transmitted but not bestowed, obtained but not fully possessed.
The case of *Lun Bian* (轮扁) was not one of inability to transmit, but rather of lacking a recipient of suitable quality.
How much more so with the *Dao* of the *Sage*!
Today’s *scholars* are but the dregs and refuse of knowledge.»

「The so-called *Discarding Scholarship* does not mean abandoning learning altogether, but rather preserving one’s innate nature without exceeding its bounds through study. Just as the *Wild Duck’s Way* (凫经) is brief, yet clinging to it brings worry, and the *Crane’s Way* (鹤经) is long, yet severing it brings sorrow. *Lun Bian* (轮扁) and *Shi Guang* (师旷) were born with natural brilliance, yet later scholars often distort the strengths and weaknesses of their innate nature—how could they ever emulate their humble yet proud virtue?

Abandoning such emulation, one finds neither worry nor joy; instead, one follows their natural *qi*, attains the Sage’s heart, and thus what is truly sought is not lacking, while what is vainly discarded is not needed. Those who labor in scholarship yet remain troubled bow their heads and pierce their thighs, seeking only empty fame, or study by candlelight, pursuing only fleeting gain—thus, they are ensnared by honor and disgrace, joy and sorrow. How lost they are!

*Yi* (唯) and *E* (阿) differ by how much? *Shan* (善) and *E* (恶) differ by how much?
*Yi* (唯) is reverent acceptance; *E* (阿) is complacent assent.
From the perspective of *social norms* (名教), *yi* is reverence while *e* is flattery.
In truth, *yi* and *e* both stem from the same voice—how distant their divergence!

If one forgets the world’s attachments, then the distinction between *yi* and *e* vanishes.
*Shan* (善) is the praise of *auspiciousness* (吉); *E* (恶) is the label of *inauspiciousness* (凶).
To study and practice it is *shan* (善); to neglect study and defy it is *e* (凶).
The *Dao* is boundless, profound, and tranquil—how could it be confined to learning or not learning?

Those who pursue *shan* (善) without attaining its essence find ruin in fame,
while those who flee *e* (恶) without avoiding its roots meet destruction in punishment.
Thus, *shan* and *e* are not the true *Dao*—they are external things.
Since both are external, how different can they be?
Only those who *Discard Scholarship* and *Free from Worries*, though they possess the Sage’s wisdom,
remain unaware of it—how much more so with *shan* and *e*, *yi* and *e*!

Some say: *"Transform conventional scholarship into true scholarship,
change *e* into *yi*, change *e* into *shan*—it is as easy as turning one’s hand!"*
The Sage’s words are of ultimate truth; these are the world’s teachings.
If one argues from this perspective, how does it differ from *yi* and *e*?

The ancients wrote: *"The distinction between *shan* (善) and *e* (恶) is as follows:
As in the previous chapter, all know what is beautiful as beautiful—thus, *e* already vanishes.
What people fear, they must fear.
The *Dao* is boundless—its depth is endless.
The Sage’s true heart and wondrous *Dao*,
*Discarding Scholarship* and *Free from Worries*,
though they forget *shan* and *e* within their breast,
must align with *yi* and *e* in outward form.
The warnings of *shan* and *e* must not be disregarded—
this is the depth of the Sage’s *Dao*!

The petty-minded are not so.
They admire *shan* yet fail to practice it,
fear *e* yet do not abandon it.
They drown in worldly scholarship,
going forth without return,
wasting their true nature,
never once halting in their folly.
*Cent* (央) means *center* (中); *Zhi* (止) means *halt*.

The crowd moves with joy,
as if feasting on the grandest sacrificial meat,
or ascending the Spring Platform in springtime.
*Cent* (熙熙) is the appearance of delight;
the *Spring Platform* (春台) is the splendor of the season.
This is the way of the common folk.

People, through scholarship, adopt falsehoods,
chase fleeting appearances, and lose their true nature—
drowned in the waves of love and desire,
they revel in joy,
like a starving man before a grand feast,
his appetite unfulfilled yet rushing along the path of honor,
watching eagerly,
like a wandering son ascending the Spring Platform,
his heart lost and never returning.

I alone remain still—
my state is not yet apparent,
like an infant before it smiles,
calm and undirected,
as if having nowhere to go.

*Cent* (怕) means *tranquil*—also, serene and effortless.
*Cent* (兆) is the initial appearance of form;
*Cent* (孩) is the look of laughter.
*Cent* (乘乘) is the movement of *qi*.
The Sage’s heart is tranquil and serene,
effortless and void,
leaving no trace of form or shape—
though amidst the clamor of the world,
watching the transformations of things,
they gaze blankly,
like an infant unable to distinguish laughter or sorrow.

Then, they drift along with the world,
mixing in its currents,
riding the crowd’s tide,
moving without sharp contrast or vast distinction—
as if bound for nowhere.

*Cent* (乘乘) is also like a hollow boat drifting east and west,
with no fixed destination.

*Cent* (怕) was originally written as *Cent* (魄).
Wang Bi (王弼) rendered it *Cent* (廓).
*Cent* (乘乘) was originally written as *Cent* (儡儡) by Wang Bi,
or *Cent* (魁魁) in another version.

The crowd all have excess,
while I alone seem abandoned—
this is the heart of the foolish man,
undifferentiated and simple.

When people’s hearts are bound to things,
they cling to appearances as if returning home—
gluttonous in the pursuit of wealth and honor,
they claim their hearts overflow with joy.

Thus, the Sage abandons worldly affairs,
unbinds the *Way of the Scholar* (绅修),
knowing that honor and disgrace come and go,
like a guest departing and a host forgotten—
this is the heart of the Sage,
undifferentiated and vast,
as if all things are one.
The foolish man’s heart is indeed simple and pure—
though they say, *"Is this not the heart of the foolish man?"*
In truth, the Sage’s heart has no distinctions,
and thus the Sage’s heart is perilously like this.

The crowd is bright and radiant,
while I alone seem dim.
*Cent* (昭昭) is the appearance of brilliance,
shining with talent and skill—
thus, the Sage’s wisdom encompasses all things,
yet they never boast,
as if in darkness.

The crowd is keen and discerning,
while I alone am dull and broad-minded.
*Cent* (忽兮) is like the sea—
floating, as if bound for nowhere.

*Cent* (察察) means strict and rigid—
also, harsh and constrained,
or proud and self-important.
*Cent* (闷闷) means expansive and yielding.
Some render it *Cent* (惛惛)—
the appearance of confusion and obscurity.

The world, through scholarship and governance,
establishes strict laws and harsh decrees,
proudly clinging to artificial *ways*—
thus, the people barely survive.
The Sage follows the *Way of Nature* (天) and aligns with the *Dao*,
conforming to the world’s flow,
in yielding and embracing all things—
thus, the people preserve their true nature,
living in broad-mindedness and simplicity.»

Zhuangzi said:

At the utmost extreme of the Dao,
dim and silent, yet though dim and silent it does not divide,
obscure and unfathomable,
yet all things return to it—
none know where they go,
the myriad folk employ it—
none know where it is exhausted.
It is skilled in descending and broadly accepting,
its depths unfathomable—
thus it is said, like the sea.

And yet, the Dao of responding to change
has no fixed method—
like flowing waves drifting and rising,
never settling in place.
Some render it: *Cent* (忽兮若晦寂), as if bound for nowhere,
speaking of the Sage’s bearing suddenly obscured,
his mind dwelling in stillness,
serene and unshaken—
this is the ninefold transformation of the waves of Huqiu Zilin.

Both interpretations are valid,
but I follow the first.

The crowd all have *ways* (有以),
while I alone am dull and broad-minded.
I alone differ from others,
yet value *food of the Mother* (食母).

*With* (以), means *employ* (用).
The common folk revere learning,
unable to *act without purpose* (无为),
yet they must employ some *way*—
thus it is said, they have *ways*.

The Sage moves as if dragging a withered tree,
dwells as if gathering dead ashes,
not adorning his form externally—
thus, he alone appears dull and broad-minded.

The Sage’s coming and going,
his speech and silence,
are not purposely different from others,
yet naturally they differ—
why? Because he values employing the Dao.

Those who employ the Dao
are in harmony with the *transforming of things* (造化),
thus it is said: I alone differ from others,
yet value *food of the Mother*.

*Food* (食), means *employ* (用).
*Mother* (母), is the Dao,
the origin.
Some say *food* (食) means *nourish* (养),
*Mother* (母) means *spirit* (神).
The spirit can give birth to the body—
thus it is called *Mother*.

The common folk value desires
and thereby lose their true form,
while the Sage values *non-desire*
and thereby nourishes his spirit—
thus he differs from others.

Others say:
*Mother* (母) is *qi* (气).
The common folk indulge in flavors,
while the Sage values *food of harmonious qi* (食和气)—
thus he differs from others.

Some say, "The Master Lao values blending light with dust to avoid conflict,
why does this passage alone speak of *me and you* (彼我)?"
I say, "This is surely not the Sage’s original intent—
rather, it is spoken out of necessity."

The Sage’s actions,
what is ever truly different?
It is merely that the common folk’s movements and stillness are contrary to the world’s flow,
so he speaks in response and establishes teachings—
thus there is *me and you*.

Worries of *transgressing the Dao* (失礼) arise from learning,
so he follows with the *dimness of the Sage’s bearing* (沌沌若昏).
The *virtue/power* (德) of the Sage is vast and luminous—
thus he follows it with the *bearing of great virtue* (孔德之容).

The bearing of great virtue
follows only the Dao.

*Kong* (孔), means *great* (甚) and *vast* (大).
*Bearing* (容), means *form* (状).
*Follows* (从), means *conform* (顺).

The Sage is one who is in harmony with the Dao.

The Dao has no name,
yet *virtue/power* (德) reveals it.
Great virtue has no origin,
it is formed by conforming to the Dao.
Thus, the Sage of great virtue,
amidst all appearances,
what is ever truly seen?
Only the bearing of conforming to the Dao—
shorn and plain, such is his form.

The Dao, as a thing,
is only *dim and vague* (倪唯惚).
The Dao is remote and difficult to speak of,
so even the keenest ears cannot perceive it—
what thing can be called *seen*?

Now, when speaking of *things*,
it is merely to borrow strong names
to probe the subtle truths.

The Sage of great virtue,
able to conform and follow the Dao,
the Dao having no form—
how can he follow it?
Since its form is nonexistent,
and yet it cannot be named—
how then can it be followed?

Only by probing its *dim and vague* (怳惚) nature,
can one resonate with its influence—
its image is formless yet audible.

*Dim* (怳) seems like *having*—
yet *having* is not *having*.
*Vague* (惚) seems like *not having*—
yet *not having* is not *not having*.
*Not having* that is not *not having*—
this is *emptiness as form*.
*Having* that is not *having*—
this is *form as emptiness*.
*Having* and *not having* are unfathomable,
yet we borrow images of *things*
to elucidate the Dao.

*Vague* (惚) and *dim* (怳),
within them lies *image*—
*dim* (怳) and *vague* (惚),
within them lies *thing*.
*Image* (象), is *qi manifestation* (气象).
*Thing* (物), is *spirit-thing* (神物)—
that which Zhuangzi calls *True Lord* (真君),
and which we now call *nature* (性).

The Dao,
dim and vague, undetermined—
if we say it has no *transgression*,
then *vague* (惚) naturally emerges from the formless,
delighting as it transforms *qi manifestations*—
becoming the portents of all things.

If we say it has *transgression*—
then *dim* (怳) arises from the initial *image*,
and *vague* (惚) dissolves back into *non-being*—
yet within the utmost *non-being*,
there exists *spirit-thing*.

*Spirit-thing* (神物),
the unfathomable interplay of *yin* and *yang*—
the subtle essence that speaks of all things.

Through myriad transformations and changes,
never exhausted—
governing heaven and earth,
creating *yin* and *yang*—
establishing *qi* and forming essence,
becoming all ten thousand kinds—
nurturing the body, nurturing the state,
refining the coarse into the subtle—
none escape the *spirit-thing*.

Profound and obscure,
within it lies *essence*—
its essence is most true,
within it lies *faithfulness*—
profound, in its depth and distance—
obscure, in its stillness and quietude.

The Dao,
dim and vague, cannot be determined—
*images* of *things* cannot be seen—
yet profound and obscure,
askers know not its substance,
responders comprehend not its truth—
within it, however, dwells pure *essence*,
and it holds the *faithfulness of Nature*.

Its transformations are not false—
thus it is called *true*.
Its changes respond to the times—
thus it is called *faithful*.
Like the crow’s foot becoming the *dish* (跻螬),
human blood becoming *wildfire* (野火),
rotting melons birthing fish,
virtuous women turning to stone—
though the movements of growth and decay vary in ten thousand forms,
none escape the *essence* sensing and transforming,
the *faithfulness* reaching its peak and not failing to change.

Who but the Sage of great virtue
could ever comprehend this?

From ancient times to the present,
its name does not fade—
employing it to measure the myriad folk.

How do I know the myriad folk are thus?
By this.

*Measure* (阅), means *assess* (度) and *unfold* (披).
*Folk* (甫), means *origin* (本始).

The Dao,
from the ancient past to the present,
serene and ever-present without form—
though unseen, its name remains.
To liken the Sage who attains the Dao for many years,
thus able to assess all *things*’ origins,
knowing they all begin from the Dao—
thus he unfolds it to complete their forms and essences.

Again, when asked how I know
all things derive their *endowment* (资禀) from the Dao,
and that birth and death, beginning and end,
are as they are—

I answer:
The Dao’s dim and vague, profound and obscure,
ever-present without fading—
thus it can respond to change and govern,
in stillness and *non-action*—
assessing the myriad folk’s transformations,
none escape deriving their *endowment* from the Dao—
by this, I know.

The Sage of Great Virtue,
conforms to the Dao—
conforming to the Dao preserves wholeness,
thus it follows: *What is bent becomes whole.*

The *Dao De Jing*’s *Hidden Chamber*:
*Volume Three of the Collected Subtle Chapters* concludes.
*Volume Four of the Collected Subtle Chapters* begins.
Compiled by **Bìxū Zǐ** (碧虚子) **Chén Jǐngyuán** (陈景元).

Then it becomes whole;
what is twisted becomes straight,
what is hollow becomes full,
what is worn becomes renewed,
what is few becomes simple,
what is many becomes confused.

The wise and virtuous scholar,
broadly versed in past and present,
probing the depths and reaching afar,
unimpeded in his singular vision—
though his talents may coil and bend,
they never flaunt themselves—
for he seeks to avoid harm and preserve his body.

Sharp in hearing, keen in perception,
his deeds and achievements manifest,
his heart straight as an arrow,
his resolve taut as a bowstring—
yet he bends himself, humbles himself,
never extending himself—
this is the man of true straightness.

Again, the Sage does not oppose things.
When things come and bend him,
he submits and receives them.
If they reflect inward and know their fault,
then straightness returns of its own accord—
thus it is said: *What is bent becomes whole.*

The rise and fall of hills and valleys,
high and low in constant flux—
valleys and gullies, low and hollow,
draw water to fill them;
peaks and ridges, high and steep,
are eroded by rain and crumble.
When a man is humble and lowly,
the multitude reveres and follows him,
bringing about his radiance and greatness—
thus it is said: *What is hollow becomes full.*

A man of talent, if he fragments his virtue
and wears himself thin,
will be joyfully promoted by the multitude,
his Way ever renewed—
thus it is said: *What is worn becomes renewed.*

Fewness means simplicity.
The *Yì* says:
*"Simple and easy, yet all under heaven’s patterns are achieved."*

The *Xīshēng Jīng* says:
*"Hold to One, and all things are complete."*

Many means extensive learning.
Zhuangzi says:
*"When ornament destroys substance, learning drowns the mind."*

The *Lièzǐ* says:
*"Many paths lead to losing the sheep;
many studies lead to losing the Way."*

Thus the Sage embraces One,
setting the standard for all under heaven.
The *Classic* says:
*"The Dao generates One."*

One is the Dao’s child—
the *Tàijí* (太极),
also the *Húnyuán* (混元),
the pure and unified *qi* of *Tàihé* (太和).

Again, it is *wuwei* (無為).
The Sage embraces and guards
the pure and unified Dao of *Húnyuán*,
restoring the ancient *wuwei* custom,
to serve as the standard for all under heaven.

How is it that One is *wuwei*?
The *Classic* says:
*"Heaven, obtaining One, is clear;
Earth, obtaining One, is tranquil."*

Zhuangzi says:
*"Heaven, through *wuwei*, is clear;
Earth, through *wuwei*, is tranquil."*

Thus it is clear.

From *What is bent becomes whole* downward,
the six matters still bear traces of counteraction—
but *Embrace One* and *wuwei* encompass them all,
thus becoming the standard for all under heaven.

Not self-displaying, hence clear.
Not self-righteous, hence manifest.
Not self-praising, hence effective.
Not self-aggrandizing, hence enduring.

These four matters are all marks of *wuwei*.
The Sage, practicing *wuwei*,
never openly shows his talents—
yet all under heaven naturally acknowledge his clarity.

River-Upper Master says:
*"Though the Sage is clear,
he does not see a thousand *li* ahead—
he uses the eyes of all under heaven to see,
thus his clarity is thorough."*

He who uses the eyes of all under heaven
also does not reveal his own intentions.
Though the words differ, their meaning is similar—
the Sage, empty and tranquil,
never asserts himself while denying others.
Self and other forgotten,
all under heaven naturally acknowledge his correctness,
and his virtue becomes manifest.

The Sage, serene and undisturbed,
never boasts of his merits—
yet all under heaven naturally acknowledge his achievements.

The Sage, still and silent,
never boasts of his worth—
yet all under heaven naturally acknowledge his virtue
and his enduring presence.

Only he who does not contend
cannot be contended with by all under heaven.

The *Honglie Jie* says:
*"When Duke Jian of Zhao died and was not yet buried,
Zhongmou surrendered to Qi.
After five days of burial,
Duke Xiang raised an army to attack it.
Before the siege was complete, a thousand *zhang* of the city wall collapsed.
Duke Xiang struck the bell and withdrew.
His officers advised:
‘Your Highness punishes Zhongmou’s crime,
yet the city wall collapses—
this is Heaven’s aid. Why withdraw?’
Duke Xiang said:
‘I was taught by Master Shùxiàng:
*A gentleman does not exploit another’s advantage,
nor force him into peril.
Let him fortify his city,
then attack when it is complete.’
Zhongmou, hearing this, begged to surrender.
Thus it is said:
*Only he who does not contend
cannot be contended with by all under heaven.*"

The ancient saying of *What is bent becomes whole*—
is this mere empty speech?
Truly whole, and returned to its source.

The Sage, pure and unified,
practicing *wuwei*,
how could he harbor contentious intentions?
The *Classic* says:
*"His virtue is as thick as that of an infant—
poisonous insects do not sting him,
ferocious beasts do not clutch him,
swooping crows do not peck him.
Even beasts behave thus—
how much more so for humans!"*

Yet in ancient times, there were these words of *bending to wholeness*—
are they not today’s parables?
If a man practices them,
truly attaining the perfection of virtue,
and returning it to himself—
this is the profound intent urged again and again.

Bending to wholeness, embracing One,
not boasting, not praising—
not boasting nor praising leads to few words naturally.

Few words naturally,
like gusts of wind that do not last till morning,
or sudden rains that do not last till evening.
The *Yì* says:
*"The words of the virtuous are few;
the words of the hasty are many.
Value their rarity and guard against their excess."*

Few and sparse words align with nature.
The Sage responds when questioned,
speaks when engaging with others,
moves and rests according to the time—
thus aligning with nature.

To explain winds and rains that come and go as they should
is beneficial to all things.
Violent and sudden, they do not persist—
*piāo* (飘) means fierce,
*zòu* (骤) means abrupt.

From dawn to midday is *zhōngzhāo* (终朝).
From early to late is *zhōngrì* (终日).

When mountains and marshes connect,
they produce *piāo* winds.
When *yin* and *yang* surge violently,
they produce *zòu* rains—
this arises from *yin* and *yang* losing their proper order,
the harmonious *qi* not blending—
thus they cannot endure long.

「To liken this to humanity—
when words and deeds clash,
joy and anger are unchecked,
how could purity and harmony not be lost?

Who causes this?
The heavens and the earth.

The heavens and the earth cannot endure long,
how much less can humanity?

If one asks who makes the *piāo* winds and *zòu* rains,
the answer is: it is the heavens and the earth.

The grandest forms are none greater than the heavens and the earth,
the broadest *qi* none more vast than *yin* and *yang*.

When *yin* and *yang* collide,
when the heavens and earth shift violently,
they produce fierce winds and sudden rains—
yet these cannot last even half a day,
how much less can humanity dwell between the heavens and earth,
like a single hair upon a horse’s body?

How dare one indulge desires and surrender to joy and anger,
reckless and abrupt without restraint,
rushing toward swift ruin?

This is not the Dao.

Those who follow the Dao align with the Dao;
those who embody virtue align with virtue;
those who stray align with loss.

*From* (从) means to act in accordance,
to follow naturally.

The Dao is the natural, boundless,
tranquil, simple, and effortless Way.

When humans act, they should align with the Dao,
restrain their words and desires,
and preserve the harmony of heaven—
how could they be like fierce winds and sudden rains,
only to vanish soon?

Yet those who follow the Dao:
in motion, they ride the waves of *yang*;
in stillness, they rest in the virtue of *yin*,
gathering to form substance,
scattering to become *qi*—
emerging without return,
they walk the Way of subtlety.

Those who embody virtue:
within, they fulfill all their duties,
never losing their spirit,
without;
they benefit all things,
extending kindness even to weeds and grasses,
completing deeds without claiming credit,
and thus align with the highest virtue.

Those who stray:
take pleasure in gluttony and greed,
consider bonds and servitude as honor,
toil like beasts without remorse,
and gladly court ruin—
they align with loss.

Those who align with the Dao,
the Dao will find them.
Those who align with virtue,
virtue will find them.
Those who align with loss,
loss will find them.

*"Faith is insufficient—
there is also unfaith."*

This speaks of how *qi* of like nature responds to one another.

Those who embody the Dao in their being—
not only do they bring joy to the people,
but the sovereign of the Great Dao also finds his match.

Those whose deeds align with the highest virtue—
not only do the people revere them,
but the sages of supreme virtue also find their match.

Those whose hearts are lost in ruin—
not only do they rejoice in their decaying bodies,
but the ranks of the lost also find their match.

Each responds in kind.

Thus, those who trust in the Dao
find their Dao.
Those who trust in virtue
find their virtue.
If one does not trust in the Dao,
or scorns virtue,
then the Dao will not respond,
and virtue will be unattained.

Who in the world truly trusts in this?

Therefore it is said:
*"Faith is insufficient—
there is also unfaith."*

Few words and sparse speech align with nature and the Dao,
virtue and the Way blend harmoniously without arrogance or boasting—
thus, those who follow are not established.

The boastful do not stand,
the presumptuous do not walk.

Boastful—
they rise on their toes to gaze,
then press forward in vain.
Presumptuous—
they grasp at things,
or leap over others in contempt.

The gluttonous and aggressive,
who lift their gaze beyond measure,
seeking favor and glory—
though they may briefly attain it,
it is like stretching their necks and rising on their toes—
how could they stand long?

Yet they lack true talent and ability,
yet they dare to surpass others—
the crowd blocks their path,
so they cannot speak.
Their strides are forced, grasping at things,
their hearts longing for swift success—
how could they ever walk?

The self-praised are not enlightened,
the self-righteous do not shine,
the self-glorified have no merit,
the self-proud do not endure.

*"The crooked becomes whole—
Chapter Twenty-Two—*
the Sage acts without doing,
does not boast or glory,
yet never fails to be manifest.

This chapter speaks of the petty man—
he is not so.

He flaunts his own talents,
obscuring others—
how could his deeds be clear?

He is right in his own eyes,
yet wrongs others—
he praises himself,
yet blames others—
how could his principles be revealed?

His benevolence does not extend to all,
his righteousness does not judge wrongs—
he takes credit for himself,
seeking glory—
how could his governance be effective?

He boasts of his great abilities,
oppressing the weak and foolish—
how could he walk the Dao long?

Of the Dao it is said:
*"Excessive food and superfluous actions—
men may detest them,
yet the Dao does not dwell there."*

These are like blemishes and deformities upon the body.

The actions of self-glorying and the like—
to the Dao of natural effortlessness,
they are like discarded food,
fit only to offend,
or like deformities,
fit only to disgrace.

All things detest them,
how much more would the Dao’s worthy
ever place themselves there?

Boasting, presumption, and superfluous actions—
the Dao does not dwell there,
their traces blend into obscurity—
thus, it follows with *"things blend into obscurity."*

Things blend into obscurity,
existing before the heavens and earth.

Those who blend into obscurity—
they are the origin of the Dao.

Thus, they cannot be seen,
cannot be heard,
cannot be grasped.

The highest principle is profound and ever-present,
thus called *"things blend into obscurity."*

The true Dao flows in myriad paths yet is undivided,
thus called *"obscurity without end."*

Yet obscurity without end cannot be known,
yet all things arise from it—
thus it is said: *"things blend into obscurity."*

Existing before the heavens and earth—
this is the source of the Dao.

The *Classic* says:
*"I do not know whose child is this,
who shaped the Emperor before his birth."*

Zhuangzi says:
*"The Dao precedes the Great Pole,
yet is not high.
It lies beneath the Six Poles,
yet is not deep.
It exists before the heavens and earth,
yet is not long-lived.
It endures through the ancient past,
yet is not old—
these mark the Dao’s grand essence.

Silent and boundless,
it stands alone yet never changes,
circulates endlessly yet never falters—
it may be called the Mother of the world.

Silent—
no sound.
Boundless—
no form.

Though it is said *"things blend into obscurity,"*
seeking its form and voice,
it is like an open window into true emptiness—
no image, no substance—
thus it is called *"silent and boundless."*

The Great Mass stands solitary,
no thing to compare with.
Even form and shadow depend on something else,
yet it is said to transform alone—
how much more the subtle Dao,
vast and clear—
what thing could pair with it?

Thus it stands alone.»

The myriad things though ever-changing, born and dying,
yet the subtle Dao never shifts or alters—
thus it is said, *"never changes."*

Moreover, the Dao’s workings:
when dispersed, the harmonizing *qi* is confined within the Great Void,
when gathered, the pure essence is stored like grains in a granary.
Circulating through the Six Empties,
its applications are boundless—
thus it is said, *"never falters."*

No thing, great or small,
does not rely upon the Dao.
To obtain it is to be whole,
to depart from it is to perish.
It gives birth and completes all things—
all depend upon it—
thus it is called *"the Mother of the world."*

I know not its name,
so I call it *Dao*.
Forced to name it, I say *Great*.

The Great Dao is formless,
so no ear can hear it,
no hand can grasp it.
Since it lacks form, sound, or trace,
its name is unknown.

Yet earlier it was said, *"things blend into obscurity,"*
so it has *body* and *use*.
Once body and use are revealed,
it gives rise to all things.
Examining its use reveals its *virtue*,
so it is named *Dao*.
It encompasses heaven and earth—
its substance is supremely vast—
thus, forced to name it, I say *Great*.

Great is called *Passing*,
Passing is called *Distant*,
Distant is called *Returning*.
Passing—this is *going*.
All great things have boundaries,
yet the Dao is boundless.

Without limit it goes,
without shore it extends—
thus Great is called *Passing*.
The farther it passes, the more distant it grows,
its source cannot be traced—
thus Passing is called *Distant*.
Though distant beyond the Eight Wastes,
it returns to stillness within the inch of the heart,
like a mirror’s clarity—
responding without storing—
thus Distant is called *Returning*.
Returning—this is *restoration*.
To go and return,
to perish and revive,
from yin to yang—
all are the Dao’s transformations.

Thus the Dao is Great,
heaven is Great,
earth is Great,
and the Sage-King is also Great.
Within the *domain*, there are Four Greats,
and the Sage-King occupies one.

The Dao is the origin of heaven and earth,
vast and boundless, encompassing all.
All things obtain it and live;
the people cherish it and honor it—
thus the Dao is Great.

Heaven is inverted above,
moving without rest,
covering all things endlessly—
thus heaven is Great.

Earth is congealed below,
still and unmoving,
yielding and tranquil,
thickly bearing all things endlessly—
thus earth is Great.

The Sage-King is serene and does nothing,
his transformations reach all things,
the leader of the black-haired masses—
he does not dare compare himself to heaven, earth, or the Dao—
thus he is also Great.

The Four Greats within the *domain* are:
the Dao, heaven, earth, and the Sage-King.
*Domain*—this is *limit*.

The Dao encompasses the cosmos,
penetrates the finest hair,
sometimes transcends form,
sometimes dwells within limits.
From earth upward, all belong to heaven—
not the lofty, distant blue.

Heaven is beyond earth,
earth is within heaven.
The Sage-King, honored among humanity,
dwells between the Nine Regions—
all within the *domain*.

Thus the Four Greats within the *domain*.
The Sage-King follows the Dao of heaven and earth,
holds the authority over all things,
and among the Four Greats,
he shares in one.

Zhuangzi said:
*"None are more divine than heaven,
none are more abundant than earth,
none are greater than the Emperor-King.
The Emperor-King’s virtue aligns with heaven and earth—
how could he be careless?"*

Humanity follows earth,
earth follows heaven,
heaven follows the Dao,
the Dao follows *naturalness*.

This admonishes the Sage-King:
to model the Two Poles,
take the Dao as his standard,
and the world will naturally be governed.

The Sage-King, guarding *yin*-like stillness,
bears all without partiality—
this follows earth.
Yet he must not cling to earth’s immutability.
When he moves with *yang*-like vigor,
his rule is impartial—
this follows heaven.
Yet he must not fixate on heaven’s unchanging course.
Instead, he follows *wuwei*,
aligning with the Dao’s substance,
letting all things return to their nature—
this is the Sage-King following heaven and earth as the Dao.

It is not that heaven, earth, and the Dao model each other,
but one should discern the profound intent of the Sage’s teachings—
not merely engaging in empty words.

The *mixed-and-formed* Dao—
this is called *strong naming*.
Strongly naming the Four Greats,
the Sage-King occupies one.
The Sage-King, with heavy things governing light,
thus places the heavy as the root of the light.

Heavy is the root of light,
stillness is the lord of agitation.

Grasses and trees—
their petals and leaves are light and fragile,
perched on branches—
they wither under wind and frost.
But their roots are firm and heavy,
deep below—
nothing can harm them, and they endure.

Likewise, governing the body and the state,
one must take substance and weight as the foundation.

Dragons and serpents,
coiled and submerged in stillness,
can transform and ascend.
Tigers and leopards,
menacing and restless,
are felled by arrows and die young.
Thus, for governing the body,
a tranquil heart brings harmony to all spirits—
no cravings or agitation will harm it.
For governing the state,
a ruler who does nothing brings the people joy and ease—
no scheming ministers will cause chaos.

Thus the noble person walks all day
without leaving his *zhi* and *zhòng*.
*Zhi*—a screen-car, also a heavy wagon.
*Zhòng*—this is *weight* in his conduct.

The perfect person and noble never speak or act lightly.
Whatever they do, they carry their *zhi* and *zhòng*.
Their words must weigh upon matters.
If their words and deeds are thus,
how much more the Dao—
would they dare for an instant depart from it?

Some interpret *zhi* as *stillness*,
also warning of stillness and weight.

Moreover, those who travel far
never abandon their *zhi* and heavy loads—
for their clothing and food depend upon them.
If they depart and discard them,
they risk exhaustion upon the road.

Likewise, the noble person,
if he abandons the Dao’s weight and stillness
to act lightly and agitated—
what greater calamity could there be?

Though adorned with splendor,
he remains serene and detached.
*Splendor*—the appearance of bustling adornment.
*Serene*—this is *ease*.
The perfect person and noble,
ever fearful in their hearts,
do not take ornate splendor as beauty.
They do not indulge in ease and comfort,
but remain detached and distant,
abandoning arrogance and excess—
this too is the essence of guarding weight and stillness.

Some texts read *honor-hall* or *feast-assembly*,
meaning they do not delight in halls of splendor,
but find ease in modest dwellings,
and remain detached and distant.

Alas, how could the ruler of a great realm—
the sovereign of ten thousand chariots—
lightly cast his own weight upon the world?
Lightness leads to losing one’s foundation,
agitation leads to losing one’s sovereignty.

*"Alas"*—this is the language of lament.
For the sovereign of ten thousand chariots,
holding the grandest treasure of rule,
how could he not guard weight and stillness,
but lightly abandon his body to reckless desires?

If a subject fails to be steadfast and weighty,
he loses his own foundation
and forfeits his proper duties.

The ancient text reads:
*"Lightness leads to losing one’s foundation,"*
and if the ruler does not uphold the Way of non-action and serene stillness,
but stirs and disturbs the people,
then he loses his sovereign position
and forfeits the harmony of Heaven.

The old saying goes:
*"If the ruler acts lightly and carelessly, troubling the people,*
*then the people scatter—who would remain as his subjects?"*

If subjects disguise themselves with deceit to seek wealth,
act agitated to gain power and influence,
they invite exile and ruin,
and cannot serve their ruler.

Both meanings are valid.

The method of guarding weight and stillness
is only practiced well by the noble person,
so it follows with *"the noble person’s actions leave no trace."*

*"Noble actions"*—no tracks or edges.
*"Noble speech"*—no flaws or censure.
*"Noble calculation"*—no need for schemes or strategies.
*"Noble closure"*—no doors or bolts yet cannot be opened.
*"Noble binding"*—no ropes or knots yet cannot be undone.
*"Noble actions"*—this means acting in accordance with nature.

The *Yin-Fu Jing* says:
*"To observe the Dao of Heaven, one must follow Heaven’s actions,"*
meaning to observe the natural Way,
which is the original observation.
To follow nature’s actions,
one must hold nothing in hand.
If one holds nothing in observation or action,
this is obtained from the heart,
so even without stepping outside the door,
there are no tracks or disturbances.

*"Noble speech"* is like Zhuangzi’s cup-speech—
words drawn from the center,
ever-renewing variations,
in harmony with nature,
without exceeding the proper boundaries.
Thus, there are no flaws or censures.

*"Noble calculation"* means
to guard the One and do nothing,
to follow the natural course of all things,
allowing their long and short,
broad and narrow,
great and small,
many and few
to each fulfill their proper measure,
without harming their natural forms and capacities.

Zhuangzi said:
*"The One and speech become two,
two and the One become three—*
*from this point onward,*
*even clever schemers cannot grasp it,*
*how much less the ordinary?"*

This is why the noble person
rejects schemes and strategies.
The *Yin-Fu Jing* says:
*"The eight trigrams, the Heavenly Stems, the Earthly Branches—
divine schemes and occult concealments—
the interplay of yin and yang in victory and defeat,*
*clearly advance into the realm of symbols.*
*All this belongs to the study of contrived actions and clever stratagems."*

The perfect person wanders in the initial realm of things,
allows all things to govern themselves without being enslaved by them,
and calculates only his own body—
thus knowing the other.
Hence, he has no need for schemes or strategies.

*"Noble closure"* means the heart’s non-action.
The one whose heart does not act,
though sounds and colors may be before him,
all such phenomena do not enter his ears or eyes—
this is why no doors or bolts are needed,
yet nothing can open it.

*"Horizontal"* is called *guān*,
*"vertical"* is called *zhàng*.

*"Noble binding"* means
to bind affairs with the Dao.
The perfect person is simple and effortless,
skilled at restraining greed and excess with the Dao,
naturally binding emotions and desires,
while maintaining serene stillness without strain.
Thus, he has no need for explanation.

Therefore, the sage is always good at saving people,
so he does not abandon people,
and always good at saving things,
so he does not abandon things—
this is called *"clinging to clarity."*

*"Clinging"* means *secretly applying*.
The sage refers to those who practice the Five Noble Virtues.

The sage harmonizes with nature,
his heart merges with the utmost One,
so he can treat all things—
even the humble and lowly—
as if they were sacred offerings,
and assign the wise and foolish to their proper roles,
allowing people’s nature to remain whole
and their forms complete,
each fulfilling their proper function—
thus, he does not abandon people.

Furthermore, he follows their growth and decline,
respects their capacities and endowments,
ensuring that square and round,
straight and crooked
do not harm Heaven’s principles.
Even to the lowliest tiles and boards,
everyone has their place—
thus, he does not abandon things.

*"Always good"* means
to nurture the constant Dao
while clearly discerning and following the natural course of things.

The *Hong Lie Jie* says:
*"In the past, when Gongsun Long was in Zhao,*
*he told his disciples:*
*'If a man has no ability,*
*I cannot consort with him.'*
*A traveler in coarse clothing and simple belt came to see him,*
*and said:*
*'I can shout.'*
*Gongsun Long turned to his disciples and said:*
*'Among your ranks, is there one who can shout?*
*They replied:*
*'No.'*
*Gongsun Long said:*
*'Enroll him as a disciple.'*
*After several days,*
*the man went to speak with the king,*
*and when he reached the riverbank,*
*he was crossing in a boat,*
*and the traveler shouted once,*
*and the boat came to him.*
*Thus, the sage’s way in the world*
*does not oppose those with extraordinary abilities,*
*so it is said:*
*'He does not abandon people.'*
*Therefore, the good person is the teacher of the ungood,*
*and the ungood person is the resource of the good.*
*The good person refers to those with the Dao.*

*The Classic says:*
*'Establish the Son of Heaven,*
*set the Three Exalted Ministers—*
*this is to teach the ungood.'*
*Thus, it is said:*
*'The good person is the teacher of the ungood.'*
*Even if there are ungood people,*
*the good person also uses and employs them,*
*gradually transforming and guiding them.*

*The Classic says:*
*'The good, I make them good.*
*The ungood, I also make them good,*
*and thus they become good.*
*The faithful, I believe them.*
*The unfaithful, I also believe them,*
*and thus they become faithful.'*
*This is to transform and subdue the ungood through virtue and power,*
*to use them as resources and tools.*

*The Hong Lie Jie says:*
*'The Chu general Zi Fa*
*loved to seek out those with martial skills.*
*There was a man in Chu skilled at theft,*
*who came to see him and said:*
*'I have heard that you seek those with martial skills.*
*I am a thief,*
*and I wish to use my skill to spare one soldier for you.'*
*Zi Fa, upon hearing this,*
*did not bother to adjust his clothes or straighten his cap,*
*but rushed out to greet and honor him.*
*His attendants remonstrated:*
*'A thief is the greatest bandit in the world—*
*why honor him?*
*The general replied:*
*'This is not something you can understand.'*
*Soon after,*
*Ami’s army rose,*
*attacking Chu,*
*and Zi Fa led his troops to meet them.*
*The enemy forces repelled his three times.*
*The worthy and virtuous ministers of Chu*
*all exhausted their schemes and sincerity,*
*yet the Ami army grew stronger.*
*Then the thief came forward and said:*
*'I have a small skill.*
*I wish to use it for you.'*
*Zi Fa replied:*
*'Agreed.'*
*He did not question his words*
*but sent him away."*

The thief then secretly untied the general of Qi’s tent at night and presented it to him. Zi Fa had someone return it, saying: *"A soldier was gathering firewood and found the general’s canopy. I had him return it to the proper authorities."*

The next day, he went again and took the general’s hairpin. Zi Fa once more had it returned.

When the Qi army heard of this, they were greatly alarmed. The general and his officers conferred: *"If we do not leave today, the Chu army may take our heads."* Thus, they withdrew their forces.

A skill may be slight, yet its use profound—it lies in how the ruler employs it. If one does not honor his teacher nor cherish his resources, though he may know the great confusion, this is called the supreme subtlety.
