Though the Sage may wander in the realm of self-realization, he cannot neglect his teacher and resources. Yet even when establishing them, he fears overvaluing his teacher or clinging to his resources, becoming mired in old traces and failing to reach the farthest heights. Hence, he repeatedly emphasizes: *"Do not honor your teacher, do not cherish your resources."*

Though a man relies on his teacher to awaken his initial understanding, he seeks the ultimate truth, which arises from his innate nature—this is the day of true transformation. Thus, the horse expert cannot transform a poor steed into a fine one, nor can a skilled artisan carve inferior wood into beams. Only when one forgets all aspiration can he attain the Dao’s ultimate.

Hence, Liezi, having learned from Lao Shang and befriended Bo Gao, later said: *"I do not know whether my teacher and friend were truly my teacher and friend—this is the true forgetting of teacher and resource."*

Though the Sage knows the petty man is rigid and his teachings may seem misleading, he still instructs the superior man, urging both to forget, and thus speaks of the supreme subtlety.

Good deeds and good words are what save people and things. To be a teacher or resource is to know softness and hardness.

Thus, next comes knowing the雄 (*xiong*—masculine, strong, preeminent). Knowing the雄, guarding the雌 (*ci*—feminine, yielding, retreat), makes one the valley of the world.

To be the valley of the world is to preserve one’s virtue unchanging, returning to the infant.

The雄 is exalted strength, precedence. The雌 is humility, weakness, retreat. To know one’s own exaltation, to stand out before others—if indulged, it breeds arrogance and invites calamity. Instead, one must dwell in humility, guard softness and weakness, be modest and retreat, and the world will submit, as water flows into deep valleys. When one’s heart and mind are like a valley, one preserves virtue unchanging, abandons harsh desires, and returns to the innocence of infancy.

Infancy is the pure, unadulterated harmony where distinctions of right and wrong are extinguished.

Knowing the white, guarding the black, makes one the model of the world. To be the model of the world is to preserve virtue without excess, returning to the boundless.

The man of the Dao, whose heart and mind are like a valley, generates clarity in emptiness—bright and distinct—yet he guards his depth and silence, holding himself in obscurity, making his light shine without dazzling. This can be the model for the world.

Those who take him as their model see his virtue without excess, aligning with the Dao’s true essence.

Knowing honor, guarding humiliation, makes one the valley of the world. To be the valley of the world is to make virtue complete, returning to simplicity.

Though the man of the Dao may be taken as a model, he must guard humiliation, hold victory in self-effacement, and let the world’s heart turn to him, as water flows into valleys. His capacity is like a valley—virtue is full yet nameless, returning to the Dao’s simplicity. Simplicity means concealing talent and hiding use.

When simplicity disperses, it becomes an instrument. The Sage uses it as an official, thus the great order is not violated.

Returning to the Dao’s simplicity means merging into the boundless, where vision and hearing find no grasp—this is the body’s attainment of true reality. Yet in governing, one must disperse and become an instrument.

River Master said: *"When the simplicity of all things disperses, it becomes an instrument. Used according to the Dao, it becomes divine clarity, flowing as the sun and moon, dividing into the Five Phases."*

When a man’s true heart disperses, it becomes thought and deliberation. A single movement of thought brings forth myriad actions and all affairs. Affairs are coarse instruments.

The Sage takes their talents and abilities, appointing them as leaders of the hundred officials, thus able to govern all things naturally without harm.

Lu Xi Sheng said: *"Ah, the great Dao! It abandons benevolence and righteousness. When simplicity disperses into instruments, the Sage uses them, thus able to lead the myriad coarse things. The great Dao is not an instrument, yet it uses these instruments. The great order is not violated, yet it governs all things. If one is not merged in the Dao yet seeks to use the world’s instruments, constrained by things and attempting to govern the world’s talents, I must see his ruin."*

Knowing the雄, guarding the雌, practicing virtue like a valley yielding downward. Knowing the white, guarding the black, obscuring oneself as the world’s model.

When the model is applied, one may wish to take the world. Yet to take the world by force—*I see it as inevitable failure.*

The Dao acts without action, naturally. Even the slightest matter follows it, let alone the world. If one does not follow its natural course but relies on boldness, seeking to forcibly take the world’s great instrument and indulge in artificial action, not only will he lose the Dao—I must see him perish.

*"Perish"* means *"die."*

The world’s divine instrument cannot be forced. To force it is to ruin it; to grasp it is to lose it. The divine instrument is the supreme position of heaven and earth, the divine clarity of the cosmos. Man is the divine thing of the world.

Zhuangzi said: *"I have heard of nurturing the world, not governing it."* *"Nurturing"* means fearing the world will corrupt its nature. *"Governing"* means fearing the world will stray from its virtue.

The world does not corrupt its nature nor stray from its virtue—how then could there be one who governs it? This is called *not* governing the world.

Not governing the world means following the natural course without action, letting things unfold spontaneously, so that the world is at peace and the divine things are tranquil.

If one does not follow the natural course without action but instead acts, it is like a clumsy woodcutter—though he applies his skill, he ruins more than he accomplishes. Thus, when the seven orifices were pierced, Pangu died; when the horse was whipped and urged, its strength was exhausted. Is this not the case where action brings ruin?

And yet, if one clings stubbornly and refuses to yield, claiming to be a sage of governance, not only does one lose the ultimate truth, but one will also lose one’s own authenticity.

Thus, things may prosper or decline, be molded or shattered, be strong or weak, bear loads or collapse. These eight outcomes prove that external things cannot be relied upon.

In the world, some act with sincere intent, while others follow with insincere motives. Sincerity brings order, insincerity brings chaos. Order arises naturally, while chaos demands action.

Some praise with warmth—those who extol and perfect the virtues of others—or criticize with coldness—those who slander and expose the faults of others. Praise with warmth signifies wealth and nobility, while cold criticism signifies poverty and lowliness, just as spring and summer nurture while autumn and winter wither. The affairs of the world change and transform in this way.

Some see strength and support it, others see weakness and suppress it. Some bolster the weak to make them strong, others oppress the strong to make them weak—these are the inclinations of human nature.

Some bear burdens and make them stable, others topple them and make them precarious. From this point onward, all are deviations from nature, relying solely on artificial action and boldness, seeking to take and thus bringing about these flaws.

Therefore, the sage avoids excess, avoids extravagance, avoids extravagance. Excess, extravagance, and extravagance are all transgressions beyond measure.

Thus, the sage who governs the world knows that calamity and blessing depend on each other, that blessing and calamity lie latent. Abandoning artificial action, returning to desirelessness, discarding extreme contrivance and returning to nature, abandoning luxurious adornment and returning to simplicity, abandoning excessive desires and returning to tranquility—though excess, extravagance, and extravagance are three distinct names, they are all facets of the unity of *wuwei* and naturalness. Through these excesses and extravagances, artificial action arises, which is why the Old Master issued profound warnings.

If one insists on forcing, ruin is inevitable. If one coerces the weak and strong, tilts the balance, indulges in extravagance and excess, it is better to assist the ruler with the Dao than to persist in such folly.

Thus, the *True Scripture of Dao and Virtue*—*Hidden Chamber of Reverence*—Chapter Four concludes.
The *True Scripture of Dao and Virtue*—*Compilation of Subtleties*—Chapter Five begins.

「Bìxū Zǐ Chén Jǐngyuán compiled: *The Dao Scripture*—*On Using the Dao to Assist the Ruler*—states, *"Do not use military force to coerce the world; such actions tend to rebound."* Here, *以* means *to employ*.

The virtuous minister, employing the Dao of naturalness, assists the sage ruler by first clarifying the *Heavenly Mandate* while *Dao and Virtue* follow, then *Dao and Virtue* illuminated, *benevolence and righteousness* follow, and so on—*benevolence and righteousness* clarified, *allocation and defense* follow; *allocation and defense* clarified, *form and name* follow; *form and name* clarified, *fitness and responsibility* follow; *fitness and responsibility* clarified, *self-reflection and restraint* follow; *self-reflection and restraint* clarified, *discrimination of right and wrong* follows; *discrimination of right and wrong* clarified, *rewards and punishments* follow. This is the method of using the Dao to assist the sage ruler.

Thus, one who holds the *Great Principle* (*dàxiàng*) will govern the world without harming its peace and stability. Military force is a cruel instrument, and warfare is a perilous affair—how can one display military strength to the world? The signs of war lie in the heart: harboring resentment before conflict is war, hasty speech and stern expressions are war, arrogant words and reckless challenges are war, indulging in battles and assaults is war. These four are the seeds of great and petty strife alike.

Moreover, when humans oppress things violently, things will inevitably retaliate—this is called *"beasts driven to desperation will strike."* Hence, it is said, *"such actions tend to rebound."*

Where troops are stationed, thorns and brambles grow; after great armies pass, calamitous years follow. Where soldiers dwell, they bring destruction: they plunder the people, obstruct agriculture, leaving fields barren and homes exposed, thorns and brambles to flourish wildly. Furthermore, once great armies have passed, slaughter knows no bounds, and the grievances of life and death disrupt the harmonious *qi*, causing *yin-yang* disturbances—surely floods and droughts will follow.

For one who governs his own body and seeks to resolve his troubles, military campaigns become burdensome like troops, vexations like thorns, cravings like great armies, and illness like calamitous years. Following righteousness with reckless abandon, one cannot fathom its depths.

Thus, the wise act decisively yet without arrogance, never daring to employ force for coercion. Act decisively yet do not boast, act decisively yet do not glory in victory, act decisively yet do not be proud. This is called *good use*.

The harm of military force is something even the enlightened abhor. Yet, even the sagest of rulers never abandon it entirely, for its origins are noble and profound. Gēngsāng Zǐ said: *"The roots of war lie in humanity’s very beginning."* Again, he said: *"There are those who die from taking medicine, yet one cannot forbid all physicians in the world. There are those who die from boarding ships, yet one cannot forbid all ships in the world. There are those who lose their nations through war, yet one cannot forbid all military forces in the world."*

War cannot be abolished, like water and fire. Good use brings fortune, poor use brings calamity. Hence, anger and corporal punishment must not be indulged in the home, legal punishments must not be indulged in the state, and military campaigns must not be indulged in the world.

The sage rulers of antiquity had *righteous wars* yet no *indulged wars*. Therefore, one who uses the Dao to assist the ruler must uphold *feminine tranquility*, never daring to coerce the world with military force. When forced to respond, they do so with restraint—this is called *good use*.

Decisive (*guǒ*) means *courageous*. Speaking of the good use of troops, it means courage in resolving crises, not daring to employ weapons to coerce the world. Though there is courage in resolving crises and repelling enemies, it must all be done without coercion.

Things grow strong only to decline—this is called *not the Dao*. *Not the Dao* is early decay. Though one may act decisively to resolve crises and repel enemies, it is all done without coercion. Only violence for the sake of violence is to be avoided; decisive action must be for righteousness alone, not for the love of war.

The growth of grass and trees is soft and tender; when they reach their peak, they wither and fall. Human infancy is marked by softness and weakness; when one reaches his prime, he grows old and frail. This illustrates that military might and strength lead only to ruin—once ruined, one cannot claim to follow the Dao.

Unrighteous acts cannot be emulated, and actions contrary to the Dao are better stopped early. Hence, *righteous wars* prevail, *responsive wars* succeed, *wrathful wars* perish, and *arrogant wars* are destroyed.

The good use of military force lies in decisive yet restrained action—without boasting, without wrath, without greed, without arrogance—responding only when forced, with righteousness in repelling enemies and saving people, not relying on strength or craving battle.

Governing one’s body and resolving troubles means: *"The withering of a man is due to his failure to follow the Dao; failing to follow the Dao means early death."*

To assist the ruler, one must rely on *Dao and Virtue*, not on fine soldiers and military force. Thus, it proceeds to discuss *fine soldiers*.

Fine soldiers are instruments of ill omen, and things often loathe them. Hence, those who follow the Dao do not dwell upon them. *佳* (*jiā*) means *to favor* or *to esteem*. To favor and esteem weapons and armor as objects of luxury is to esteem what is not good.

Weapons and armor, which harm the form and spirit of men, are only enjoyed by the cruel and obstinate. Even ordinary things may be loathed, how much more so by those who follow the Dao?

The gentleman values the left when at home, but the right when in war. The Dao of Heaven and Earth is such that the left is *yang*—generative, soft, and weak—while the right is *yin*—punitive, hard, and strong.

Thus, in times of peace, the gentleman esteems those of virtue to dwell on his left, but in times of war, he esteems the courageous to stand on his right.

Weapons are instruments of ill omen, not for the gentleman. This is a reiteration to emphasize the point—*the Scripture explaining itself*.

Since weapons are instruments of killing, of hardness and strength, and their purpose is to harm, they are indeed instruments of ill omen.

The gentleman takes *wuwei* and naturalness as his heart, and *Dao and Virtue*, *benevolence and righteousness* as his practice. Military force is only for defense and preparedness, not an instrument the gentleman habitually employs.

When forced to use it, he does so with calm restraint. Barbarians and savages, bandits and deceitful thieves—these are the reasons why the gentleman, who follows the Dao, may be forced to use military force. Yet he never initiates war, so he does not dare to be the aggressor but only the defender, nor to advance an inch when he must retreat a foot—all these are done without coercion.

Righteous wars are used to conquer and settle chaos, to respond to enemies—never for the sake of seizing land or treasure.»

Zhuangzi said: *"When the market-seller Yi Liao of Shì Nán played with his ball and resolved the feud between the two families, and when Sun Shu Ao took his jade staff and feathered robe while the people of Ying cast down their weapons, this is what is called victory without battle—calm and tranquil is the highest. To resolve without disorder is called tranquil; to quiet the mind is called serene. When matters arise and one remains unmoved and unperturbed, this is the method for cultivating oneself.*

*To win yet not take pleasure in it, but others do—this is one who delights in killing. He who delights in killing cannot achieve his will in the world. The great virtue of Heaven and Earth is *generation*; this is why all beings hold it in common esteem. Now, if one cannot win over others with virtue but instead raises armies, wages war, and seeks victory through slaughter, how can this be the conduct of one who follows the Dao?*

*The *Lüzi* says: When Zhao Xiangzi sent his retainers to attack Zhi and won yet wore a worried expression, this was the conduct of a virtuous ruler holding victory—thus it is said, *‘to win yet not take pleasure in it.’* Yet if there are those who praise this, it is because they delight in killing. Those who delight in killing are not only shunned by others but will themselves be slain. These are the cruel and violent who love slaughter—such men must not be allowed to achieve their will in the world. If they do, they will surely defy Heaven’s virtue, unleash indiscriminate slaughter, and how could Heaven then aid them?

*In auspicious rites, the left is honored; in funeral rites, the right is honored. The *Pianjiang* (Vanguard General) stands on the left, the *Shangjiang* (Commander-in-Chief) on the right—this is to treat them as in mourning rites. The left is *yang*, the principle of life, so in ordinary times the left is honored—this is auspicious. The right is *yin*, the principle of death, so in funeral rites the right is honored—this is inauspicious. The *Liji·Tan Gong* says: The Master stood with his disciples in a respectful bow, all facing right. The Master said: ‘Your eagerness to learn is admirable. As for me, I am mourning my sister.’ The disciples should then face left. The Commander-in-Chief, who has the power of life and death, stands on the right; the Vanguard General, who does not, stands on the left—this shows that the conduct of war follows the same principle as funeral rites. Now, if the Commander-in-Chief stands on the right, it is because he is treated as in mourning rites. When many are slain, one weeps in sorrow; when victorious, one is treated as in mourning rites. Truly, righteous war is waged only when there is no alternative. Victory brings many deaths, yet one does not take pleasure in it—thus one weeps in sorrow. The Commander-in-Chief stands on the right, for the right is the position of mourning—this is why.*

*The military is an inauspicious instrument, to be used only when there is no alternative. The gentleman values it only as a secondary matter, placing *Dao* and its constant practice above all else. Thus it is said: *‘Dao is constant and nameless.’* Though crude and small, the uncarved block (*pu*) is not dared to be enslaved by the world. If lords and kings could guard it, all things would come of their own accord. The Dao, in its greatness, is not exhausted; in its smallness, it is not neglected. It encompasses all things, penetrating the minutest details. Empty within yet full without, it contains *yin* within *yang*—this is the Dao, not the Dao of names, nor the names of the Dao. Thus it is said: *‘Dao is constant and nameless.’*

*Once nameless, it is not an instrument. Before instruments are manifest, it is called *pu*. The Dao is subtle and mysterious: unseen when viewed, unheard when listened to—this is called *small*. The world’s talents and tools have bright eyes to enslave sight, keen ears to enslave hearing, minds to enslave will, courage to enslave strength, eloquence to enslave speech, and skill to enslave action. All these named things are what the *wuwei* (non-acting) uses to govern. Only the nameless *pu* of the Dao cannot be enslaved by the world. If lords and kings could hold fast to the root of *wuwei*, vague yet present above, precious yet empty within, then external things could not burden their authenticity, desires could not deceive their spirit, and all things would come of their own accord. The four classes would have none who did not submit.*

*When Heaven and Earth unite in *wuwei*, sweet dew descends. No one commands it, yet it is evenly distributed. Heaven’s *wuwei* causes *yin* qi to descend; Earth’s *wuwei* causes *yang* qi to rise. When both act in *wuwei*, harmonious qi and sweet dew descend, bringing auspicious omens—this is the way of lords and kings. Heaven and Earth’s virtue is harmonious and divine. The *Classic* says: *‘I act not, yet the people transform themselves; I do not praise, yet the people return to simplicity.’* This is to say that without command, things naturally follow and become balanced.*

*At first, there was the Dao—nameless. Once named, one must know when to stop. Knowing when to stop is how one avoids peril. The beginning was the Dao—nameless, the constant and nameless Dao. Named things are the instruments of all things. The Dao, at its beginning, was nameless, yet it could govern all named things—this is called *pu*. When dispersed, it becomes instruments; when the sage uses it, it becomes officials and rulers. Named instruments are all the possessions of the king. Once distinctions are established, ranks are set—none may transgress above or below. Thus one must know when to stop. To know when to stop is to know oneself—this is wisdom. To know oneself and others is enlightenment. How could there then be peril?*

*The Master of Riverbank (Heshang Gong) wrote: *‘Heaven too will know it.’* This means that if one follows the Dao and practices virtue, Heaven too will recognize it. The analogy is that the Dao in the world is like streams and valleys to the rivers and seas. The Dao pervades the world without exclusion: above, it reaches the Celestial Capital and Purple Bureaus; below, it reaches the humble and lowly. To follow it brings prosperity; to defy it brings ruin. If lords and kings guard *wuwei* and tranquility, they will above attract harmonious qi and below win the people’s hearts. Root and branch will be in accord, and none will escape its blessings. This is like the flow of streams and valleys, which, when aligned with the rivers and seas, nourish all things, but when obstructed, become turbulent and turn to sand.*

*To guard the Dao is to have all things submit of their own accord. To know oneself and others is wisdom.*

「To discern others is called wisdom, hence knowing speech is how one discerns others—this is the wisdom of adapting to people, yet not knowing oneself. To know oneself and hold fast in silence is called clarity, hence knowing without speaking is how one knows Heaven—this is the self-adaptation of clarity alone, which transcends wisdom.

To overcome others is to have strength; to overcome oneself is to be mighty. To overcome others is not the Dao of enduring victory. The Dao of enduring victory is called might. Might, though strong and forceful, when encountering one who surpasses it, will falter. But to overcome oneself is the Dao of constant victory. The Dao of constant victory is called yielding. Yielding can humble itself, and things will naturally defer to it—this is called not overcoming yet overcoming oneself, not assuming burdens yet bearing them. It is ever serene and never falters; what else could be called might?

To know contentment is to be wealthy; to persist in strong conduct is to have resolve. To know contentment means to curb one’s desires. When desire does not indulge in excess, frugality suffices, and this may be called wealth. To persist in strong conduct means to exert oneself diligently. Diligence in action will surely succeed, and this may be called having resolve.

Master Zhuangzi said: Master Zeng lived in Wei, his patched robe without lining, his face swollen and his voice hoarse, his hands and feet calloused. For three days he did not light a fire, and for ten years he did not make new clothes. His crown was upright but its ribbon frayed, his collar was grasped yet his elbows exposed, his shoes were worn but their heels stacked, and as he pulled his belt he sang the *Shang* hymns, his voice filling the heavens and earth, as if issuing from metal and stone. The Son of Heaven could not find a subject like him, nor any lord a friend. Thus, those who cultivate resolve forget form, those who cultivate form forget profit, and those who perfect the Dao forget the self.

This is what is meant by knowing contentment is to be wealthy, and persisting in strong conduct is to have resolve. To never lose one’s place is to endure; to die yet not perish is longevity. *Place* refers to Heaven’s bestowal of life and death, adversity and fortune, nobility and humility, poverty and wealth. To preserve its natural order and accept it as one’s fate, without letting gain or loss unsettle the heart—this is how one endures. Thus, when alive, one does not take life as joy, knowing its inevitable course; when facing death, one does not take death as sorrow, knowing its inevitable return. To follow one’s fate in uprightness and wait for its end, without harming life through self-destruction—this is called longevity.

Master Yan Junping said: Not knowing others means one cannot navigate affairs; not navigating affairs means one cannot engage with the world. Not knowing oneself means one cannot know Heaven; not knowing Heaven means one cannot foresee what has not yet come to pass. Not overcoming others means one cannot stand above; not standing above means one cannot rule. Not overcoming oneself means one cannot attain self-mastery; not attaining self-mastery means one cannot win others. Not knowing contentment means one cannot grasp wealth; not grasping wealth means one cannot curb desire. Not persisting in strong conduct means one cannot align with the Dao; not aligning with the Dao means one cannot find harmony. Without harmony, one cannot endure in life; not enduring in life means one cannot nurture the spirit. When the spirit is not cultivated, one cannot attain longevity.

Therefore, to establish oneself in the world and benefit others while removing harm, to interact with people and adapt to change—nothing is broader than knowledge. To gather one’s intelligence and cultivate clarity, to found a state and govern its people, to realize the intent of the Dao and the mind of Heaven—nothing is greater than self-knowledge. To wield authority and govern the people, to establish laws and rites, to set forth transformations and issue commands, to rectify the ministers of the land and the lords of the states—nothing is more noble than overcoming others. To uphold virtue and morality, to align with the divine and luminous, to receive from Heaven and Earth, to harmonize yin and yang, to act or rest with measured advance and retreat—nothing is more exalted than overcoming oneself.

To govern one’s household and preserve the state, to bring the people ease and joy, to dwell in harmony with reverence and caution, to practice kindness and filial piety while fearing the law—nothing is higher than knowing contentment. To wander freely in the divine and luminous, to be tranquil and at peace, to be honored and adorned—nothing is better than finding harmony.

To serve in office and fulfill duties, to attend to superiors and oversee inferiors, to complete others’ undertakings and continue their legacies, to extend these to all the people—nothing surpasses what is enduring. What Heaven and Earth value, what all creatures depend upon, to dwell in without ever tiring, to rejoice in without ever ceasing, where all blessings flourish and none can rival its splendor—this is called longevity.

Master Lu Xisheng said: Knowledge that comes from without is called wisdom, like the external radiance of the sun and fire. Knowledge that returns within is called clarity, like the internal reflection of gold and water. To use yielding strength to overcome others’ violence is the force of the Daoist Way; to uphold yielding to overcome one’s own restlessness is the might of virtue. To know contentment in matters of profit and desire is to not lose one’s great undertaking, hence it is called wealth and might.

To practice benevolence and righteousness is to reach eminent virtue, hence it is called having resolve. To act without ever losing one’s place is to endure; to die yet the Dao not perish—this is called longevity. To know others is to overcome others; to know oneself is to know Heaven. To never lose one’s place and to die yet not perish, drifting freely without attachment, attaining the Dao—this is why it is followed by the great hymn: *"The great Dao drifts, drifting, its left and right unbound."* The great Dao drifts, drifting—without clinging or attachment. The Dao is supremely high without equal, supremely deep without bottom. It is level as a carpenter’s plumb and straight as a mason’s line, round as a compass and square as a ruler. It encompasses the universe without exterior, and penetrates all things without limit. It drifts, drifts, filling the boundless expanse, yet without clinging or attachment—this is the very body of the Dao. Whether it manifests on the left or the right, all things meet their source, and none escape its reach—this is the very use of the Dao. All things rely upon it for life yet it does not decline; its work is done yet it claims no credit. To rely means to depend. Heaven depends on the Dao’s yang-qi to move and turn above, Earth depends on the Dao’s yin-qi to rest and settle below. Though Heaven and Earth are vast, they still depend on the Dao for its function. How much more so the myriad things, which all arise from it! Moreover, the great Dao is without sentiment, giving birth to Heaven and Earth; how could it ever refuse to labor for the myriad things? Yet again, the great Dao is without form, shaping the world without material; though all things have names of reliance and dependence, when tracing their origins...」

「Lofty and singularly transformative, the Dao gives rise to things as they change and yet remains self-generated—thus leaving no trace of reliance or repayment. The Sage’s accomplishments are complete yet he does not claim them as his own; this is the very use of the Dao/Way.

He nurtures the myriad things yet does not lord over them, ever without desire, and thus may be called *virtue/power* in its small aspect. The Dao can cover and nourish all beings, yet it does not expect their gratitude in return. Hence, the Sage cultivates benevolence and love to sustain all classes of beings, allowing each to fulfill its nature and grow without restraint. The *Sutra/Scripture* says: *"Ever without desire, one beholds its subtlety; subtlety allows entry into all things, and thus one may name the Dao’s simplicity as small."* One variant reads: *"The Dao clothes and covers all things"*—here, *clothes and covers* means *cover and nourish*.

When all things return to it yet it does not claim lordship, this may be called *virtue/power* in its great aspect. The Dao covers and sustains all things, encompassing them without exclusion; all things return to it yet it remains undifferentiated, undifferentiated yet not claiming lordship. Zhuangzi says: *"Undifferentiated yet encompassing all things—this is what is called great. Greatness contains all things, and thus the Dao may be called great."* Therefore, the Sage never claims to be great, and thus he achieves true greatness. The Sage holds to the Dao, humbly calling himself *孤 (Gū, solitary)*, *寡 (guǎ, few)*, and *不谷 (bùgǔ, not plentiful)*, demonstrating his refusal to claim greatness.

All nations return to him, yet his joy in promoting them never wanes—this is how he achieves true greatness. Du Guangting says: *"The Sage loves the people and relieves the suffering of all things, extending his virtue to great and small alike—this may be called small virtue/power."* Yet, he allows all things to follow their nature and attributes his achievements to Heaven—this may also be called great virtue/power.

The Dao/Way applies its transformations, dispenses its virtue to humanity, and stirs the pure and harmonious winds, while clothing the world with its clear and tranquil governance. Forgetting his own deeds, not exalting himself above others, his grand achievements may be called great, and his virtue may endure forever—because he does not claim greatness, he attains this very eminence.

Those who cultivate their personal conduct are expansive and unconfined, like clouds without form or water without vessel. They may turn left or right, adapt to every shape and direction, yet remain unconstrained by fixed norms. When things come, they respond; when they reflect, they broaden. They do not boast of their responsive and expansive nature, though their benefits to all things are manifold. Benevolence extends even to the lowliest creatures, yet he never makes it his own. All good deeds converge in harmony, yet he does not claim them as his own.

Thus, he manifests the virtue/power that is neither small nor great, bearing no name of self-exaltation or self-denigration, and may be said to align with the complete and true Dao.

The great Dao is vast and boundless, yet left or right, all things meet its source. When all things return to it, this is because he holds to the great principle and its manifestations—thus, it follows with the next step: holding to the great manifestations.

When he holds to the great manifestations, all under Heaven proceed, yet they do so without harm, in peace and stability. The great manifestations are the great principles. The eight trigrams and nine categories are what is meant here.

The rulers of the *太古 (Tàigǔ, Great Antiquity)* governed with no action, relying solely on the virtue/power of Heaven. When the Sage established the trigrams and observed the manifestations, the distinctions of auspicious and inauspicious became clear, and transformations arose—thus there came to be gains and losses, advances and retreats.

When the vast and primordial transformations first emerged, the governance of the great principles began to shape the world, and the world’s original purity and simplicity departed. *往 (wǎng, to proceed/leave)* here means *to depart/abandon*.

Once the original purity and simplicity have departed, the great principle may still be upheld—thus, to proceed does not bring harm. To proceed without harm is because he acts according to the times, establishes achievements based on resources, assigns roles according to ability, and does not damage their inherent nature—thus all under Heaven are at peace and stable.

Yan Junping says: *"The Dao has no form, so Heaven and Earth rely on it to give birth. The Dao has no possession, so yin and yang rely on it to begin. The Dao has no fixed principle, so the four seasons rely on it for their work. The Dao has no manifestation, so all classes of beings rely on it to proceed."* Thus, the great principle has no principle, the great manifestation has no manifestation; greatness is without limit, yet it is not without possession. It gives birth where there is no birth, and denies where there is no denial—so the Dao achieves without action, and Heaven and Earth are thus formed; virtue/power is realized without labor, and all things find their place. What more need be done?

Not without limit yet not without possession, not acting yet not denying—here the Dao finds its own way, and all things find their own way. This is what is called the virtue/power of Heaven.

If the Dao waits for transformations to occur through deliberate action before they manifest, then what it achieves will be few. If virtue/power waits for labor to arise before it applies its function, then what all things receive will be sparse. This is what is meant by gains and losses, advances and retreats—thus the following passage states:

*"Music and baits: passing guests will stop."* This is an illustrative metaphor.

When music’s harmony is played, people must linger briefly. When the *鐸饵 (duò’ěr, ritual bells and lures)* are beautiful, guests must pause.

When music is exhausted, the ears are filled yet pass onward. When the baits are overindulged, the tongue grows weary and recoils. When guests stop, the host grows weary and takes offense.

When the law first arises, the people hold it dear and praise it. But when its flaws appear, they fear it and scorn it. Zhuangzi says: *"Benevolence and righteousness are the ancestral halls of the former kings, where one may stay but one night, yet not dwell long-term. To observe and then criticize excessively—this is the proper use of law."* If it lingers in the world and grows corrupt, becoming stagnant and enduring, then reason must fail.

Once reason has failed, how could proceeding not bring harm?

「Once having suffered its harm, then how could one speak of its peace and tranquility?

The Dao/Way, when uttered, is bland and without flavor.

When music is exhausted and the baits are overindulged, the ears and tongue grow weary and recoil in excess. When the great law shows its flaws, fear and scorn become manifest.

Yet the Dao/Way, when spoken of, is bland and without taste—this is what is meant by *"truthful words are not beautiful."* In stillness and simplicity, without contrivance, the people delight in their nature, thus they content with full bellies and dwell in ease, lost in the boundless and indistinct, unaware of their whereabouts.

Could there ever be harmonious melodies or sweet flavors to be savored and chewed upon?

The Dao/Way is unseen when looked upon, unheard when listened to, and cannot be exhausted when applied.

For laws are shaped by contrivance, and those who cling to them will find their limits.

The Dao/Way is natural and without contrivance, yet neither sounds nor colors can fathom its depths.

Thus it is said: *"Unseen when looked upon, unheard when listened to—from ancient times to today, its name never fades."* The common folk use it daily without knowing its true nature, yet it cannot be exhausted when applied.

The *象 (xiàng, form/manifestation)* without form means there is nothing to grasp. Without grasping, its application cannot be exhausted. But if one grasps it and claims possession, how could one avoid the extremes of excess and deficiency? Hence, it is followed by the principle: *"When about to exhaust, first fully extend. When about to weaken, first fully strengthen. When about to abandon, first fully establish. When about to take away, first fully bestow."*

This is called *微明 (wēimíng, subtle clarity)*.

Many learned interpreters of this chapter have offered varied explanations, yet none align with its proper structure. Some cite Confucius: *"One may follow the Way with them, but not yet establish it; one may establish it with them, but not yet wield it with subtlety."* Others draw parallels with statecraft and strategic maneuvering, akin to the *纵横 (zōnghéng, sophistry/scheming)* school—equally unreliable. This is truly in harmony with the *阴符 (Yīnfú, Yinfu)*’s celestial mechanics, a secret employed by those of lofty insight and adaptable understanding.

Lu Xisheng said: *"The profound dwelling of the Sage is nothing more exquisite than the interplay of *权实 (quánshí, expedient and truth)*. Truth is rooted in conformity with the natural order, while expedient arises from reversing established principles. Expedient aids truth, and truth enables expedient—though they differ in application, their essence converges in unity."*

After Laozi first guided people with truth, establishing the teaching of *知常 (zhīcháng, knowing the constant)*, he further employed expedient to address practical matters, clarifying the necessity of reversal. This is what the *易 (Yì, I Ching)* speaks of: *"Bending to complete all things without loss, encompassing heaven and earth without overreaching."*

The *Honglie Jie* says: *"Qi Huan of Qi loved delicacies, and Yi Ya cooked his own son as bait to please him. The Lord of Yu loved treasures, and Duke Xian of Jin used a jade and a horse to lure him. The Hu King loved music, and Duke Mu of Qin used female dancers to entice him. These were all instances where people were ensnared by their own desires."*

Yan Junping said: *"The Dao/Way traverses the moral realm, the divine illuminates it, heaven and earth transform through it, and yin and yang regulate it. Truth reverses into emptiness, clarity into obscurity, abundance into decline, tension into relaxation, possession into loss, life into death—these are the inherent natures of things and the natural principles of the Dao/Way.

Thus, the advantages of reversal and the benefits of yielding and extending: the Dao/Way governs heaven, heaven governs the sovereign, the sovereign governs his ministers, and the ministers govern the people. All living beings rely on this to sustain themselves.

When tigers and leopards prepare to pounce, they first conceal their claws. When wolves and jackals are about to devour, their teeth are not seen. The Sage abandons his personal intentions to conform with the Dao/Way, the wise adapt to the world to serve the people. Loyal words, though harsh, preserve the truth; treacherous ministers, when rising, must be appeased by their lord. Knowing this and applying it, all within the six directions shall prosper.

Ignoring this and applying it, even within the household, kin become bandits.

Thus, sons and brothers may become wolves and jackals when given advantage, while enemies and rivals may act as fathers and elders when denied. Yet within advantage lies disadvantage, and within disadvantage lies advantage—one may vanish while the other persists. Hence, even those who are not loyal cannot be trusted if they are close, and even those who are not virtuous cannot be loved if they are near. This is the lament of the worthy and the vigilant concern of the Sage and the wise.

Zhuangzi praised Xu Wugui’s audience with Duke Wu of Wei, where he first discussed horses and dogs before tiring the lord’s spirit and form, ultimately binding him with political counsel—this was the expedient of the wise, wielding extension and exhaustion.

If one applies this principle and extends it, then the meaning of *微明 (subtle clarity)* becomes evident.

Softness overcomes hardness, weakness overcomes strength—this is the logic of the Dao/Way. Examining this reveals the application of *权变 (quánbiàn, expedient adaptation)*.

Fish cannot be pulled from the depths without harm, nor can the state’s instruments of utility be displayed to the people.

The state’s *权谋 (quánmóu, expedient strategies)* are great instruments for benefiting the world. If fish are wrenched from their natural pools and streams, even ants may torment them. If the state’s expedient strategies are leaked to the lower ranks, petty officials will exploit them—what of the cunning tyrants?

The *Yinfu Jing* says: *"Heaven has five hidden calamities; those who perceive them thrive. How could they be recklessly revealed?"*

Another interpretation: *"Fish pulled from the depths cannot be retrieved, and expedient lost to ministers cannot be reclaimed."*

Han Fei said: *"The sovereign’s power is like the depths—his ministers are fish within it. The ancients spoke in parables because of this.

Rewards and punishments are instruments of utility. The sovereign wields them to control his ministers, while the ministers seize them to dominate the sovereign. Hence, if the sovereign first reveals what he will reward, the ministers will scheme to earn his favor, claiming it as his virtue. If he first reveals what he will punish, the ministers will band together to shield themselves, calling it the strength of his rule. The state’s instruments of utility—should they not be handled with care?"*

The *Honglie Jie* says: *"In ancient times, Sici Zhihan served the state of Song. He told the Song ruler: 'The stability or peril of the state, the order or chaos of the people, rests on your administration of rewards and punishments.

When you grant titles and favors, the people rejoice. When you mete out executions and punishments, the people resent them. Let me, your subject, bear the resentment, while you enjoy the favor.'

The Song ruler said: 'Good.' When I receive the praise, you shall bear the blame. I know well that I shall not be laughed at by the other lords."*」

The people of the state all knew that the power of executions rested solely with Sici Zhihan. The great ministers favored him, while the common folk feared him. Yet even after not reaching a full year in office, Zhihan resigned, leaving the Song ruler to monopolize governance—this was like a fish escaping from the depths, while the sharp tools of power were handed to the lower ranks.

The art of She Zhang in granting and withholding lies in subtlety, which is called *Dao of the Profound and Mysterious*.

When the Dao is overt, the fish escapes from the depths;
When it is subtle, it aligns with the *Dao of the Constant and Natural*.
Thus, it follows with the principle of *Dao of the Constant and Natural, Without Action*.

The *Dao of the Constant and Natural, Without Action* yet accomplishes all things—
If a lord or king could uphold it,
All things would naturally transform.

For the Dao is ever constant,
Deep and still, unmoving—
Thus it is called *Without Action*.

Yet when it responds to things, all things move of their own accord—
Thus it is called *Without Not-Action*.

If a lord or king could always hold emptiness as his heart
And *Without Action* as his form,
Unyielding in his grasp yet never rigid,
Then all things would naturally conform to his transformation.

The *Classic* says:
*"I act without action, and the people transform themselves.
If they transform yet wish to act,
I shall govern them with the nameless and pure."*

For the virtuous few in the world are outnumbered by the unvirtuous many—
Thus, the sage’s *virtue-transformations* are always in aid of the good,
And even if some do not follow his transformation but instead stir and act contrary to the Dao,
The sage still remains lenient,
And shall govern them with the nameless Dao of purity,
To bring them to stillness and *Without Action*—
Then the world will naturally settle.

The Dao itself leaves no trace,
Yet it borrows the pure and simple to speak of it;
Those who cling to its traces,
Who grasp at the pure and simple as something fixed,
Become the bane of later ages.
The sage fears that this bane cannot be cured,
So he shall also not desire to preserve this nameless purity—
Then the world will gradually settle of its own accord,
Entering the *Dao of the Constant and Natural, Without Action* yet *Without Not-Action*.

Lu Xi Sheng says:
The first chapter takes *Constant Dao* as its essence,
*Constant Dao* as its application,
And culminates in the profound depths.
This chapter takes *Without Action* as its essence,
*Without Not-Action* as its application,
And unifies them with *comprehensive forgetting*—
Its beginning and end are interlinked,
And it exhausts the essence and application.

The *Dao of the Constant and Natural, Without Action*—
This is called *Constant Dao*.
*Constant Dao* responds to changes,
Yet accomplishes all things without fail.

**The Daoist Classic of the *Zangshi Mowei* Chapter Five concludes.**
**The Daoist Classic of the *Zangshi Zuanwei* Chapter Six concludes.**

**The Daoist Classic of the *Zangshi Mowei* (Continued) – Chapter Seven**

**Bìxū Zǐ Chén Jǐngyuán’s Annotation:**

The lower section elucidates *virtue/power (dé)*, taking *not-virtue (bùdé)* as its origin.
*Not-virtue* is the forgetting of *virtue/power* in order to respond to things.

The *highest virtue/power* is *not-virtue*—thus it possesses *virtue/power*.
*Virtue/power* is *obtaining (dé)*.
Within, it is obtained in the heart;
Without, it is obtained in things—
Always obtained yet never lost,
Beneficial yet never harmful.
When things thrive through it, this is called *virtue/power*.

Its origin lies in the *Dao’s* accumulation—
Thus it has *virtue/power*.
Having *virtue/power* yet no name—
This is the *Dao’s* profundity.
Having *virtue/power* yet a name—
This is the *Dao’s* shallowness.
The *Dao* has depth and shallowness—
Thus *virtue/power* has high and low.

The so-called *highest virtue/power*—
This is *ultimate virtue/power*.
*Ultimate virtue/power* is profound,
Is distant,
And harmonizes with things.
People do not see its traces—
Thus it is called *not-virtue*,
For it contains light yet conceals brilliance,
Its actions are fragmented yet complete,
Causing the people to use it daily without knowing,
And its *virtue/power* is whole—
Thus it is said to possess *virtue/power*.

The *lower virtue/power* does not lose *virtue/power*—
Thus it is *not-virtue*.
The *lower virtue/power*—
Its traces and actions gradually become manifest,
Scattered like a single flower’s petals,
Following things passively,
While things preserve their own ease.
The world praises it,
Attributing beauty to itself—
Yet it is distant from *ultimate virtue/power*,
And loses *ultimate virtue/power*—
Thus it is said to be *not-virtue*.

The *highest virtue/power* acts *without action*,
Yet does nothing—
The one with *highest virtue/power*,
Naturally receives the utmost subtlety,
Obtains the essence of *original qi*,
His spirit permeates heaven and earth,
His clarity rivals the sun and moon,
With no thought, no deliberation—
His heart is naturally *without action*,
Forgetting merit, forgetting traces,
Doing nothing yet obtaining all—
This is called *without action* yet *without not-action*.

The ancient text reads: *The highest virtue/power acts without action yet without not-action.*
It speaks of those with *highest virtue/power*—
Their mind is still, their nature serene,
Even their desires do not defy nature,
Their actions do not oppose all things—
Thus they act *without action* yet *without not-action*.

The *lower virtue/power* acts,
Yet does nothing—
The *lower virtue/power* descends from the *highest virtue/power*,
Naturally receiving the plain and ordinary,
Obtaining the pure harmony of *original qi*,
His spirit aligns with yin and yang,
His clarity mirrors the sun and moon,
The people all revere and joyfully extol him—
Thus it is said to do nothing.

The ancient text reads: *The lower virtue/power acts yet does nothing.*
It speaks of those with *lower virtue/power*—
Their minds imitate emptiness,
They cling to the void,
Responding to things with caution,
Daring not to act hastily—
Thus it is said to do nothing.

*Highest benevolence (shàngrén)* acts,
Yet does nothing—
The benevolent universally bestow and love all—
All things are possible.
Possible yet without partiality—
Thus it is called *highest benevolence*.
With a mind to benefit all beings—
Thus it is said to act.
Merit accomplished yet not claimed,
Affairs completed yet not controlled—
Thus it is said to do nothing.

*Highest righteousness (shàngyì)* acts,
Yet does something—
Righteousness is what is fitting.
What is fitting must entail some loss,
Yet in all matters, nothing is unfitting—
Thus it is called *highest righteousness*.
There is fitting and benefit—
Thus it acts.
What it does involves cutting and severing—
Thus it is said to do something.

*Highest ritual (shànglǐ)* acts,
Yet none respond—
Then it rolls up its sleeves and drags them along.
*Rolling up its sleeves*—
Exposing the arms by pushing aside sleeves.
*Drags*—draws.
Ritual is *treading*—
Treading the *Dao* and walking,
Distinguishing high and low,
Fixing noble and base,
With humility, respect, harmony, and gentleness—
This is the root of ritual—
Thus it is called *highest*.
Jade and silk exchanged,
威仪相答 (威严的仪节相互应答),
Bowing, kneeling, clenching fists,
Advancing, retreating, glancing and bowing—
This is the ornament of ritual—
Thus it is called *lower*.

Zhuangzi said:
*The greatest ritual aligns with heaven and earth in rhythm.*
Again:
*Those who use ritual as wings—*
This is how they move through the world.

The ancient sages established ritual
To settle the mind and *qi*,
To refine appearance—
Thus it is said to act.
Yet the mediocre and lowly
Abandon the root and exalt the branches,
Twisting explanations and multiplying complexities,
Unable to respond—
Thus it is said none respond.
This is the slight failing of ritual.
But there are those who flush with anger,
Brandishing arms,
Dragging fingers to point out right and wrong,
Competing—
This is the extreme failing of ritual.

The Master Lao enumerates the undifferentiated and scattered,
Arriving at this in the lower text.

Yán Jūnping said:
*Empty and without action, guiding all things—*
This is called the *Daoist*.
*Clear and tranquil, responding without fail—*
This is called the *virtuous*.
*Compassionately loving all things, endlessly bestowing—*
This is called the *benevolent*.
*Rectifying names, aligning reality, acting appropriately—*
This is called the *righteous*.
*Humble, retiring, courteous, guarding harmony—*
This is called the *ritualist*.

These five are all traces of the *Dao’s* exposition,
Not the *ultimate ultimate*.

The *ultimate ultimate* transcends all distinctions—
How could there be these five?

Thus, losing the *Dao* leads to *virtue/power*,
Losing *virtue/power* leads to *benevolence*,
Losing *benevolence* leads to *righteousness*,
Losing *righteousness* leads to *ritual*.
*Dao, virtue/power, benevolence, righteousness, ritual*—
These five are the *Dao’s* essence,
Unattainable through inquiry—
Thus they are merged into one.
Once divided, the five distinctions appear,
And their traces become manifest—
Thus they are applied according to the age.

The *Dao* is the essence of *virtue/power*,
*Virtue/power* is the function of the *Dao*.
Separating essence for function—
Thus losing the *Dao* leads to *virtue/power*.
*Virtue/power* is *obtaining (dé)*.
When things obtain life, this is called *virtue/power*—
Where there is life, there must be love—
Thus losing *virtue/power* leads to *benevolence*.
*Benevolence* is *kinship (qīn)*.
Loving things appropriately—
Thus losing *benevolence* leads to *righteousness*.
*Righteousness* is *what is fitting (yí)*.
What is fitting demands humility and respect—
Thus losing *righteousness* leads to *ritual*.
*Ritual* is *treading (lǚ)*—
Treading the *Dao* to form its patterns—
Simple and direct, it is easy to follow;
Complex and twisted, it leads to chaos.
*Losing*—this is *extinction*.
When the branches flourish and the root perishes—
This is the natural order.

Yán Jūnping said:
*The emperor’s foundation—*
The *Dao* is its root,
*Virtue/power* is its beginning.
Losing the *Dao*, *virtue/power* follows;
Losing *virtue/power*, *benevolence* follows;
Losing *benevolence*, *righteousness* follows;
Losing *righteousness*, *ritual* follows;
Losing *ritual*, chaos follows.
These five are the *Dao’s* unified body,
Yet the world’s rulers vary in length.

The purpose is not what one truly desires,
The pursuit is not what one truly obtains—
They neglect the natural Way and instead cling to petty trifles.

Now, *ritual* as a practice:
Within and without, it contradicts itself,
Flourishing in ornament while lacking substance,
Its branches thrive while its roots wither.
*Ritual* grows thin compared to loyalty,
Authority grows light compared to *virtue/power*,
Faith does not reach *righteousness*,
*Virtue/power* does not suffice for *benevolence*.
This is the end of governance,
The beginning of chaos.
Deceit and pretense arise from it,
Anger and contention take root—
This is what is called *superficial* teaching.

For *ritual* is the thinning of loyalty and faith,
And the origin of chaos.
If one abandons *virtue/power*, *moral cultivation*, *benevolence*, and *righteousness*,
And relies solely on *ritual* and teaching—
Is this not the thinning of loyalty and faith,
And the origin of chaos?

But if one reveres *virtue/power* and the seven *righteous acts*,
And harmonizes them with *ritual* and teaching—
Then *ritual* is elevated above them,
And where would there be disorder?

Zhuangzi said:
The Sage acts through unsaid teaching.
The *Dao* cannot be attained,
*Virtue/power* cannot be reached,
But *benevolence* can be cultivated,
And *righteousness* can be diminished,
While *ritual* is where pretense thrives—
This aligns with the origin of chaos.

*Foresight* is the ornament of the *Dao*,
And the beginning of folly.
To cultivate and exalt *ritual* and teaching is wisdom—
Wisdom is foresight,
Thus it is called *foresight*.
Once foresight precedes things,
How can one remain detached?
Thus governing with wisdom is a state’s ruin—
Is this not the ornament of the *Dao*
And the beginning of folly?

But if one conceals foresight and wisdom,
Thinking and deliberating nothing,
Then one returns to simplicity and purity.

Excessive *ritual* leads to chaos,
Excessive wisdom leads to deceit—
This is an inevitable truth.

Thus, the true person dwells in substance, not ornament,
In reality, not superficiality—
Hence, they discard the latter and embrace the former.

The true person is one who follows the *Dao*.
Wenzi said:
The true person is serene without thought,
Unburdened without worry,
Walks where there are no paths,
Moves without fatigue,
Exits without gates,
Enters without walls,
Unites their vital essence,
Lulls their perceptions,
Vacant and unforced—
Yet nothing remains undone.

Thus, their dwelling is naturally pure and substantial,
In harmony with the highest *virtue/power*.

Residing in loyalty and faith,
Diligently pursuing sincerity—
How could they indulge in ornament, pretense, or deceit?

Therefore, they discard the shallow ends of *ritual* and *righteousness*,
And take the great foundation of *virtue/power* and *moral cultivation*.

The ancient text originally combined these four sentences with the character *chǔ* (处).

There is a wondrous *Dao*, and from it all things are born.
The great *virtue/power* of Heaven and Earth is called *generativity*.
Things receive *generativity* and call it *virtue/power*—
Thus, it is called *uppermost* *virtue/power*.
In *virtue/power*, there is no loss—
Thus, it is called *lowermost* *virtue/power*.
Though upper and lower differ,
Each attains its unity—
Hence, it follows with the *One* of antiquity.

The *One* of antiquity:
Heaven, obtaining *One*, is clear;
Earth, obtaining *One*, is tranquil;
The *Spirit*, obtaining *One*, is numinous;
The *Valley*, obtaining *One*, is full;
All things, obtaining *One*, are born;
The sovereign, obtaining *One*, corrects the world.
This is how they attain *One*.

*Antiquity* refers to the distant past.
*The One* is the primal *qi*.
Primal *qi* is the child of the Great *Dao*,
The mother of the numinous,
The ancestor of Great Harmony,
The progenitor of Heaven and Earth.
Condensed, it forms living beings;
Dispersed, it radiates as light.
In the yin, it shares the yin’s *virtue/power*;
In the yang, it rides the yang’s waves.
It dwells in the Celestial Capital yet remains untainted,
It resides in the humble hut yet remains unsoiled.
Its position is unchanging above and below,
Its essence is undivided through ages—
Thus, it is called *One*.

Concealed within the heart, it is the *Numinous Treasury*;
Ascending to the mind, it is the *Numinous Platform*;
Motionless and still, it is the *True Sovereign*;
Guiding the form, it is the *True Ruler*.
When coiled, it retreats into the pores;
When unfurled, it fills the vast expanse.

The *Western Ascension Scripture* says:
Obtain *One*, and all matters are complete.
If a person empties their heart,
*The One* will naturally return.

Zhuangzi said:
*Qi* is the void that awaits things.
Only the *Dao* gathers the void.
Again, it says:
*The empty chamber births white auspiciousness*—
Stop, stop.

Heaven is the pure yang *qi*.
And Heaven, obtaining it, thus moves vigorously and dynamically,
Its dome vast and encompassing,
Its symbols clear and bright—
All things derive their origin from it.

Earth is the pure yin substance.
And Earth, obtaining it, thus remains faithful, yielding, and weak,
Its foundation firm and thickly bearing,
Its stability unshaken—
All things derive their sustenance from it.

The *Spirit* is the subtle expression of all things.
And the *Spirit*, obtaining it, thus transforms without limit,
Its reversals endless,
Its rectitude resonant and responsive—
Its correspondence to things is inexhaustible.

The *Valley* is the deepest of the Han.
And the *Valley*, obtaining it, thus moves *qi* and channels water,
Its fullness unexhausted.

The *Ten Thousand* refers to the greatness of numbers.
*Things* are the visible forms.
And things, obtaining it, thus generate and complete without ceasing—
Their extinction is unknown.

The *Marquis* and *King* are the five ranks of nobility.
The *King* is the general term for ruler.
Obtaining it, they thus eternally possess the great treasure,
Their will unchallenged—
And they correct the world.

These five attain clarity, tranquility, numinosity, and fullness,
And all things thrive because the sovereign’s *Dao* is correct.

*Correctness* means attaining the *One* of the primal void.
If the *One* is lost, all things perish—
As stated below.

The *Kaiyuan* edition reads: *"Their governance lacks the One,"*
Two characters missing in meaning.
This follows the ancient text as correct.

Yán Jūnping’s version omits:
*"All things obtain it and thus are born,"*
And the following fourteen characters:
*"All things, without it, would fear extinction."*
For humans are the most refined of all things,
And the sovereign is the ruler of humans—
Thus, it is summarized.

With it, the text is complete;
Without it, the meaning is not lacking—
This also aligns with principle.

Heaven, without clarity, would fear cracking;
Earth, without tranquility, would fear upheaval;
The *Spirit*, without numinosity, would fear cessation;
The *Valley*, without fullness, would fear exhaustion;
All things, without *generativity*, would fear annihilation;
The sovereign, without exalted nobility, would fear ruin.

*"Without it"* refers to the language of warning through consequences.

Heaven is the manifestation of the *virtue/power* of Yang, the emblem of the sovereign. When it is said that Heaven lacks clarity, if it relies on itself as if on something secure, it loses the *Way* of supreme luminosity, leading to discord between Yin and Yang, the collapse of order and principles, and the stagnation of the unified *qi*—thus risking the omen of fissures. Moreover, when Yang *qi* is excessive, calamities and decline must follow; when the harmonious balance disperses, fissures must occur. Thus, the *Yin-Yang Book* states: *"Heaven’s cracking is the result of insufficient Yang *qi* and the decline of the sovereign’s virtue."*

From here onward, all are words of admonition.

Earth is the manifestation of the *virtue/power* of Yin, the emblem of the minister. When it is said that Earth lacks tranquility, if it relies on itself as if on something secure, it loses the *Way* of serenity, leading to the distortion of firmness and softness, the collapse of mountains and rivers, and the failure to uphold the unified *qi*—thus risking the consequences of eruption and overflow. Moreover, when Yin *qi* reaches its extreme, there must be flooding disasters; when the harmonious balance dissipates, eruptions must occur. Thus, the *Yin-Yang Book* states: *"Earthquakes arise from excess Yin *qi* and the neglect of the minister’s duty."*

The *Spirit* is the essence of transformation. All things with refined vitality possess their *Spirit*—an unfathomable interplay of Yin and Yang. When it is said that the *Spirit* lacks numinosity, if it relies on itself as if on something secure, it loses the *Way* of supreme transformation, leading to the birth of calamities and omens, the rise of portents, and the abandonment of the unified *qi*—thus risking the time of cessation. When the *Spirit* strays from the *Way*, it harms the people; when the people are harmed, they rebel in destruction. Destruction without *Spirit*—what else but cessation?

The *Valley* is the void that receives all things. When it is said that the *Valley* lacks fullness, if it relies on itself as if on something secure, it loses the *Way* of flowing nourishment, leading to the fear of collapse and desolation, the obstruction of the unified *qi*—thus risking the isolation of exhaustion. Exhaustion affects the state, hence the Luo River’s drying led to the fall of Xia, and the Yellow River’s depletion caused the ruin of Shang.

All things have form and substance, destined to transform. When it is said that all things lack *generativity*, if they rely on themselves as if on something secure, they lose the *Way* of yielding compliance, leading to the inversion of nature’s order, the dispersal of the unified *qi*—thus risking the period of annihilation.

The sovereign unites the Three Realms as sovereign and governs all things. He must cultivate humility to nurture the talents of the people. When it is said that the sovereign lacks exalted nobility, if he relies on himself as if on something secure, he loses the *Way* of pure transformation, causing officials to neglect their duties, the people to abandon loyalty, and the risk of ruin and overthrow. All the matters mentioned above—though extending to Heaven and Earth—ultimately serve as warnings to the sovereign.

If the sovereign heeds these warnings, how much more should the common people?

Thus, nobility finds its foundation in humility, and height finds its base in lowliness.

Nobility—how can it arise from itself? It must be upheld by the humble, and only then does it attain nobility. This is the foundation of humility.

Height—how can it arise from itself? It must be borne by those beneath, and only then does it attain elevation. This is the base of lowliness.

Therefore, the sovereign addresses himself as *"lonely, widowed, and unworthy"*—is this not the embodiment of humility as foundation? Is it not so? *"Lonely, widowed, and unworthy"* are terms of common reproach, yet the sovereign adopts them as titles, cultivating humility to avoid arrogance. Is this not the foundation of humility? And if it is not, then truly it is the foundation of humility.

Thus, when praised repeatedly, he does not take pride. A sage ruler forgets his name and self; though the world praises him often, he does not grow more encouraged. Though the world criticizes him often, he does not grow discouraged. How would he know whether he is truly noble or not?

The *Guoyu* text reads: *"When praised repeatedly, there is no praise."* When considering the components of a chariot, there are wheels, axles, frames, and shafts—yet none is called a "chariot" alone. Only when combined do they form a chariot. When considering the components of a state, there are scholars, farmers, artisans, and merchants—yet none is called a "state" alone. Only when combined do they form a state. How could a chariot be formed without its parts? How could a state survive without its people? This is a grave warning to the sovereign, urging him to love his people.

He does not wish to shine like jade, nor to be dull like stone.
*Jianjian*—the appearance of jade.
*Luoluo*—the appearance of stone.
A sage ruler does not desire ostentation like jade, nor does he wish to be obscure like stone. Instead, he cultivates a hidden light, guarding the *Way* of the unified *qi*, becoming the model for all under Heaven.

The ancient text reads: *"Like jade, like stone."* The transmitted versions by Yan Yuan and Xu Xian adopt this.

Having attained nobility and height, yet grounding himself in humility, addressing himself as lonely and widowed, repeatedly practicing modesty—this is why it is followed by the *Way* of return.

*Return* is the movement of the *Way*.
*Return* means restoration, transformation.
The origin of all things lies in emptiness and stillness.
Before things arise, they first return to the origin of emptiness and stillness. When they transform, they emerge from emptiness and stillness into motion—thus, first returning, then moving.

Therefore, the *Yijing* states in the *Fu* (Return) hexagram:
*"The firm returns and moves, yielding and proceeding—
Thus, entering and exiting without haste—
This is what is meant."*

The weak are the application of the *Way*.
Since returning to emptiness and stillness is the movement of the *Way*, then softness, weakness, and receptivity are truly the application of the *Way*.

All things under Heaven are born from existence, and existence is born from nonexistence.
Existence is the One.
The One is the Original *Qi*.
It is said that all things under Heaven are born from the Original *Qi*. The Original *Qi* possesses light without form—though light and shadow emerge from the void.

The void is the substance of the *Way*.
*The Liezi* states:
*"Formed things arise from the formless—
Then how did Heaven and Earth come to be?"*
It also says:
*"Movement without form does not produce form,
Yet produces shadow.
Sound without substance does not produce sound,
Yet produces echo.
The void in motion does not produce the void,
Yet produces existence."*

Thus, all things are born from existence, and existence is born from nonexistence—yet all things transform uniquely.

Yan Junping says:
*"Heaven and Earth are born from Great Harmony,
And Great Harmony is born from the void—
This is the origin of returning to emptiness and stillness."*

The moment things are about to move, they first return to the origin of emptiness and stillness.
Only the superior sage understands how to move from emptiness and stillness—
thus, the next passage speaks of the superior sage hearing the *Dao*.

The superior sage hears the *Dao* and diligently practices it.
The mediocre sage hears the *Dao* yet is half-convinced, half-doubtful—
thus, it is as if it exists and as if it does not.
The inferior sage hears the *Dao* and laughs loudly in disbelief.
If it is not laughed at, then it is not worthy to be called the *Dao*.

The superior sage, by nature, is pure and tranquil,
serene and undisturbed, empty and without action,
unadulterated and unchanging, still as one—
when he hears the *Dao*, people observe his conduct,
truly believing he diligently practices it,
yet in reality, he does nothing at all.
This is what is called effortless mastery.

The mediocre sage, by nature, is moderate—
things the world does not use.
He retreats to marshes and valleys, seeking quiet and spaciousness,
exhales the old and inhales the new,
endures hardship like the bear and soars like the phoenix,
merely nurturing his body and preserving his spirit.
But when he is put to use by the world,
he speaks of great achievements,
establishes great reputation,
honors the relationships of lord and subject,
rectifies the order of high and low,
and governs affairs—
this is what is called as if it exists and as if it does not.

The inferior sage, by nature, is coarse and defiled—
his eyes crave to see colors,
his ears crave to hear sounds,
his mouth craves to savor flavors,
his intentions crave to be fulfilled.
When he hears of serene tranquility and non-action,
he laughs loudly and rejects it.
If it is not rejected and laughed at by the inferior sage,
then it is not worthy to be the true *Dao*.

Yan Junping says:
*"The quail and partridge fly high,
yet their hearts are set on the humble weeds.
The wild goose and crane take great strides,
crossing mountains and rivers,
yet their hearts are set on the marshes and pools.
The phoenix and cranes soar above the lofty peaks,
gliding through the boundless purity,
their brilliance obscuring the sun and moon,
their gaze reaching far and wide,
dwelling in the eight extremes,
thus finding their perfect place.
All three take pleasure in their own ways—
thus, the infinite source and the deep spring
are where the divine dragon returns,
while the small fish flee."*

High mountains and great mounds,
dense forests and vast ravines,
are where the wild goose and tiger delight—
yet where the fowl and dogs recoil.

Thus, to diminish sharpness and abandon clever scheming,
to return to truth and embrace simplicity,
to wander in the Great Purity,
to hold the world in contempt,
to remain untainted and unperturbed,
to let joy and anger not dwell in the heart,
to let gain and loss not linger in the mind,
to treat noble and base alike,
to embrace *virtue* and contain harmony—
these are the ideals of the Great Sage,
the true pursuits of the superior sage,
the bewilderment of the mediocre sage,
and the great laughter of the inferior sage.

Lu Xisheng says:
*"The *Dao* above form is called the *Dao*—
it transcends all forms.
The *Dao* below form is called *instrumentality*—
it conforms to all forms within.
The superior sage knows both subtlety and manifestation,
transcending all forms—
thus, when he hears the *Dao* and believes it,
he diligently practices it.
The mediocre sage is caught between subtlety and manifestation,
dwelling in the space between *Dao* and *instrumentality*—
when he hears the *Dao*, he is half-doubtful, half-convinced—
thus, it is as if it exists and as if it does not.
The inferior sage knows only manifestation and not subtlety,
stopping at mere forms—
thus, when he hears the *Dao*, he laughs loudly,
not only laughing but also rejecting it."*

The *Dao* is subtle and profound, mysterious and all-pervading,
deep beyond recognition—
if it is not rejected and laughed at by the inferior sage,
then it is not worthy to be the true essence of the *Dao*.

The following words explain:
*"Establishing"* means *"to set up"*—
the passage that follows explains the words of the three sages,
their differing perceptions, and the state of being laughed at.

*"Clarifying the *Dao* as if in darkness"*—
*"Clarifying"* means *"to meet and understand face-to-face"*—
those who awaken to the *Dao* hold their light within,
their actions and demeanor like an unlit lamp.
Thus, the mediocre sage doubts its existence or non-existence,
and the inferior sage laughs loudly at it.

*"Advancing the *Dao* seems like retreating"*—
The superior sage reaches enlightenment without effort,
much less does he advance it.
Even with sage-like achievements,
he does not take them for himself,
and in mingling with the dust and engaging with the world,
it seems as if he is retreating and failing.

*"Equalizing the *Dao* as if it were a kind"*—
*"Equalizing"* means *"level and even"*—
*"kind"* refers to uneven silk, which is discarded by weavers.
The superior sage’s heart is broad and level,
his *virtue* undivided—
yet if it is uneven like silk with flaws,
it will not be used by the world.
River Master’s version reads *"kind"*—
those who follow the Great *Dao* are broad and level,
adapting to all without distinction.

*"Highest *virtue* resembles a valley"*—
Those of highest *virtue* act without action,
their minds aligned with the void,
neither lofty nor low, beyond measure—
like a deep valley, containing all.

*"Great purity resembles defilement"*—
Great purity is like the whiteness of snow and frost,
unrestricted in its reach,
even in filthy, neglected places,
it spreads without discrimination.
Those who follow the *Dao* are surely no different—
thus, in a defiled world,
they remain purely white and untainted.

*"Vast *virtue* seems insufficient"*—
Sun Deng says:
*"If one’s *virtue* is deep and vast,
then it appears distant and neglects small details,
its wisdom seemingly lacking.
Thus, a good merchant hides his wares as if empty,
and a gentleman of great *virtue* looks foolish in appearance."*

Lu Xisheng says:
*"To clothe all under Heaven yet not claim its *virtue*—
this is vast *virtue* seeming insufficient."*

*"Establishing *virtue* resembles stealing"*—
This refers to hidden, secret *virtue*.
Those who establish hidden *virtue*
do not display their deeds,
fearing others’ knowledge—
thus, it seems like stealing.

Wu Jun’s *Treatise on the Primordial Thread* says:
*"One’s deeds should be hidden,
one’s mistakes should be obvious—
hidden deeds can be preserved,
obvious mistakes are easily corrected.*
This is what is meant.

*"True essence resembles corruption"*—
*"Corruption"* means *"change"* and *"dullness"*—
those of pure essence act without ostentation,
like uneven silk, their brilliance unexpressed.
Zhuan Yi’s *Explanation of Meaning* states:
*"Ancient texts read ‘*shū*’ (输).*
*The *Guangya* says: ‘*shū*’ means ‘*foolish*.’*
Some read ‘*yú*’ (榆).
Dong Yu reads ‘*yáo*’ (摇).
Now, following Wang Bi and Zhuan Yi’s reading, we take ‘*yú*’ (渝).*

*"The square has no corners"*—
Kaiyuan Imperial Commentary says:
*"Square"* means *"rectitude"*—
*"corners"* means *"angles"*—
those who polish their reputation and name
to establish a sharp, upright stance,
do so to create a corner of integrity.

This is what is meant by the "bounded teachings" of the common scholar—not the "great square" of the sage.

To polish without brilliance,
to dwell in filth without staining—
this is the great square.
To blend light with dust,
to walk without sharp distinction—
this is without corners.

Great vessels take long to mature;
their use is complete when their purpose is fulfilled.
River Above Master said:
The great *gān* (柑) of humanity,
like the Nine Tripods and coral waves,
is not formed in a single day.
Accumulated habits yield enduring beauty—
perfection lies in duration.

Great sound is rare in voice;
River Above Master said:
Great sound is like thunder waiting for its moment to move—
it is the *qi* of love, sparse in words.
Thunder still strikes the ears—
how much more so the profound words?

Lu Xi’s *Hixiang* said:
Through silent teaching,
stirring all things without resorting to frivolous speech—
this is great sound, rare in voice.

Great form is shapeless;
River Above Master said:
Those who embody the great *xiàng* (象) are simple and unadorned.
He who possesses great *xiàng* responds without form—
saying that great *xiàng* responds to all *xiàng* is to say it cannot be fixed by form,
hence it is shapeless.

The Dao is hidden, nameless.
The Dao originally had no name,
yet it was forcibly named *Dao*.
Now the Dao is hidden again—
what name remains?
This is truly the erasure of traces, concealment of origins.

Heaven follows the Dao—
virtue is lent and fulfilled.
*Across* (贷) means to bestow.
To praise this wondrous Dao alone—
it can move gods and spirits, emperors and deities,
generate heaven and earth.
It lends its pure, harmonious, wondrous *qi* to all things,
fulfilling their nature and returning to spontaneity.

Jun Ping said:
The Dao’s transformation begins with nothing,
ends with no end,
exists in non-existence,
lends without expectation.
In motion, it forms all things;
in stillness, the world is at ease.

Lu Xi’s *Hixiang* said:
Only those who generously bestow upon all things,
without demanding repayment,
are why all things receive their fulfillment
yet know not its virtue.
Thus, the lesser scholars hear this Dao and scoff,
doubting its power.

The superior scholar diligently practices,
establishing virtue—
the Dao generates One.

The Dao generates One,
One generates Two,
Two generates Three,
Three generates all things.

The Dao is the emptiness of emptiness,
the nothingness of nothingness—
the natural thusness.

Mysterious, boundless, supreme void,
profound silence, deep unity—
ineffable and unspeakable.

Yet when it moves, it produces transformation—
this is called *húnlún* (浑沦).
*Húnlún* is One.
*Húnlún* One—
before *qi* disperses,
there must be spirit and clarity,
