latent within.

Spirit and clarity are Two.
With spirit and clarity comes division—
thus, the three *qi* of purity, turbidity, and harmony
emerge in sighs,
each returning to its place.
Hence, pure *qi* becomes Heaven,
turbid *qi* becomes Earth,
harmonious *qi* becomes Humanity.

With the Three Talents complete,
all things are nourished to life.

Jun Ping said:
The emptiness of emptiness generates the nothingness of nothingness;
the nothingness of nothingness generates the formless.
Thus, all formed things belong to their kind,
and each kind has its origin.
Heaven and Earth are the greatest of things,
with Humanity following.

When Heaven and Humanity are born,
form arises from *qi*,
*qi* from harmony,
harmony from spirit and clarity,
spirit and clarity from the Dao.
The Dao’s natural unfolding sustains all things,
so Heaven is Heaven not by itself,
Humanity is Humanity not by itself.

Gu Shenzzi said:
The Dao naturally transforms and generates spirit,
spirit moves and forms harmony,
harmony disperses and *qi* congeals,
*qi* congeals and form is made—
thus, the Dao generates One,
One generates Two,
Two generates Three,
Three generates all things.

River Above Master said:
The Dao’s first generation is One;
One generates Yin and Yang,
Yin and Yang generate the three harmonious *qi* of purity, turbidity, and harmony,
which divide into Heaven, Earth, and Humanity.
Heaven and Earth, with Humanity, generate all things.
Heaven bestows, Earth transforms,
Humanity nurtures them.

Kaiyuan Imperial Commentary said:
The Dao is the supreme spirit of ultimate emptiness.
One is the refined *qi* of pure harmony.
To generate is to move and produce.
Speaking of the Dao’s movement producing harmonious *qi* to generate things,
yet this still does not suffice for the principle of response—
further, Yang *qi* is generated,
accumulating Yang to complete One—
thus, One generates Two.
Pure Yang alone cannot generate,
so Yin *qi* is generated,
accumulating Yin to complete Two—
thus, Two generates Three.
Three generates all things—
when Yin and Yang interact harmoniously,
and the pure *qi* refines,
then myriad beings proliferate.
The three schools’ accounts differ slightly in detail;
here, all are preserved.

All things bear Yin on their backs and embrace Yang,
using harmonious *qi* as their mediator.
*Negative* (负) means to turn away;
*embrace* (抱) means to face.
Living things fear death and seek life,
plants turn from cold and toward warmth.
Their skin and texture envelop externally—
skin and texture are where Yin *qi* congeals,
hence it is said they bear Yin.
Their marrow fills internally—
marrow is where Yang *qi* gathers,
hence it is said they embrace Yang.
The充和 *chōnghé* *qi* circulates between,
thus forming the essence of life.

When harmonious *qi* is full and complete,
the essence of life is unharmed,
and vitality reigns.
When harmonious *qi* decays and scatters,
the essence of life separates,
and death ensues.

Thus, the sage empties his spiritual repository,
and pure whiteness converges.
The virtuous man stirs not his heart,
and the vast *hé* *qi* may be nurtured.
Pure whiteness and vastness are alternate names for harmonious *qi*.
Harmonious *qi* is gentle and weak,
capable of mediating Yin and Yang—
hence it is said harmonious *qi* mediates.

What humanity despises are the terms *gū* (孤), *guǎ* (寡), and *bùgǔ* (不谷),
yet kings and nobles adopt them as titles.
*Gū*, *guǎ*, and *bùgǔ* are terms of gentle weakness and humility—
what the vulgar find objectionable,
yet the sage and noble take as their own,
for this is how they attain royal nobility.

This follows the harmonious *qi* as mediator,
reducing the heart’s rigid strength
to seek benefit in virtue and the Way.

Thus, things sometimes benefit when diminished,
and are diminished when over-benefited.

There are things that can reduce emotional desires,
that do not boast of their own merits,
and that lower themselves to preserve their essence.
As when kings and nobles adopt the titles *gū* (孤), *guǎ* (寡), and *bùgǔ* (不谷),
their humility brings them greater honor and nobility.
The vulgar, however, seek only excess and fullness,
and those who greedily pursue wealth and noble status,
knowing no restraint, invite ruin and peril.
The *Shū* says: *"Fullness invites loss, humility invites benefit."*
When things are diminished by external means,
it is the Dao’s way that brings true benefit.
The Dao’s benefit comes when things are diminished by their own nature.

What humanity teaches is also what I teach.
The teachings of the world each assert their own *wǒ yì* (我义).
As with the hundred schools of thought,
each establishes its own principles—
those whose intent is good are aligned with the sage’s teachings,
while those whose intent is perverse become the arts of *zònghéng* (纵横).
The arts of *zònghéng* lead to entrapment by force and violence,
and one cannot escape their consequences.
Those who rely on force and violence cannot escape their fate—
I shall take this as my guiding principle.

Gentleness and weakness belong to Yang, the principle of life;
force and violence belong to Yin, the principle of death.
When rulers above are forceful and violent,
they lose the hearts of their people and meet downfall without delay.
When subjects below are forceful and violence,
they are overthrown by the multitude, and ruin is imminent.
To *"not escape their fate"* means to die before one’s natural end.

The Master Lao observed the world’s deviation from the Dao
and wrote these warnings for future generations,
knowing that those who practice humility and gentleness will thrive,
while those who indulge in greed and violence will perish.
Thus, he recorded these lessons to instruct posterity.
*"Father"* (父) means origin, foundation.
The principle of *chōnghé* (冲和) with its humility invites benefit,
while abandoning one’s own *wǒ yì* (我义) in favor of force and violence
leads to ruin—
hence, one must adhere to the utmost gentleness.
Therefore, I follow it with the observation:
*"The Dao’s utmost gentleness overcomes the world’s utmost firmness."*

The gentlest thing is water,
the firmest are metal and stone.
*"Driving"* (驰骋) describes its ceaseless flow,
like a swift steed racing without pause.
Water, though supremely gentle,
can penetrate metal and stone through its use,
and drown all things—
thus it is said to *"drive"* past the world’s firmest substances.
The Dao is without form or boundary,
yet it can encompass Heaven and Earth without end,
and invade the minutest hairs without omission.
How could anything with form enter its boundless void?
Zhuangzi said:
*"Metal and stone cannot sound without emptiness—*
*this refers to containing the Dao’s *qi* (气)."*
Jun Ping remarked:
*"Things with form, though sharp, do not enter the formless;
the spirit’s wisdom moves within the body,
emerging without boundary,
entering without aperture—
in the span of a breath, it traverses thousands of miles."*
Thus, I understand the benefits of *wúwéi* (无为).

*"I"* (吾) is the Master Lao’s self-reference.
The Way of emptiness and nothingness,
gentle and formless, yet penetrates all things.
Wang Bi said:
*"Nothingness cannot be exhausted,
gentleness cannot be broken—
from this, one knows the Dao of *wúwéi* (无为) benefits all."*

The teaching of silence and *wúwéi* (无为) is rarely attained.
The sage, observing the natural Dao of Heaven,
adjusts his body and simplifies his actions,
refines his bearing and conduct to enlighten others—
thus his teaching prevails without words.
If one resorts to forceful action,
it leads to stagnation and harm to things.
Yet when one returns to *wúwéi* (无为),
the benefits are manifold—
hence, the Way of quietude and simplicity
is truly the supreme guardian of the world.
Few attain it.

The *Classics* say:
*"Those who know me are rare—*
*thus I am precious."*

Wenzi said:
*"Gao Yao sighed and established the great laws,
and there was no cruelty in the world—
does governance have greater value than words?"*
Shi Kuang, though blind, became the Grand Steward,
and the state of Jin knew no disorder—
does administration have greater value than sight?

The sage’s commands are spoken without utterance,
his governance is seen without form—
this is how he becomes a teacher.

Gentleness and non-competition lie in *wúwéi* (无为).
The benefits of *wúwéi* (无为) are closest to one’s own body—
thus, I follow with: *"Which is dearer, name or body?"*

Which is dearer, name or body?
The body is substance, the name is guest—
to abandon substance for guest is called inversion.
The *Liézǐ* says:
*"Substance is poor, falsehood is rich;
to say substance has no name while name has no substance—
this is falsehood indeed.
Yet those who chase fame relentlessly—
can fame truly be abandoned?
Can fame truly be treated as a guest?"*

Now, having a name brings honor,
losing it brings disgrace.
Honor brings ease and pleasure,
disgrace brings sorrow and suffering.
Sorrow and suffering harm one’s nature,
ease and pleasure align with it—
this is where the matter lies.
How can fame be abandoned?
How can it be treated as a guest?
Only to fear those who cling to fame,
burdening their substance,
and neglect the peril of ruin—
is this not more than the difference between ease and suffering?

Bao Jiao extends this reasoning:
Those who cling to fame and burden substance.

Which is more: body or wealth?
Wealth refers to resources and possessions.
All who dwell in riches and delight in their means of support
are yet to find one who does not covet wealth.
The rich toil endlessly, day and night,
amassing treasure yet unable to enjoy it fully—
their bodies are but external forms.
The noble fret through the night and day,
pondering whether their actions are good or bad—
their bodies are but distant shadows.
Though their halls be filled with gold and jade,
if they lack virtue and benevolence,
they earn only the mockery of thieves and the scorn of those who hoard wealth—
and when their families fall and their bodies are shamed,
what greater loss is there?

To gain or to lose—
which brings greater harm?
False fame and fleeting profit
are as light as a feather,
while the loss of life, body, spirit, and nature
is as heavy as a great mountain.
A discerning person weighs which is the greater affliction.

Zhuangzi said:
*"To nourish one’s will is to forget the body;
to nourish the body is to forget profit;
to attain the Dao is to lose the heart—*
*it is gone."*

Thus, excessive love brings great depletion,
and excessive hoarding invites heavy loss.
*"Depletion"* (费) means harm.

Hoarding wealth brings ruin,
for greed and avarice lead only to self-destruction—
how could such folly be called light?

The contented know no disgrace,
the restrained face no peril.
Thus may one endure.

*"Peril"* (殆) means ruin.

The contented do not covet riches.
Without coveting riches,
they escape the calamities of shame and slaughter.

Zhuangzi recounts how Confucius spoke to Yan Hui:
*"Your home is poor, your station low—
why do you not seek office?"*
Yan Hui replied:
*"I have no wish to serve."*

He had fifty *mu* of fields outside the city walls,
enough for rice gruel,
and ten *mu* within,
sufficient for silk and hemp.
Playing the *qin* brought him joy,
and what he learned from the Master
filled him with delight.
Thus Yan Hui had no wish to serve.

Confucius grew solemn, his expression changing.
*"Ah, well said!
I have long heard that the contented
do not burden themselves with profit.
Those who find true self-sufficiency
lose nothing and fear no loss,
while those who cultivate virtue within
act without needing rank.
Now I see this in you—
this is my gain."*

The restrained do not covet fame or station.
Without coveting fame or station,
they know no fear of ruin or peril.

Zhuangzi said:
Yuan Xian lived in Lu,
in a hut of sun-dried bricks,
its thatched roof patched with wild grass,
its doors of woven reeds,
its windows framed with mulberry branches.
Within, two rooms were partitioned with coarse hemp,
leaking above, damp below—
yet he sat upright, plucking his strings.

Zigong arrived in a grand carriage,
drawn by fine horses, draped in white silk,
so wide the streets could not contain it.
He went to visit Yuan Xian,
who wore a fine cap and loose sandals,
leaning on a staff of black bamboo at the door.

Zigong exclaimed:
*"Ah, Master, what ails you?"*
Yuan Xian replied:
*"Poverty is lack of wealth.
Illness is knowing but not practicing.
I am poor, not sick."*

Zigong hung his head, ashamed.
Yuan Xian laughed:
*"To conform to the world’s ways,
to flatter and fawn in friendship,
to study for others’ gain,
to teach for self-aggrandizement—
these are the corruptions of *ren* and *yi*,
like gilding a horse’s trappings.
I cannot bear them.
But the truly contented and restrained
need fear no disgrace, no peril—
they endure."*

To cherish the self is to know contentment;
to distance oneself from fame is to avoid disgrace.
Contentment without disgrace—
the Dao is complete, like a broken vessel.
Thus, the greatest completion resembles a flaw.

A vessel with a flaw is never exhausted in use.
*"Flaw"* (缺) means broken;
*"exhausted"* (敝) means ruin, decay.
The truly accomplished—those of full virtue—
though their forms may seem incomplete,
are like vessels with flaws:
rarely discarded, their use never fails.
Thus they preserve their wholeness,
free from ruin or decay.

Again, like the great abyss:
draw from it, yet it is never emptied;
like a bright mirror:
reflect all things, yet never obscured.
Thus its use is never exhausted.

Great fullness seems empty,
yet its use knows no end.
*"Empty"* (冲) means humble, unassuming.
Those of vast virtue and achievement
are modest, not proud;
the wealthy in modest dwellings
are frugal, not extravagant.
Their abundance overflows—
how could they ever be wanting?

Great straightness seems bent,
for true straightness follows things as they are.
It bears grime without complaint,
its rectitude not its own—
thus it seems bent.

Great skill seems clumsy,
for true skill follows nature’s course,
crafting nothing unnatural—
thus it seems clumsy.

*The Liezi* tells of a man from Song
who carved jade into mulberry leaves,
taking three years to perfect them,
so seamless they blended with the real—
and thus he fed his skill to Song.
Liezi heard of this and said:
*"If Heaven and Earth took three years
to grow a single leaf,
then few things would bear leaves at all."*
Thus the Sage relies on the Dao’s transformation,
not on clever artifice.

The Dao’s transformation—
this is true skill:
following things as they are,
not boasting of one’s own power—
thus it seems clumsy.

Great eloquence seems tongue-tied,
for true eloquence knows no bounds.
It speaks as things demand,
without obstruction.
If one relies only on one’s own words,
some truths may elude it.
Thus it speaks no forbidden laws,
no unseemly words—
doing nothing forced,
it seems tongue-tied.

Vigor conquers cold,
stillness conquers heat.

*"Vigor"* (躁) means movement.
Spring and summer, the yang qi rises from the earth,
and all things thrive.
But extremes invert—
at the summer solstice, a single shade of yin appears within.
When movement reaches its peak, cold follows,
and all things wither.
Thus vigor is the root of decay,
excess the origin of decline—
a warning that perfection without flaw must fail,
that fullness without emptiness must topple,
that rigid vigor must perish.

Jun Ping said:
*"The movements of Heaven and Earth—
advance and retreat—
give rise to all things.
Change is inevitable, unblocked;
flexibility is inevitable, unobstructed.
Thus when yin reaches its height,
the earth splits, ice forms,
howling winds rage, frost descends like rocks,
turtles and tortoises retreat,
all things curl into balls.
At such times,
though you dwell in heated chambers,
stand by blazing fires,
wear thick fox and badger furs,
and wrap in fine wool,
you cannot ward off the cold.
But when the mind is still,
the body at ease,
when vitality drains and filth flows—
casting aside your clothes,
stepping outside—
you find warmth beyond measure."*

Stillness conquers heat,
for in autumn and winter, the yang qi rests beneath the cold depths.
What is rejected gives rise to what is affirmed—
at the winter solstice, a single shade of yang appears within.
Heat then brings forth the harmonious qi,
and all things are born from it.
Life arises from stillness—
thus stillness is both the root of life and its opposite, vigor.

Jun Ping said:
*"When yang reaches its height,
sand is boiled, stones melt,
birds fall from the sky,
water creatures writhe in agony,
all things wither and dry,
rivers and streams run low.
At such times,
though you plunge into deep, cold springs,
wear thin, loose robes,
soar on high towers,
consume icy stones—
you cannot endure the heat."*

When the mind is released and the will set free,
when the spirit rests in pristine stillness,
the body transcends the four seas’ bounds,
while the will roams where nothingness resides—
then the heart is balanced, the qi harmonious,
and cold is cast aside from the body’s core.

This speaks of vigor as the root of death,
stillness as the foundation of life—
to illustrate the sovereign’s path.

Vigor and strength repel foes,
adorn falsehood, and provoke the nature of things,
leading to the ruin of family and state—
this is called **vigorous strength**,
which brings only stillness and cold decay.
Cold decay brings decline and extinction.

Yet **stillness in harmony**—
with effortless governance, preserving all things’ truth—
brings enduring peace to the realm—
this is called **stillness in harmony**,
which brings warmth and human accord,
and human accord brings prosperity.

Purity and stillness are the right way for all under Heaven.
This concludes the meaning of what came before.

The most refined clarity lies within the turbid,
yet nothing can obscure it.
The most profound stillness dwells amid motion,
yet no affair can disrupt it.

Moreover, the **Great Completion, Great Fullness, Great Rectitude, Great Skill, Great Binding**—
these have their counterparts:
**not exhausted, not depleted,**
**as if bent, as if lacking, as if hesitant**—
yet only **clarity and stillness**
can uphold all things
and stand as the central balance for all under Heaven.

Zhuangzi said:
*"Hold the spirit in stillness, and the form will align itself.
Be still, be clear—
do not weary your body,
do not agitate your essence—
then you may attain longevity."*

When the outer is deficient yet the inner is whole,
this is called **the Way**.
Thus, it is followed by: *"All under Heaven has the Way."*

**End of *Dao De Jing: Hidden Chamber, Revered Subtlety*, Scroll Six**
**Begin *Dao De Jing: Hidden Chamber, Compiled Subtlety*, Scroll Seven**

**Begin *Dao De Jing: Hidden Chamber, Compiled Subtlety*, Scroll Seven**

The **Biqiuzi** Chen Jingyuan compiled:

**The Virtue Classic (De Jing):**
When the Dao prevails under Heaven,
warhorses are driven out to till the fields.

*However*, **屏去 (bǐng qù)** means to set aside.
*And* **治田 (zhì tián)** means to cultivate the land.

When the Dao prevails under Heaven,
this speaks of a time of **Great Peace (shí tài)**.
In times of Great Peace, the people flourish,
and ancestral temples shine in splendor.
The realm is at ease,
and feudal lords come as honored guests.
The seas grow tranquil,
and arms rest in repose.
All turn to the roots of life,
with every household striving in agriculture and sericulture.
Thus, warhorses—**the martial prowess (wǔ gōng)**—
are set aside,
and the people return to **the common arts (cháng yè)**.

Hence, the **Heavenly Heart (Tiān Xīn)** is harmonious and content,
and all things rejoice in their natural state.

When the Dao does not prevail under Heaven,
warhorses breed in the **交 (jiāo) [borderlands]**.
*Here*, **交 (jiāo)** refers to the frontier between two states.

When the Dao does not prevail under Heaven,
this speaks of a time of **Great Disorder (shí fǒu)**.
In times of Great Disorder, official duties are neglected,
and the sovereign grows anxious.
The treasuries are drained,
and floods and droughts plague the land.
Counties and districts fall to bandits,
and the strong oppress the weak.
All lose their means of livelihood,
and the custom of war takes hold.
Naturally, the **戎骇 (róng hài) [battle]**-horses breed in the borderlands,
thus **阴阳 (yīn yáng) [the cosmic forces]** are severed and blocked,
and all living things mourn in sorrow.

No crime is greater than **可欲 (kě yù)**.
If a man has **可尚 (kě shàng) [worthy of reverence]**, **欲爱 (yù ài) [desires to love]**, and **之心 (zhī xīn) [heart]**
that crave what is **非理 (fēi lǐ) [unreasonable]**, then his **贪求 (tān qiú) [greed and grasping]**
will be like **火驰 (huǒ chí) [wildfire]**, unstoppable and unreturning.
Thus, he who pursues the **秋毫 (qiū háo) [finest hair]**
loses the **太山 (Tài Shān) [great mountain]**.
He who indulges in **侨奢 (qiáo shē) [excessive desires]**
must bear the **校之凶 (jiào zhī xiōng) [punishment of calamity]**.

For the cause of crime, nothing is heavier than this.

*The Liezi* says: A man from Qi desired gold. At dawn, he dressed formally and went to the market, arriving at the place where gold was sold. There, he seized the gold and fled. The sheriff captured him and asked, *"All were present—why did you seize another’s gold?"* He replied, *"When I took the gold, I saw no one—only the gold. Like gazing into murky water and losing oneself in a clear pool."* No greater calamity exists than **unknowing contentment**.

Calamity—harm. Among the things one may desire, even within greed, there are limits. But the unknowingly contented—whatever passes before their eyes, they take nothing, yet their cup overflows without spilling. Ruin and disaster follow, so no calamity surpasses **unknowing contentment**.

No greater misfortune exists than **desiring to possess**. Misfortune—ruin. Even the things that pass before one’s eyes have bounds. If one desires all under heaven—seen and unseen—and seeks to hoard them all, leaving none untaken, this is the height of **without-Dao**. How could ruin not befall oneself alone? It also brings ruin to future generations.

Crime speaks of transgressing prohibitions. The first offense is called *guilt*; the Daoists call it *overt*. The Classics say: *"The first offense is guilt, also called error."* Error—mistake. To commit error a thousand eight hundred times brings *calamity*, heavier than guilt. To commit error two thousand one hundred sixty times brings *misfortune*, heavier still than calamity. These three are all deeds of **without-Dao**. The small harms the body; the great destroys the state. How could one not take warning?

Thus, **knowing contentment’s contentment** is always contentment. Though external things may suffice, if the heart does not know contentment, this is called **unknowing contentment**. Though external things may lack, if the heart remains content, this is called **knowing contentment**.

*The High Sage’s Transmission* says: The strict **Yán Jūn Píng** was friendly with the wealthy man **Luō Chōng** of Shu Commandery. Hearing that Jūn Píng declined an official summons, Chōng asked, *"Why do you not seek office?"* Jūn Píng replied, *"I lack the will to rise."* Chōng then prepared carriages, horses, provisions, and attendants. Jūn Píng said, *"I am ill—it is not lack."* *"I have abundance, yet you have not even a single coin—how can you say you have excess?"* Chōng retorted. Jūn Píng said, *"I once lodged in your home. When others slept, your servants labored without rest, day and night, ceaselessly—never content. Now I earn by divination; money comes without effort, and I have tens of thousands in surplus, piled high. I do not know what to do with it. How can you say I have excess while you lack?"*

Chōng withdrew, deceiving himself: *"He who enriches me diminishes my spirit; he who gives me fame destroys my life. If one truly knows contentment’s contentment, how could **battle-horses breed in the borderlands**, or calamity and misfortune be so heavy?"*

The Dao is nowhere absent—why seek it far? Thus, it follows: *"Do not leave the door, and you know the world."* Not leaving the door, one knows the world. The sage’s governance begins with self and heart, measuring near before reaching far. Without descending from the ancestral hall, one knows beyond the four seas. By observing things, one recognizes things; by knowing people, one understands people. When eating, think of the world’s hunger; when clothing, think of the world’s cold. Loving one’s elders reveals the world’s aged; pitying one’s children reveals the world’s young. If such, why leave the door to know the world?

*"Without peering through the window, one sees the Dao of Heaven."* A man’s seven-foot frame—four limbs, nine orifices, five viscera, six palaces—all contained within. Thus, the original *qi* of the body communicates with the Dao of Heaven. If the ruler preserves form in stillness, the Heavenly *qi* ascends bright and self-corrects. If the ruler indulges in excess desires, the Heavenly *qi* grows dim and turbid. The ruler, sharing virtue with the Two Forces, aligns with the sun and moon in clarity. Leading Heaven, Heaven does not resist; following Heaven, one honors its timing. Without peering through windows or gazing afar, the Dao of Heaven is self-evident.

Yán Jūn Píng said: Thus, the sage does not leave the door. Above, he examines parents; below, he measures descendants. Danger, security, profit, and harm return to himself, so he is clear on the sayings of death and life, discerning in right and wrong, comprehending the roots of profit and harm, reaching the foundation of order and chaos. Knowing oneself, one knows the family; knowing the family, one knows others. Affairs grasp their principles; things follow their order. In motion, one knows where to go; in stillness, one knows what to guard. Virtue and power are father; spirit and clarity are mother; stillness and purity are teacher; Great Harmony is friend. The world is home; all things are body. View others as oneself; view oneself as others. The heart dares not stir; the will dares not rise. Abandoning old knowledge, extinguishing the three and five, one does not dwell in cliffs or caves, does not slaughter living things, does not eat raw plants, does not wear unripe fruits, does not pluck before maturity. Heaven, Earth, and all things each preserve their own. This is called, within the unity of the body, modeling Heaven, Earth, and all things.

The farther one goes, the less one knows. The ruler who loses the Dao cannot model Heaven or walk the Way, yet relies on cleverness to discern things. The farther he seeks, the less he knows. Jūn Píng said: *"Examining Heaven and Earth, verifying past and present, actions never contradict—thus I know the Dao of Heaven is complete within me."* The family is the root of knowing people. The body is the source of knowing Heaven and Earth. To observe the world without the body is to observe people without the family. Near is small, far is great; small knowledge leads to great confusion. Leaving the family and the door, one does not see the world. Leaving the body to peer through windows, one does not know the Dao of Heaven. The farther one goes, the less one knows. Wandering the four seas, one is ever more confused. The more one seeks, the greater the pursuit; the greater the pursuit, the smaller the achievement. Without seeing or hearing, seek within oneself. At the juncture of Heaven and man, the Great Dao is complete.

To govern the realm, first govern one’s own country; to govern one’s country, first govern one’s family; to govern one’s family, first govern one’s self; to govern one’s self, first cultivate one’s heart; to cultivate one’s heart, first rectify one’s intentions.

Thus, the virtuous person, if not sincere, will see all things turn against them—this refers to the body and the heart. Therefore, the Sage acts without movement yet knows, does not see yet names.

The Sage, though not traversing the world, yet discerns human affairs by observing the self within the self and knowing the outer through the inner—this is called *solitary enlightenment*.

The Sage, though not witnessing celestial omens, yet can name and command the Dao of Heaven by examining the small to comprehend the great, clarifying the near to illuminate the far—this is called *mysterious contemplation*.

The Sage achieves without action. The Sage transforms the world without contrivance, merely illuminating the natural inclinations of things and following their course.

Confucius said: *"He who governs without action—is he not Shun?"*

To learn is to grow daily, yet the Dao is diminished daily. The Dao grows through learning, but once grown, it must be diminished; knowing the child yet guarding the mother, returning to the origin—this is the root.

Returning to the origin, one must first discard cleverness and grasp the essential. Clinging to the vital and holding fast to the root, the Dao is diminished daily—this is entering the realm of boundless mystery.

Diminish again and again, until one reaches *non-action*. When one acts without contrivance, there is nothing that cannot be achieved.

The one who diminishes does not force gain nor discard what is extraneous. When one discards until one forgets what is wrong, then there is nothing minute that is not removed. When one removes until one forgets what is right, wrong is not wrong, and right is not right.

Having diminished what is wrong and what is right, it is said: *"Diminish again and again."* If both right and wrong are forgotten, self-interest naturally vanishes, and nature aligns with the Dao, reaching *non-action*. Once in *non-action*, one does not compete with things, but lets all things follow their own course. Thus, all things are achieved without contrivance.

To take the world, one must act with *non-interference*. When there are affairs to attend, it is insufficient to take the world. The Sage and the virtuous follow the Dao: first cultivating the self and the heart, then extending to the world.

Earlier, it was said to govern the self through *non-action*; now it is said to govern the world through *non-interference*. Thus, the Sage’s way of thinking is always concerned with one thing’s loss of place, and the key to rectifying flaws and chaos lies in taking the hearts of all under Heaven.

To take the hearts of all under Heaven, one must use *non-interference* as defense, *non-action* as guidance, and *non-desire* as treasure. When the people naturally transform into simplicity and tranquility, they willingly embrace and do not tire of it.

If one governs through contrivance and interference, the people will labor under burdensome rule, stand in dread, and criticize in secret. How can one then take the hearts of all under Heaven?

Once growth leads to diminishment, and diminishment reaches *non-heart*, it is said: *"The Sage has no fixed heart."* The Sage takes the hearts of the people as their own.

The Sage embodies the Dao with an empty heart; things respond to their own nature, and since responses are not uniform, the Sage’s heart remains without fixed inclination.

Yet the hearts of the people always desire to secure their lives and fulfill their nature. The Sage who lets all people attain what they desire—is he not the one who takes the hearts of the people as their own?

Zhuangzi said: *"The Perfect One’s use of heart is like a balance scale—neither pushing nor pulling, responding without storing. Thus, they can overcome things without harming themselves."* The Sage has no fixed heart.

To those who are good, I am good to them; to those who are not good, I am also good to them—*virtue* is goodness.

The people have a natural inclination toward goodness. The Sage does not contradict their nature, responding with goodness. Those whose nature is inherently good, the Sage further aids with greater goodness, ensuring their goodness is perfected.

If there are those whose nature is not good, the Sage still treats them with goodness. Under the influence of the Sage’s *virtue*, their hearts naturally shift toward goodness.

Thus, there are none in the world who are not good—this is the Sage’s way of harmonizing with natural inclinations to complete transformation, not forcing things to conform to themselves.

To those who are trustworthy, I trust them; to those who are not, I also trust them—*virtue* is trust.

The people have a natural sincerity toward trustworthiness. The Sage does not suppress their resolve, responding with trust. Those whose trust is firm, the Sage aids with greater trust, ensuring their trust is perfected.

If there are those who are not sincere in their trust, the Sage still treats them with trust. The untrustworthy, influenced by the Sage’s *virtue*, naturally awaken their sincerity into trust.

Thus, there are none in the world who are not trustworthy—this is the Sage’s ability to accommodate human nature, not out of love or self-interest.

The Sage moves through the world with *solemn vigilance*, regulating the hearts of all under Heaven.

*Solemn vigilance* is the appearance of concern and movement, yet never ceasing. *Undivided* means without distinction.

In ancient times, rulers in the world, though their governance appeared concerned and active, shared in the people’s affairs, yet their hearts remained serene and empty, aligned with nature. Thus, they could embody transformation, harmonize change, and reach nothing that could not be achieved. They balanced all things as one, and nothing was left untransformed.

For the people of the world, the Sage makes their hearts undivided and their desires unified, follows their nature to harmonize their transformation—who would not willingly embrace this?

To labor over the mind and exhaust thought, treating affairs as affairs, is to achieve something. Lu Xi-sheng said: *"When the Sage is present, the world is in awe. Responding to things and their influences, they never cease, yet their heart is undivided, in harmony with all under Heaven. They never have their own agenda."*

Thus, the four things that Master Zhongni renounced were: *no intentions*, *no absolutes*, *no rigidity*, *no self*.

Therefore, they could achieve *no may or may not* and *no action or inaction*.

The *Riverbank Master*’s text reads: *"chùchù"* (怵怵).

The *King Pi* version reads: *"xīxī"* (歙歙).

The common people all pour their ears and eyes into [worldly] affairs, while the Sage treats them as children.

*"Zhù"* (注) means to pour forth, to apply.
The Sage cultivates with *wuwei* as virtue, never opposing the natural inclinations of all things. Thus, the people are bathed in their sacred virtue, each fulfilling their inherent capacities.

The wise are made to scorn, the perceptive to listen—all pour their ears and eyes into imitating the Sage’s natural Way. Yet the Sage, his crown flowing loose, his gaze distant, his ears blocked, does not strain his body with cleverness nor probe the hidden in things. He cradles the common folk in his thoughts, treating them all as infants—thus it is said: *"to treat them as children."*

Without fixed intentions, one is undivided. Responding to transformations as a child does, one obscures life and death—hence the next stage: *birth and death*.

**Birth and Death:**
From emptiness arises natural spontaneity; from spontaneity arises the Dao; from the Dao arises *yī qì*—one breath, which transforms and gives rise to all things. Thus it is called *birth*.
At the peak of life, transformation renders it formless—thus it is called *entering death*.
This is yet confined within the realm of beginning and end.

To transcend this realm, how could life and death ever bind one?

There are thirteen categories of those who cling to life, and thirteen of those who court death.
*"Tú"* (徒) means kind, type.

Those who cling to life: those who practice *shèshēng* (摄生) fall into this category.
Those who court death: those who rush toward death fall into this category.
The thirteen: as Han Fei said: four limbs, nine orifices—thirteen in total, their movement and stillness all tied to life and death.

Now, the adept at *shèshēng* does not wastefully gaze, does not wastefully listen, does not wastefully smell, does not wastefully speak, does not wastefully grasp, does not wastefully tread—his movements and stillness are effortless, committing no evil. These are the ranks of those who seek longevity.

Those who court death, however, let their eyes wander over garish colors, their ears indulge in wanton sounds, their noses grow weary of foul odors, their mouths crave what is unrighteous, their hands favor wicked tools, their feet quicken on forbidden paths. Their movements and stillness are drowned in vice, with no virtue remaining—these are the ranks of those near death.

The roots of life and death all hinge on these thirteen matters.

Sun Deng said: Among the things of Heaven and Earth, those that align with nature are few, while those that defy it are many. Thus, in ten cases, only three follow nature.

Now, life not lived in accordance with the Dao, death not met with reason—those who follow life are few, while those who defy death are many. Hence, in death, only three submit to fate.

Some interpret this as: the threefold business of ten evils—those who master and subdue them belong to the ranks of longevity. Those who indulge belong to the ranks of near-death.

By reasoning through it, the nine orifices and four limbs are the most thoroughly discussed.
Human life is a descent into death’s domain through these thirteen matters.

Now, why is it that people’s ordinary actions lead them toward death’s domain?
It is because they are seduced by desire for gain.
They crave rich flavors, fine silks, beautiful colors, pleasing sounds—all to indulge the nine orifices and four limbs. Thus their movements are strained, as if compelled against their will, yet they fall into death’s domain without knowing it. None escape the thirteen matters.

Why is this?
Because those who advance life excessively do so at the expense of their own substance.
*"Shēngshēng"* (生生) means to further life.
To forget life’s weight upon oneself is to hope for longevity, while to advance life at one’s own expense is to draw near death’s domain.

Moreover, to advance life must first rely on external things. Though external things suffice to preserve the body and delight the nature, serving as means for prolonged life, yet those who forget themselves in their greed for wealth overreach in seeking life-preserving things, letting the thirteen matters slide into chaos and hastening themselves toward death’s domain.

It is said that the adept at *shèshēng* does not speak of *shèshēng*—*"swēi wén"* (盖闻) is a humble phrase.
*"Shè"* means to guard. Within guarding life, they grasp its subtleties, hence the adept at *shèshēng*.
Traveling on foot, they avoid rhinoceros and tigers; entering battle, they wear no armor.
The rhinoceros has no place to strike with its horn, the tiger no place to strike with its claws, weapons no place to lodge their blades.
They meet without design.

「The rhinoceros emerges from the southern banks of the Xiang River, its hue a deep azure-black, its form resembling a wild ox, with a single horn weighing a thousand *jīn* [~220 lbs].

The adept at *shèshēng* [guarding life] attains internally to the self, so all illnesses fail to arise. Externally, they harmonize with things, so all calamities cannot befall them. When they abandon all selfish gain, they naturally meet with fortune.

Of all the harms that tools bring, none surpass the sword and spear. Among beasts, none are fiercer than the tiger and rhinoceros. Thus, though the sword and spear stand before them, they are not alarmed—this is because there is no place for their blades to lodge. Though tiger and rhinoceros block their path, they are not startled—this is because there is no place for their claws and horns to strike. Hence, though all calamities assail the sentient, they cannot harm the unsentient.

Why is this? Asking why all calamities do not assail and spare the adept at *shèshēng* [guarding life]—it is because they have no place of death.

The supreme person does not indulge their inner desires, nor do they harbor outward harm toward things. Thus, they can forget the self while the self remains, for they have no place of death.

Zhuangzi said: Those who grasp the Dao/Way must reach *lǐ* [principle/reason], those who reach principle must discern *quán* [opportunity/strategic measure], and those who discern opportunity will not let external things harm them. The virtuous/powerful ones are not scorched by fire, drowned by water, harmed by cold or heat, or preyed upon by birds and beasts—not because they are callous, but because they perceive danger and safety, rest in calamity and blessing, and are cautious in departure and approach. None can harm them.

To die is to lose the Dao/Way; to awaken is for the Dao/Way to arise. Thus, it follows with *dào shēng zhī* [the Dao/Way giving birth to it].

The Dao/Way gives birth to it, and virtue/power nurtures it. The Dao/Way is the essence of emptiness. Virtue/power is the function of nature. The Dao/Way’s essence is emptiness, yet through movement it gives rise to things. All things receive *qi* from the Dao/Way, hence it is said to give birth to them.

Virtue/power’s function is natural, encompassing and nurturing things. Things take form from virtue/power, hence it is said to nurture them.

Things are shaped by their form, and their fate is completed by their circumstances. All creatures and plants are rooted in the Dao/Way and born, sustained by virtue/power, and when their material is fully formed, they take shape, hence it is said to shape them.

Once things have taken form, they follow the changes of the four seasons to reach completion. Thus, all things cannot but revere the Dao/Way and honor virtue/power.

The Dao/Way descends as pure essence, giving birth to the nature of things. Virtue/power contains harmonious *qi*, nurturing the form of things. Hence, all things revere and honor the Dao/Way as if it were their father, and virtue/power as if it were their mother.

The reverence of the Dao/Way and the honor of virtue/power—none can confer rank upon them, yet they are always naturally present. Rank is the bestowal of command. The example of this is the titles of duke, marquis, earl, viscount, and baron.

The reason the world holds things in reverence and honor is because the sovereigns bestow rank and command upon them. Yet the Dao/Way, with its pure essence, gives birth to things, and all things naturally revere it like a father. Virtue/power, with its harmonious *qi*, nurtures things, and all things naturally honor it like a mother.

All things universally receive the Dao/Way’s and virtue/power’s nurturing and completing functions, revering and honoring them as if they were parents—not because of conferred rank, but because they are always naturally present and have the power to guard and subdue.

Thus, the Dao/Way gives birth to it, nurtures it, lengthens it, educates it, completes it, ripens it, sustains it, and protects it. Earlier, it spoke of the Dao/Way giving birth and virtue/power nurturing, but here it does not mention virtue/power, for the Dao/Way permeates all without omission.

To receive its essence is to be born, to contain its *qi* is to nurture, to fulfill its form is to lengthen, to refine its nature is to educate, to aid its function is to complete, to bring it to its end is to ripen, to preserve its harmony is to sustain, and to guard its injuries is to protect. These eight are the fundamental functions of the Great Dao/Way. Even the most minute of creatures—those that dart and creep—cannot escape its reverence and honor.

Born yet not possessed, acting yet not relying, lengthening yet not ruling—this is called *Xuán Dé* [Mysterious Virtue/Power]. This concludes the eight meanings above.

If the Dao/Way were said to give birth to all things as if it had possession, then its benevolence would be diminished. If it were said to nurture all things as if relying on its actions, then it would claim its function. If it were said to lengthen and educate all things, completing and ripening all types, sustaining and protecting all creatures and plants, as if it were proud of its rule—then it would be seen as presuming its length. With these attributes yet remaining hidden in secrecy, one can say its mysterious virtue/power is profound and distant.

The Dao/Way gives birth and virtue/power nurtures, providing things with their beginning. Thus, it follows with *tiānxià yǒu shǐ* [the world having a beginning].

The world having a beginning, the Dao/Way is the mother of the world. Once one has obtained its mother, one knows its child. The beginning is the Dao/Way, the root, the nameless.

The mother is nurturing, the named. The Dao/Way externally encompasses Heaven and Earth, internally fills the cosmos. All things draw upon it to be born and follow it to reach completion. That which completes is the child, that which gives birth is the mother.

The child is the One, and the One is the *chōngqì* [primordial breath], the Dao/Way’s child. The Dao/Way is the body of true essence, and the One is the function of wondrous things. Once the Dao/Way’s body is obtained, one knows the wondrous function. Body and function are interdependent, merging in the ultimate emptiness.

Having known the child, one again guards the mother. Thus, throughout life, one is unharmed. Having known the wondrous function of things, one again guards the true essence of the Dao/Way. Body and function become indistinguishably one, responding to stimuli without end, and only then can one act without doing and achieve all without action—thus, throughout life, one is unharmed.

Close its *duì* [joyous/pleasure], shut its gates—then one will labor not a day in their life. This illustrates the practice of cutting off desire and guarding the mother.

*Duì* means delight. It refers to the ears and eyes delighting in sounds and colors, the nose and mouth delighting in scents and flavors. The six senses each have their delights, and gates signify the paths of entry and exit.

The ears, eyes, and all the senses are the means by which dust and defilements enter. If one seals the gates of love and delight, then misfortune ceases and the body does not toil.

Again, *duì* is the eyes. If one closes the insatiable gaze, then all scenes will be cut off. Gates are the rivers. If one stops the mouth that speaks excessively, then all calamities will not approach.

When all scenes are cut off, the source of desires is sealed. When all calamities cease, the path to action is closed. Thus, one will be tranquil and at ease, laboring not a day in their life.

Open its *duì* [joyous/pleasure], and its affairs will flourish—yet one’s life will never be saved. If one does not guard the mother Dao/Way, opening the source of love and delight without sealing it, then the emotions of desire will grow ever longer.»

If the path of calamities is left open and not closed, then the affairs of misfortune will flourish. Thus, one will face sorrow, suffering, peril, and death, and never be saved.

To perceive the small is called clarity; to uphold softness is called strength. This is called preventing before sprouting, governing before chaos arises.

When calamities and chaos have not yet appeared, they are small—yet clearly visible, that is clarity. If one knows the arts of sealing *duì* and closing the gates, then one sees the subtle and minute.

Dulling the sharpness of desires and dissolving the turmoil of calamities—this is upholding softness and weakness. To uphold softness and weakness means that things cannot overpower one; this is called strength. To see the subtle and minute means that affairs cannot become obscured; this is called clarity.

Employ its luminosity, and return to its clarity. Luminosity is the illumination of wisdom. Wisdom arises externally, externally shines, and is ever in motion—motion is the function of things. Clarity is insightful understanding. Insight dwells internally, internally reflects, and remains ever still—stillness is one’s true nature. When wisdom shines outward, it responds to affairs; when it returns, it aligns with principle. Thus, function returns to essence, and it is said: *return to its clarity*.

This is a further elucidation of perceiving the small and upholding softness, causing the external to cease and the internal to return. To leave no harm to the self is called *clinging to the constant*.

*Heru* (遗) means to bestow or to leave behind. If affairs and principle are both clear, essence and function merge in obscurity, and one does not enslave wisdom’s external illumination while guarding insight’s internal reflection—returning desires before they sprout and restoring them to the primal Mother Dao—then there will be no self-inflicted harm, and this is called *secretly employing the constant Dao*.

To have a beginning and an end, to know the Child while guarding the Mother, to seal and close love and delight—is this not the upright and discerning? Thus, it follows with: *If one makes the upright and discerning have knowledge*.

If the upright and discerning have knowledge, walking the Great Dao, only their actions are to be feared. *Jie* (介) means solitary, upright, and resolute. Alas, if in this age one does not follow the ancient Dao, then it is said: *If one makes the solitary and resolute scholar have knowledge of governance, then they will walk the Great Dao*.

The Great Dao is the Way of effortless clarity, utmost fairness, and utmost straightforwardness. Yet those who walk the Dao must have actions. If actions are simple and easy, they guide the people toward the realm of clarity. If actions are convoluted and vexing, they lead the people toward greed and filth.

Only actions are to be feared—feared that they do not conform to the ancient Dao. The Great Dao is exceedingly smooth, yet people love shortcuts. The Great Dao is exceedingly smooth, like a grand thoroughfare. A grand thoroughfare is level and easy, reaching everywhere without obstruction, for its vast straightness does not fear minor detours. Yet people desire speed, seeking shortcuts, and thus become entangled in rugged paths, lost and confused, never reaching their destination.

Therefore, the Ancient Sage lamented this, cautioning only the methods of instruction, making them feared, and guiding the people along the broad path—referring to the following passage.

The palace halls are excessively cleared,
The fields are overgrown with weeds,
The granaries are empty,
Yet adornments are worn,
Fine silks and embroidered patterns are displayed,
Sharp swords are carried,
Beasts are fed sumptuous foods,
And wealth and goods abound.

Han Fei commented: *"Resource and goods are in excess."*

Enter a land, and its governance and teachings become apparent.

Observe the palace halls meticulously maintained, the walls and halls elaborately carved and towering—then one knows the sovereign delights in grand construction and indulges in frivolous pleasures. Observe the fields overgrown with weeds, agriculture neglected—then one knows the sovereign relishes forced labor, seizing the people’s time. Observe the granaries empty, farmers starving—then one knows the sovereign favors frivolous pursuits while abandoning essential livelihoods. Observe garments adorned with excessive embroidery, substance abandoned for superficial ornamentation—then one knows the sovereign indulges in licentious craftsmanship and exploits female labor. Observe belts adorned with sharp swords, valorized strength and martial competition—then one knows the sovereign cherishes martial prowess while neglecting governance. Observe feasts rejecting plain fare, savoring rich meats and fresh kills—then one knows the sovereign revels in gluttony and forgets state affairs. Observe wealth and goods in surplus, hoarding rare treasures—then one knows the sovereign amasses riches, impoverishing the people.

Confucius said: *"If the people lack, how can the sovereign suffice?"*
If plunder continues unchecked, the people’s strength will be exhausted—this is not the Dao of the Sage.
This is called *robberious boasting*, not the Way.

The seven misdeeds above all rely on deceitful expansion, seizing the people covertly without principle—thus, they are theft. Already thieves, yet they still boast grandly—hence, *robberious boasting* is not the Way.
This is what is meant by *"only instruction should be feared"*—here lies its proof.

Those who know the Way and practice it well have virtue that cannot be undermined by teaching, hence the next step: establishing what cannot be uprooted.
Those who establish well cannot be uprooted—*establish* means *to stand firm*. Those who stand firm in the Way, rooting their person and nation, first solidify their foundation before cultivating their branches, thus their roots run deep and their branches flourish, making them unshakable.

Those who embrace well do not lose—*lose* means *to depart*. *Separate* means *to detach*. Those who embrace the people with the Way have them loyal and undetected. Those who embrace the primal unity with the Way preserve their spirit whole and unlost. Their descendants’ sacrifices know no end.

Cultivate it in the self, and virtue becomes true.
Cultivate it in the family, and virtue abounds.
Cultivate it in the village, and virtue endures.
Cultivate it in the nation, and virtue flourishes.
Cultivate it in the world, and virtue is universal.
These five stages begin with self-cultivation and extend to the world.

To cultivate the Way in the self: the heart is at ease, nature joyful, *qi* nurtured, spirit nourished, desires few and private concerns minimal—thus life is prolonged, years extended, and one becomes a True Person.

To cultivate the Way in the family: fathers are benevolent, sons filial, brothers harmonious, younger siblings obedient, husbands trustworthy, wives virtuous—nine clans in concord, blessings flowing to future generations.

To cultivate the Way in the village: the aged are honored, the young cared for, the ignorant instructed—people united, ruler and subjects in trust, virtue enduring.

To cultivate the Way in the nation: natural joy arises, officials fulfill their duties, calamities and chaos do not arise, all treasures abundant—thus all things are plentiful.

To cultivate the Way in the world: transformation occurs without speech, governance without teaching—effortless, harmonious, universally inclusive—neighborhoods may be ennobled, influence reaching distant lands, virtue extending universally.

Thus, observe the self with the self, the family with the family, the village with the village, the nation with the nation, the world with the world.
*Observe* means *to scrutinize*.
Reflect on the Sage’s methods of self-cultivation to examine one’s own heart and mind—if one’s heart and mind embody the Way, then virtue becomes true.
Reflect on the Sage’s methods of family governance to examine one’s household—if one’s family harmonizes with kin, then virtue abounds.
Reflect on the Sage’s methods of village governance to examine one’s neighbors—if one’s community trusts in friendship, then virtue endures.
Reflect on the Sage’s methods of national governance to examine one’s people—if one’s people thrive, then virtue flourishes.
Reflect on the Sage’s methods of world governance to examine the world’s people—if the world’s people are without desire, without knowledge, then virtue is universal.

How do I know the world is thus? By this.
The Ancient Sage spoke: *"How do I know that those aligned with the Way prosper while those who abandon it perish?"*
The answer: By the five observations I have laid out, scrutinized in reflection—thus I know.

The Classic says: *"Without leaving one’s door, one knows the world."*
The *Yijing* says: *"Observe my life, observe the people."*—is this not its meaning?

To establish oneself and one’s nation with the Way, virtue extending to the world—how profound their contained virtue must be! Hence, next follows: *the depth of contained virtue*.

---
*The Daoist Classic of Authentic Virtue: Hidden Mysteries, Volume Seven*
*The Daoist Classic of Authentic Virtue: Compiled Mysteries, Volume Eight*

**The Daoist Classic of Authentic Virtue: Contained Virtue’s Depth**

**Chapter on the Depth of Contained Virtue**

The Master of Azure Emptiness, Chen Jingyuan, compiled:
*"The Depth of Contained Virtue"*—
**Contained virtue is like that of the infant.**

*Contained* means *to cherish within*.
The Perfect One is pure and unadulterated, his virtue deep and profound. His emotions return to their original nature, his desires extinguished—his form stands serene, like that of an infant.

The infant is taken as the epitome of pure harmony.
Poisonous creatures do not sting, fierce beasts do not seize, raptors do not strike.

*Poisonous creatures*—scorpions, wasps, and fireflies—sting with their tail tips.
*Fierce beasts*—tigers and leopards—seize with claw and fist.
*Raptors*—hawks and eagles—strike with talon and beak.

The Perfect One is divine—his essence lost and regained like the infant’s unknowing heart, so his spirit remains whole and nothing can harm him.

Master Yan Junping said:
The infant, as a thing,
knows yet does not manifest,
is aware yet does not reach,
is capable yet does not act,
is skilled yet appears clumsy,
lives yet seems dead,
is new yet seems worn,
acts without acting,
harmonizes with the Dao’s completeness—
life without life,
body without body,
use without use,
hearing without hearing,
doing nothing yet nothing undone,
no intention, no mind,
seeking neither virtue nor power,
accumulating no spirit,
thinking not at all,
yet no obstruction or burden,
spirit and qi unattached,
cleverness without knowledge,
soft and weak, yet empty and still,
soul and spirit undisturbed,
joying joy without joy,
resting in desire without desire,
neither wasting life nor bending will—
thus he can embrace the Dao and contain virtue,
aligning with Heaven and Earth.
So wasps and serpents sting without malice,
raptors and beasts attack without intent.

His bones are weak, his sinews soft, yet his grip is firm.
The infant’s perfect harmony illustrates the Perfect One’s pure virtue—
before knowing joy or anger, his clenched fist is unbreakable,
for his true nature is singular and undivided.

Before knowing union with male and female, his essence flows freely—
*essence* is the infant’s vital source.
The infant’s desires have not yet stirred; his innate virtue moves spontaneously,
as the Perfect One’s breath circulates without obstruction,
and all desires fail to intrude.

[*Essence flows* was originally written as *fullness*.]
Master Wang Bi said:
*Flow* means *to grow*—
unharmed by external things, he remains whole and thrives.

The *Upper Clarity Scripture of True Perfection* says:
When a person is born,
he receives Heaven’s and Earth’s qi as spirit and form,
and the Primordial One’s qi as fluid and essence.
When Heaven’s qi is exhausted, the spirit scatters.
When Earth’s qi is exhausted, the body falls ill.
When the Primordial qi is exhausted, life ends.
Thus the Emperor’s method of returning to the Origin
reverses the flow of the hundred vessels,
supplementing the muddy nine [organs],
filling the brain so the spirit is whole,
the spirit whole so the body is whole.
When the body is whole, the hundred passages are regulated within,
evil qi vanishes without,
marrow solidifies into bone,
intestines transform into sinews—
pure and unmixed, eternal life is attained.

He cries all day yet his voice does not grow hoarse—
this is harmony perfected.
*Crying* is *weeping*.
When weeping reaches its extreme, the voice fails—*hoarse*—
or becomes raspy.
The infant weeps endlessly, yet his throat does not tire or scatter,
for Heaven’s harmonizing qi is fully present.
Thus the True One’s breath aligns with *la* and *wa*—
harmony is complete.

Knowing harmony is constancy;
knowing constancy is clarity.
The infant’s full harmony is true—
the Perfect One values harmony as the constant way,
so knowing constancy does not change,
and guarding it brings self-illumination.
This is the depth of contained virtue.

Master Du Guangting said:
The Five Constants are all present in harmony—
when a person’s body is harmonized, virtue is complete and he attains truth;
when a nation is harmonized, its transformation spreads and its blessings endure;
when among the people, harmony brings ritual alignment;
when leading armies, harmony ensures victory.
Great is the meaning of harmony!
This is knowing harmony and constancy,
so virtue is whole and self-illuminating.

*Beneficial days* bring auspiciousness;
the heart directing qi brings strength.
*Auspiciousness* is the omen of good or ill.
Once formed, one’s inherent nature is fixed—
no artificial enhancement will bring fortune;
only ruin follows.
Master Zhuangzi said:
*"One should follow nature without artificial aid."*

The heart has right and wrong, yet qi has no discrimination—
trusting qi brings softness and weakness,
trusting the heart brings rigidity and force.
Moreover, will can move qi, and qi can move will—
when the heart directs qi, qi grows strong and the heart rigid.
Master Zhuangzi said:
*"Do not listen with the heart, but with qi—
this is the heart directing qi,
which benefits life but misleads the Dao."*

When things grow strong, they age—
this is called *not the Way*.
*Not the Way* brings early decay.
When things reach their peak, they wither and grow old.
When the heart grows strong, it turns violent and cruel.
Yet the Way employs softness and weakness—
to wield strength and rigidity as the heart is *not the Way*.
Thus the Ancient Sage warned:
*"The path of not the Way—
better to stop early."*
*Stop* is *death*.
He who does not walk the Way dies young.

Virtue is deep, still, and silent—
enlightened, yet speechless—
thus follows: *The Wise Do Not Speak*.

The Wise do not speak;
those who speak are not Wise.
The Wise know the Dao with the heart, not with words,
valuing action over speech.
Those who discuss the Dao rely on words, not the heart—
they lose the Dao yet keep their talk.

Master Yan Junping said:
Five tastes are in the mouth, five notes in the ear—
yet what is sweet is not sweet,
what is bitter is not bitter,
what is *shang* is not *shang*,
what is *yu* is not *yu*—
yet Yi Ya and Shi Kuang could distinguish them.
How? Because the mouth cannot speak.
If taste and sound are thus,
how much more the subtle Dao!

Master Zhuangzi said:
When the Wise traveled north, he was asked three times—
he answered *"Do nothing"* yet gave no reply,
not because he refused to answer,
but because he did not know how.
His intent aligns with this.

The *Western Ascension Scripture* says:
The Dao is natural—
those who practice attain it,
those who hear can speak,
but the Wise do not speak.
Those who speak do not know.
They speak to be heard by sound—
thus, those who discuss the Dao with words
are merely making noise.

「The unknowing trouble others with words,
yet those who do not hear do not speak—
they do not know how to begin.

· For instance, those who know the music
discern the sound by the strings,
yet though their heart understands the tune,
their mouth cannot convey it.
The Dao is profound and subtle—
the knowing do not speak.
When the sound of music brings sorrow,
one suppresses the external note
and lets the heart speak—
yet the speaker does not know.
This is the case for those who can act and speak.

Plug its *yuè* [口], shut its gate—
this is similar in wording to Chapter 52,
yet differs in meaning.
There, it restrains the Dao by quietude,
to block the roots of cravings and desires;
here, it upholds the Dao’s wordlessness,
thus advocating the blunting of hearing and discarding of sight.

The Dao is formless—
it cannot be seen with the eyes,
it cannot be spoken with the mouth;
thus the heart is perplexed,
knowing not how to grasp it,
and the mouth is silenced,
unable to discuss it.
This is why the Perfect One
does not need to block sight or close the gate—
his nature is inherently so.

Dull its sharpness,
resolve its confusion,
blend its brightness,
equalize its dust—
these principles have already been stated in Chapter 4,
yet there, they were applied to the Dao to discuss its efficacy,
while here, they are grounded in human conduct to clarify practice.

The Perfect One, in harmony with Heaven’s intent yet without knowledge,
aligns with the Dao’s essence yet lacks form—
thus, the heart of sharpness and confusion,
where would it arise from?
The mind of distinction and separation,
where would it be born?

This is called *Xuántóng* [玄同].

The Perfect One’s movements and dwellings:
When manifest, he shares the root with all things;
when obscure, he blends with emptiness and nothingness.
Thus, his words and silences adapt to the times without deviation,
and his spontaneous speech follows the sun’s rising to respond to change—
this is called *Xuántóng* [玄同].

Thus, he cannot be approached or distanced,
cannot be swayed for gain or harm,
cannot be honored or slighted—
yet for this reason, he is honored by all under Heaven.
He interacts with the Dao above without excess,
and though the world praises him, he is not emboldened—
thus, he cannot be approached.
He deals with affairs below without impropriety,
and though the world condemns him, he is not disheartened—
thus, he cannot be distanced.
His *dànpō* [澹泊] is without force,
his adherence to *fèn* [分] is contented—
thus, he cannot be enticed by profit.
Though in lowly stations he is not humiliated,
and though in ugliness he does not contend—
thus, he cannot be trapped by harm.
Rank and reward cannot sully him,
power and influence cannot sway him—
how could he be held in esteem?
Losing his resolve does not bend him,
dwelling in poverty makes him only more serene—
how could he be deemed base?

The Perfect One practices these six virtues:
he does not prosper in fame nor suffer in obscurity,
bears no Heaven’s resentment nor man’s blame,
is not burdened by things nor haunted by ghosts’ reproach—
thus, he is honored by all under Heaven.

· When one governs himself without coercion,
then he governs things naturally—
this is *zhèng* [政], the next stage is *zhèngzhì* [政治] to govern the state.
To govern the state with *zhèngzhì* [政治],
to use unconventional strategies in warfare,
to take the world with *wúshì* [無事]—
*wúshì* [無事] means *wúwéi* [無為].

The *zhèng* [政] of the *zhèngzhì* [政治] ruler,
who relies on governance and instruction,
though the people may not be extravagant,
their exhaustion and decline grow ever deeper.
Once the traces of governance are made clear,
how could such rule endure long?

The *bàwáng* [霸王] ruler,
who employs cunning strategies in warfare,
though the state may not topple,
misfortunes and chaos accumulate daily.
Resentment and longing multiply—
how could such rule remain firmly established?

The ruler with the Dao,
who seeks to win the world’s heart
and establish an enduring legacy,
finds nothing better than *wúshì* [無事].
Thus, Chapter 48 states:
*"To win the world, one must rely on wúshì [無事];
when one resorts to governance,
it is insufficient to win the world."*
This is the Master Lao’s teaching
on the way to govern the world in great peace.

*Zhèng* [政] is written as *zhèng* [正] in the *Héshàng* [河上] edition.

How do I know this to be true?
From this [text].

The Master Lao does not dare claim these words as his own,
thus he cites the Sage’s saying:

Some say the Master Lao was the historian beneath the pillars of Zhou,
who surveyed the ancient texts of the Three *Fèn* [坟] and Old Scriptures.
Hence, he cites them to verify his point.

*"I do nothing, yet the people transform themselves;
I take no action, yet the people grow rich."*

The Master Lao continues:

*"I love stillness, and the people align themselves;
I have no desires, and the people return to simplicity."*

I—this is the self-designation of the sovereign in an age of governance—
speaks of how I, without action, follow the Dao,
making no alterations, allowing the people to live as they will,
their customs transforming naturally.

I, without interference or disturbance,
frugal in my expenditures,
the people enrich their livelihoods,
their households prospering naturally.

I remain quiet and unspoken,
guarding myself in humility and fear,
the people embrace the harmony of Heaven,
their customs pure and upright.

I, without desire, am empty and void,
discarding ornamentation and honoring the root,
the people do not seek vain ambition,
their nature returning to simplicity.

If I act or desire,
yet hope to lead the people to wealth and longevity,
I have never seen such a possibility.

Zhuangzi said:
*"Heaven and Earth possess great beauty yet speak not,
the four seasons shine with clear principles yet debate not,
all things follow their natural laws yet are not explained."*

The Sage, thus,
originates from Heaven and Earth’s beauty,
penetrates the principles of all things.
Hence, the Perfect One acts without interference,
the Great Sage does without contrivance—
this is called observing the Dao of Heaven and Earth.

The *Héshàng* edition further states:
*"I have no emotions, and the people remain pure."*
The annotation reads:
*"By cultivating the Dao and guarding the True,
severing the Six Emotions,
the people naturally follow me in purity."*

In governing the state and transforming the people,
nothing surpasses *wúshì* [無事].
When *wúshì* [無事] is practiced,
its governance is expansive and relaxed—
thus, it follows with *"its governance is dim and tranquil."*

Its governance dim and tranquil,
its people are sincere and virtuous.
The *Kāiyuán* Imperial commentary says:
*"Dim and tranquil—without contrivance, expansively relaxed.
Sincere and virtuous—unadorned, thick with integrity."*
This means the sovereign without action,
whose governance is broad and lenient,
allows things to follow their natural course,
whose laws are not harsh or violent,
thus their customs become sincere and virtuous,
dwelling in purity and stillness,
ever growing in integrity.

The ancient text reads *zhèngzhèng* [偆偆], but Wang Bi renders it as *chúnchún* [惇惇].
Its governance is sharp and probing,
its people are depleted and scattered.
The *Kāiyuán* Imperial commentary says:
*"Sharp and probing—with contrivance, strict and urgent.
Depleted and scattered—withered, impoverished, and dispersed."*
This means the sovereign with contrivance,
whose governance is stern and urgent,
binds the people with laws,
whose decrees grow ever more explicit—
thieves and robbers multiply,
thus the people wither and disperse,
every movement entangled in prohibitions,
fearing and avoiding them,
their moral cultivation daily more ruined and deficient.

Disaster, oh! Fortune depends upon it;
fortune, oh! Disaster lurks within it—
who can know its limit?
*Yī* [倚] means "to rely upon";
*fú* [伏] means "to conceal."

The dim and tranquil governance,
though the world calls it lax,
yet the people are sincere and virtuous—
truly, they find joy in it.

The sharp and probing governance,
though the world calls it capable,
yet the people are depleted and scattered—
truly, they find sorrow in it.

What the world calls disaster,
none but rejoice in it—
fear brings caution,
and fortune arises from within.

What the world calls fortune,
none but fear it—
joy brings arrogance,
and disaster hides within.

Disaster and fortune rely upon and conceal each other—
none can fathom their ultimate bounds.

Thus, Heaven and Earth have their moments of rest and activity,
the sun and moon their waxing and waning—
this is the law of relying and concealing.

Disaster hides within fortune:
if fortune brings arrogance,
then disaster arrives.
Fortune conceals disaster within:
if disaster brings caution,
then fortune comes.
This is how the world operates.

Hence, the sovereign of the Dao,
guards it with purity and stillness,
trusts it to unfold naturally,
does not profit from wealth or treasure,
does not seek proximity to the noble and rich,
does not delight in longevity,
does not grieve over early death,
does not glorify the open and flourishing,
does not despise obscurity and want—
thus, disaster and fortune rely upon and conceal each other in what way?

There is no right or wrong in this.

Disaster and fortune rely upon and conceal each other—
how could there be no right or wrong?
It lies in whether the sovereign of the Dao
acts without interference or contrivance,
forgets form and substance,
and only then does it become right.
If one deliberately seeks to make it right,
that rightness will surely become strange.
If one deliberately seeks to do good,
that goodness will surely become unnatural.

Rightness becomes strange,
goodness becomes unnatural.

The hearts of the people are not singular.
The sovereign of the Dao,
uses his heart like a balance—
neither pushing nor inviting,
responding without seizing,
thus he can master all things without harming them.

If he enforces rightness upon what is not right,
that rightness will be strange.
If he enforces goodness upon what is not good,
that goodness will be unnatural.
If he allows things to naturally become right and good,
then disaster and fortune will not be perverse,
but will rely upon and conceal each other.

The people’s confusion is long-standing.
The shallow customs cannot naturally become right and good,
yet they force truth into rightness,
reverse nature into goodness,
and thus become strange and unnatural—
bewildered and unaware,
their origin of confusion is indeed long.

The *Xīshēng Jīng* [西升经] states:
*"To enforce rightness is to find no place—
rightness naturally returns to itself.
It does not receive what is perverse,
thus perverse *qi* departs—
this is called the Dao naturally aiding."*

Hence, the Sage is square yet does not cut,
the sovereign of the Dao
is square and upright in himself,
allowing things to awaken by themselves,
not enforcing his own square and upright upon them,
lest they follow him while losing their nature.

He is frugal yet not impure.
*Lián* [廉] means "clean";
*huì* [秽] means "impure."
The sovereign of the Dao,
follows his nature in cleanliness and frugality,
allows things to transform naturally,
not using his own purity to flaunt others’ impurity,
but merely letting things know to encourage
and wash away the impure.

The ancient text reads *chuàng* [创], meaning "to harm."
This means the Sage is generous in himself,
yet does not punish others to cause harm.

He is straightforward yet not domineering.
*Shì* [肆] means "to apply force."
The sovereign of the Dao,
inherits a straightforward and honest *qi*,
takes responsibility without bending,
yet does not impose his own directness
to forcefully apply or provoke upon others—
this is akin to great directness appearing as bending.

He shines yet does not dazzle.
*Guāng* [光] means "enlightened and wise."
The sovereign of the Dao,
is enlightened and wise, his discernment radiant,
yet also preserves and conceals himself,
not using his own strong wisdom
to dazzle and overwhelm others,
lest they be extinguished.

From this point onward,
all are examples of dim and tranquil governance,
not sharp and probing rule.

When governance is expansive, the people are fortunate;
when rule is strict, the people suffer disaster—
disaster and fortune rely upon and conceal each other,
originating from the people and from Heaven—
thus, it follows with *"the affairs of the people and the response to Heaven."*

To govern the people and serve Heaven,
nothing surpasses frugality.
Frugality is love.

The common folk dissipate their *qi* through excessive desires,
pursuing luxury yet never satisfied,
while the Sage cherishes *qi*, restrains expenditure,
and lives with moderation—thus having abundance.
Therefore, the ruler who governs without action,
maintaining clarity, tranquility, and simplicity,
reduces the people’s burdens,
fills the granaries and armories,
and teaches them propriety—
then preserves their vitality and loves their *qi*,
purifies sacrifices, and rejects extravagance,
so that both humanity and the divine are in harmony.
Thus it is said: *nothing surpasses frugality.*

Only through frugality is it called *early service*;
restraining expenditure without luxury,
moderating desires while cherishing *qi* and spirit—
this is to align with the Dao.
The Sage, before calamity or fortune manifests,
constantly aligns with the Dao—
this is called *early service.*

*Early service* is called *heavily accumulating virtue*;
to economize the people’s wealth,
cherish *qi* and spirit,
and thus serve the Celestial—
this is one virtue.
Again, by *early service* to the Dao,
bringing harmony between humanity and the divine,
it is called *heavily accumulating virtue.*

*Heavily accumulating virtue* means nothing is unconquered;
the one who *heavily accumulates virtue*
can rule over the people,
and all under Heaven will turn and revere him—
none will fail to submit.
If nothing is unconquered,
then its limits are unknown;
if none fail to submit,
then all things transform and are civilized,
and virtue and the Dao are boundless—
thus its limits are unknown.

If its limits are unknown,
then one can have a realm;
he whose virtue and Dao are boundless
will place himself last yet be first,
set himself aside yet endure—
all under Heaven will joyfully follow without tiring,
and thus can be the sovereign of a land.

In days past, Master Gēngsāng resided on the slopes of Yǔshān.
For three years, his people knew no sickness or plague,
and their grain stores overflowed.
They whispered among themselves:
*"When Master Gēngsāng first arrived,
we were struck by his difference.
Now, when we calculate daily, we lack nothing,
and when we calculate yearly, we have abundance—
could he be a Sage?"*
*"Shall we then sacrifice to him as a spirit,
and enshrine him as a deity?"*
This is what is meant by:
*"Virtue and the Dao have no limits,
and thus one can have a realm."*

「The mother of a realm, one may endure long;
The mother of a realm refers to the accumulation of virtue.
Virtue can nurture the people abundantly—
when the people are prosperous and rich,
then the altars of soil and grain (*shèqì*) may endure long.
This is called the Dao/Way of deep roots and firm stems,
of enduring life and lasting vision.
Roots are the foundation;
stems are the flower’s base.
He who cultivates fruit trees,
when their roots are deep, their stems are firm—
even if great winds arise,
they cannot uproot the foundation
nor shake the fruit from its place.
Thus, deep roots yield flourishing branches,
and firm stems ensure the fruit does not fall—
this is what is called enduring long.

The ruler who accumulates virtue,
in governing people and serving Heaven,
nourishes his land and sustains his people—
plants his roots in *wuwei* (non-action),
and secures his stems in *qīngjìng* (serene tranquility).
Then the altars of soil and grain extend far,
and this is called enduring life;
his reign is ever bright,
and this is called lasting vision.

Du Guāngtíng said:
Those who cultivate the Dao/Way,
harmonize their spirit and body,
accumulate *qi* and preserve their wholeness,
fortify their inner gates (*sānjiān*) while relaxing all concerns—
all spirits submit in order,
all affairs proceed in harmony,
transformation knows no bounds,
and their years are long and enduring.
Their stems are rooted in the *Hùnyuán* (Primordial Domain),
their roots run deep in the *Wúhé* (Land of No-Where),
and they stand shoulder-to-shoulder with the Nine Elders and Seven Ancients.

The ancient text read: *"deep roots and firm stems"* (*shēnguì*).
Serving Heaven and accumulating virtue,
one may have a realm—
thus it follows with governing great states.

Governing great states is like cooking small fish.
