To govern a realm of ten thousand chariots (*wànshèng zhī guó*),
is like cooking a fish of mere inches—
adjusting the water and fire,
letting it cook naturally, then it is whole and perfect.
Agitate it, and the fish is harmed;
the fish harmed, it rots and decays.
A skilled ruler nurtures his people,
harmonizing his governance and teachings,
letting them find their own way—
then they are naturally at ease.
Disturb them, and the people are harmed;
the people harmed, they grow chaotic and rebellious.

When the Dao/Way permeates all under Heaven,
its spirits are not fearsome.
The Sage employs the *wuwei* Dao/Way of serene tranquility,
and as he moves through the world,
his harmonizing *qi* spreads across all lands—
in valleys, valleys are full;
in ravines, ravines are full.
Thus winds and rains come in season,
floods and droughts do not arise,
human hearts remain natural,
and no one seeks false blessings.
Though spirits and deities exist,
they dare not display their fearsome transformations.
The *Xīshēng Jīng* says:
*"This is the Dao/Way of natural assistance—
not skilled in ritual sacrifice (*cí*),
yet spirits flee of their own accord."*

Spirits are the essence of transformation.
It is not that their spirits are not fearsome,
but that their fearsome transformations do not harm the people.
It is not that their spirits lack transformation,
but that their fearsome changes cease and vanish.
This arises from the people not making disorder—
then calamities and disasters (*qìxiáng*) how could they emerge?
When people uphold their constant virtue,
spirits’ fearsome transformations have no cause to harm the people—
this is the natural order.
It is not that the spirits do not harm the people,
nor that the Sage harms the people.
Their spirits, though fearsome, do not harm the people,
because the Sage’s *wuwei* and serene tranquility
move the spirits and deities to sense his radiant virtue,
and they themselves regulate their shadowy essences (*yīnlíng*).

The words of *Lièzi* on the Sage’s governance:
He neither gives nor bestows favors,
yet all things find sufficiency;
he neither hoards nor collects,
yet nothing is lacking.
The harmony of *yin* and *yang* is constant,
the sun and moon shine eternally,
the four seasons follow their course,
winds and rains are ever balanced,
the people are nurtured in time,
the land yields abundant harvests,
the soil knows no erosion,
the people no untimely deaths,
the world no sickness or plague,
and the spirits no fearsome manifestations.

For the two do not harm each other,
their virtues return in harmony.
The two are the Sage and the spirits.
Hé Shànggōng said:
The two do not harm each other—
people are governed by the *yang*,
spirits by the *yin*;
people preserve their lives,
spirits preserve their spirits—
thus their virtues return in harmony.
Moreover, when the ruler can preserve the spirits,
the spirits can bless the ruler—
and this is called the return of virtue.

Du Guāngtíng said:
The people are the foundation of the state;
when the foundation is firm, the state is secure.
The people are the masters of the spirits;
when the master is at peace, the spirits are content.
The Sage governs through the Dao/Way—
since he does not harm people or spirits,
the spirits, moved by the Sage’s achievements,
also do not harm the world.
The two take joy in each other,
their virtues return in harmony.

Great states govern through serene tranquility to secure the small,
thus it follows with the small states beneath them.

Great states are the lowest currents (*xiàliú*),
the confluence of all under Heaven.
The seas and rivers can be kings of all valleys
because they excel in descending;
great states can be emperors of all lands
because they excel in humility and descending—
thus they are where all under Heaven’s subjects gather.

The confluence of all under Heaven is female (*pín*),
for the female ever conquers the male through tranquility,
and the female is ever the descending one.

*(Continuing from the previous text:)*
The great state’s excellence in descending
is what makes it the confluence of all under Heaven.

The great state, though mighty, forgets its might—
thus no small state escapes its embrace.
In high position yet humble in descent,
it does not rely on coercive force or arms—
so neighboring states submit in gentleness,
all willingly becoming its servants and subjects,
thus winning the small states’ affection.

Yán Jūnping said:
The enlightened kings and sage rulers,
when governing great states,
bestow without expecting return,
descend without demanding obedience—
they enfold the lands of all feudal lords (*zhūhóu*)
yet none dare to fear them;
their virtue encompasses the strength of all feudal lords,
yet none dare to resist them.
They lower their station to align with others’ intentions,
prioritizing loyalty, integrity, and sincere trust,
holding scholars and worthies in esteem—
they cede land to attract virtuous talent,
encourage agriculture and weaving to amass wealth,
employ alliances and counter-alliances to guard against unforeseen threats,
open their granaries and distribute wealth,
support the aged and care for orphans,
relieve the poor and aid the distressed—
they honor reclusive worthies,
bestowing gifts yet not seizing them,
granting authority yet not claiming it,
practicing gentleness and simplicity,
employing *wuwei* in governance.
Though feudal lords may be greedy, cruel, or arrogant,
relying on brute force or their own might,
they are still subdued by the weight of the great state’s virtue—
when it descends in tranquility and humility,
those rulers regulate their conduct,
reform their errors,
cede land and offer treasures,
risk their lives to pledge allegiance,
and beg to be made vassals of its borderlands or tributary states.
Thus they win the hearts of the small states.»

The small state submits to the great state, and thus takes the great state’s authority.
The small state dwells in its smallness yet forgets its smallness,
so there is no great state it cannot dominate—
humble and reverent in its lowly position,
not indulging in arrogance or contempt,
thus neighboring states comfort and assist it,
all desiring to aid it—
this is how it seizes the great state’s might and prestige.

Mencius said:
Only the virtuous can act with greatness toward the small,
thus Tang served Ge Bo, and King Wen served Kun Yi.
Only the wise can act with smallness toward the great,
thus Taiwang served Xuanchuang, and Goujian served Wu.

Those who act with greatness toward the small are joyful in Heaven’s will.
Those who act with smallness toward the great fear Heaven’s will.
Joyful in Heaven’s will, they preserve the world.
Fearful of Heaven’s will, they preserve their own state.

The *Odes* say:
*"Fear Heaven’s might, and in its time, you shall be preserved."*
This is from the *Zhou Preface*,
praising King Cheng’s fear of Heaven’s might,
and his ability to secure lasting peace.

Yan Junping said:
*"When in a weak and small position,
move according to the Way, follow virtue without fail,
act with sincerity and loyalty,
value simplicity and till the fields,
harmony between ruler and people,
the ruler as the heart within the body,
the people as limbs to the body—
unified in purpose, they can die together."*

Even if the great state’s ruler
is burdened by strength, wields authority,
possesses virtue, and practices benevolence,
his heart like a hungry tiger, his wrath like a surging spring—
yet if he aligns with Heaven’s will,
gains the people’s favor,
earns the trust of ministers and neighbors,
unleashes the hidden might of his enemies,
humbles himself in body and spirit,
offers sincere allegiance,
and governs with quiet clarity—
then his spirit is moved, his resolve shaken,
his anger dissipates, his misfortunes shift,
and he pledges fealty to you.

Thus, sometimes submission takes authority,
sometimes submission seizes authority.
This is ambiguous phrasing.
*"Taking"* refers to the superior taking through humility,
subduing with meekness to win the people’s favor.
*"Seizing"* refers to submission to gain authority,
obeying with reverence to secure the great state’s power.
This is mutual reciprocity through righteousness.

The great state does not exceed its bounds,
desiring only to nurture all under its sway.
The great state’s ruler,
elevating humility and honoring reverence,
refining himself through rites and law,
subduing the small states—
does he truly harbor greedy ambitions,
seeking to annex and hoard the small states’ people?
He merely upholds the constant virtue of humility and reverence.

The small state does not exceed its bounds,
seeking only to serve others.
The small state’s ruler,
preserving loyalty and obedience,
refining himself through institutions—
does he truly harbor excessive desires,
pretending to serve the great state’s people?
He only holds firm to self-preservation,
guarding his inherent virtue.

Both attain what they desire,
so the great state ought to submit.
The great and small states,
each content with their portion,
mutually upholding their due,
without encroachment—
this is *"settled in their place, content with their customs,"*
neighboring states visible yet distant,
their roosters and dogs heard but never seen,
their people dying of old age without ever meeting.

Yet the small state’s submission is the constant of ritual.
The great state’s humility is truly complete sincerity.

The great state ought to submit—
urging deeply, making it enduring and steadfast,
thus securing the world’s alliances.

The great state’s humility contains the essence of the Way.
The Way is the deepest essence of all things.
Essence means hidden, obscure, contained.
The Way encompasses all things without exclusion,
its origins most profound, most hidden—
the fundamental root of all existence,
with nothing escaping its protection.

The *Western Ascension Scripture* says:
*"The Way is profound and hidden,
a boundless void—
speaking of the Way as the refuge of all things,
nothing not contained within it.
From the lofty dome of heaven to the vast earth,
from the sun and moon’s radiance to the finest of life—
all draw sustenance from the Way,
obscurely relying on its shelter."*

The virtuous man’s treasure,
the wicked man’s refuge.
The virtuous man—
the superior sage—
hears the Way and diligently practices it,
refining his essence and nurturing his spirit,
ascending with lightness and ease,
firmly guarding the wondrous root,
making it his enduring treasure.

The wicked man—
the inferior sage—
hears the Way and scoffs at it,
until he encounters its contrary and stumbles into misfortune,
then longs to return to fortune,
seeking the Way’s protection once more.

Beautiful speech can be sold,
noble conduct can elevate one.
This explains the wicked man’s refuge.
Sweet words find favor everywhere—
even the most cunning merchant’s schemes
are met with eager agreement.
How could they compare to sincere speech,
dull and flavorless, dismissed without thought?

Noble conduct commands respect—
nowhere can it be ignored,
even the lowest traveler knows its worth.
How could it be likened to vulgar ways,
or whitewashed as shame?

Thus, even those without speech or conduct,
borrowing sweet words and noble deeds,
can win over crowds and rise above the masses—
how much more so those who embody the Way!

Why abandon those who are unvirtuous?
Even the deceitful and false,
relying on beautiful speech and noble conduct,
can please the multitude—
how much more so the Way, which can be leaned upon for refuge.
From this, how can one say the unvirtuous shall always remain so?
Only fearing transformation’s reach—
why abandon them at all?

Thus, the Son of Heaven is established,
and the Three Ministers appointed.
Heaven entrusts its Way to the ruler,
commanding him to transform the unvirtuous.
The ruler, fearing transformation may be incomplete,
further appoints the Three Ministers to assist him.

Du Guangting said:
*"The vastness of the Four Seas,
the abundance of all things—
at their origin, humanity could not exist without governance,
thus the Son of Heaven is established to nurture them,
revering Heaven and Earth as father and mother,
and is called the Son of Heaven."*

One ruler cannot govern alone—
thus officials are appointed to oversee him,
and among them, the Three Ministers stand supreme.
*The Book of Documents* and *The Rites of Zhou* declare:
*"It is the Three Ministers alone
who discuss the Dao and govern the realm."*

The Three Ministers—
the Grand Marshal (太尉), Grand Tutor (司徒), and Grand Minister of Works (司空)—
assist the Son of Heaven,
regulate yin and yang,
cherish the myriad people,
and broaden the teachings of the Way.
Even if one offers a jade *guangbi* as tribute before four-horse chariots,
it is not as valuable as presenting this Dao.

Du Guangting explains:
*"Two hands clasped form the gesture of 'guang' (拱).
The *bi* (璧) is a ceremonial jade.
The *guangbi* is the largest of jades.
Four horses harnessed together pull a single carriage.
In ancient times, when feudal lords paid homage to the Son of Heaven,
assembled at great states, or visited small ones,
or met in the wilds,
when two rulers faced each other,
they observed the ritual of presenting tribute first—
leading with gifts and currency, called 'advancing' (先).
Now, the Three Ministers should prioritize discussing the Dao
and governing the realm.
Even if they wished to offer four-horse chariots and great jades to the ruler,
it would still be the duty of subjects.
Such gifts only benefit licentious extravagance,
but contribute nothing to governance.
It is far better to advance the Dao of *wuwei* (無為) and serene stillness
to transform the world—
so that the unvirtuous turn to goodness,
the unrepentant change their hearts,
and the Way’s transformation spreads universally,
while imperial virtue extends far and wide.
What need is there for jades or horses?"*

Scholars who pursue learning
value an inch of time over a foot of jade—
how much more so the ruler who embodies the Dao?
Why did the ancients hold this Dao in such esteem?
Because it is the Dao of *wuwei* and serene stillness.

Someone asks: *"What did the ancients mean by valuing this Dao?"*
Did they not say:
*"Seek and you shall obtain,
commit guilt and you shall be absolved"*?
Thus, it is held in high regard by all under Heaven.

The subtlety of the Dao
begins with enlightenment within the mind and body,
and ultimately illuminates the cosmos beyond.
Its mysteries penetrate the formless void,
while its unfolding fills the boundless expanse.
To embody it is to perfect oneself,
to apply it is to benefit the world.
Though it may not be said *"seek and you shall obtain,"*
it cannot be neglected.

To obtain it without seeking is to attain it naturally.
If attained naturally, why need the Three Ministers at all?
Thus, it is not *"seek and you shall obtain,"*
but rather *"seek and you shall obtain"*—
a principle the ancients held constant.
Those who comprehend this constant Way
shall have no guilt to bear.

If one were to stray from the Dao and commit a fault,
by amending one’s errors and returning to the Way of *wuwei* and serene stillness,
one may yet avoid disgrace.
The original text reads: *"Did they not say 'seek and you shall obtain'?"*
Yan Junping’s version states: *"Obtain it without seeking."*

The profound mysteries of attaining the Dao
lie in acting without action—
thus it is called *wuwei*.
To do without doing,
to act without laboring,
to taste without indulging—
the Perfect One does nothing,
yet lets things act of themselves.
Things act of themselves, yet there is no doing without doing.
Still, the Perfect One’s heart never stirs.

To labor without toil is to let things manage their own affairs.
Things manage their own affairs, yet there is no labor without labor.
Still, the Perfect One’s body never wearies.

To taste without desire is to follow the essence of things.
Things follow their own essence, yet there is no taste without taste.
Still, the Perfect One’s palate never craves.

If the heart remains undisturbed, clarity is boundless.
If the body labors not, substance is profound.
If the mouth desires nothing, tranquility endures.
If tranquility endures, vital essence flows without end.

Another interpretation of *"taste without taste"*:
It is not the speech of beauty or ugliness,
but the speech of the Great Dao’s flavorlessness.
The Classic says:
*"When the Dao issues from the mouth,
its taste is faint and without flavor."*

Great or small, much or little—
rewarding resentment with virtue.
People say there are great and small matters in the world,
and that human affairs range from much to little.
They say that all actions diminish one’s true nature
and stray from spontaneity.
Diminishing true nature breeds resentment,
straying from spontaneity spawns malice.
Resentment and malice together
are the stages of calamity and chaos.

Only the Perfect One, with *wuwei*, *wuwei*, and *wuwei*,
can extinguish the heart and wither the body.
Even if there are resentments from those who act,
they are all met with the Perfect One’s heartless, boundless virtue.
Someone asks: *"If one has virtue, how does one repay it?"*
The world’s affairs—great or small, much or little—
its anger, resentment, kindness, and virtue—
are all repaid with the Perfect One’s heartless, boundless virtue.

Lu Xisheng says:
*"Those who embody the Dao are profound and boundless,
responding to the world while rooted in *wuwei*,
attending to affairs while rooted in *wuwei*,
tasting without indulgence while rooted in *wuwei*.
Though their form may be vast, their essence is simple and small.
Though their deeds may be many, their essence is subtle and few.
Thus, they flourish in simplicity and stillness,
unshaken by all things."*

Only thus can there be no desires,
no desires mean no selfishness,
kindness is the accumulation of selfishness,
resentment is the seed of kindness.
Only the Sage can have no selfish desires,
no selfish desires mean no selfish kindness,
no selfish kindness means no selfish resentment.
Common people are not so—
because they have selfish desires,
they have kindness and resentment.

Yet those under Heaven who harbor resentment,
the Sage repays them with virtue.
Those who act unvirtuously,
the Sage cultivates them with virtue.
Thus, the people live in harmony,
and ruler and ruled have no resentment—
this is what is meant.

Zhongni said:
*"To walk the Way fully under Heaven is to have no resentment.
How could the Sage harbor resentment toward anything?"*

Plan for difficulty when it is easy,
act on greatness when it is small.
Difficulty and ease, greatness and smallness—
these are the world’s great and small matters.
Right and wrong, beauty and ugliness,
resentment and anger, kindness and virtue—
all arise from subtle beginnings.
None do not begin as easy and end as difficult,
or start as small and become great.
If one plans at the time of ease,
there will be no difficulty at the end.
If one acts at the time of smallness,
there will be no greatness later.

But if one waits until difficulty arises,
or acts when greatness is already upon them,
resentment deepens and calamities accumulate.
To then seek to dissolve difficulty and untangle chaos—
is it not difficult?

The great difficulties of the world must be undertaken when they are easy.
The great affairs of the world must be addressed when they are small.

For arduous tasks, they must arise from ease—
one must plan for them when they are still easy.
For weighty matters, they begin in subtlety—
one must act upon them when they are still minute.

The myriad types of affairs are too numerous to enumerate,
thus the world is spoken of in general terms.

Yan Junping said:
When great difficulty is about to arise,
it is like a malicious wind entering a person.
Before it manifests, if one is cautious and prevents its coming,
it remains at the skin and hair—
boiling with soup can dispel it.
When it penetrates to the sinews and veins,
a fine needle can extract it.
When it reaches the organs,
hundreds of medicines can remove it.
When it sinks to the marrow,
even Heaven and Earth cannot transform it,
and the processes of creation cannot cure it.
Thus it is said: the world’s great difficulties begin in ease.

When great affairs are about to emerge,
they are like water issuing from a mountain—
first appearing as dampness and droplets,
then flowing ceaselessly, gathering into streams and valleys.
Therefore, the Sage never seeks greatness directly,
and thus achieves true greatness.

The earlier passage spoke of those who lose the Dao,
who delight in difficult and great affairs,
and thus often meet with ruin.
Here, it cites the Sage’s refusal to seek greatness—
the utmost caution toward subtleties.

The Sage does not pursue difficult and great affairs,
yet does not remain idle—
easy, simple, and effortless,
thus able to accomplish enduring and vast undertakings.

Yan Junping said:
The Sage’s establishment of merit and fame is subtle,
thus able to reveal the hidden,
thus able to illuminate the small,
thus able to achieve great concealment,
thus able to manifest intent—
dwelling beneath the common people,
yet becoming a ruler of kings.

Light promises bring few fulfillments.
Many easinesses bring many difficulties.
Those who speak without threefold deliberation,
lightly granting promises,
engage in many affairs yet cannot keep their word.
Those who do not plan at the beginning but are cautious at the end,
who act lightly,
accumulate difficulties and face unpredictable changes.
How could one not be cautious?

Thus the Sage treats even these matters with difficulty,
and so ultimately faces no difficulty.

With the Sage’s talent and virtue,
they still find difficulty in small and easy matters—
how much more so for those without the Sage’s talent and virtue,
who neglect them?

Thus, the common world accumulates burdens,
while the Sage ultimately faces no difficulty.

Yan Junping said:
The Sage’s heart is silent and unmoved,
their mouth silent and unspoken,
their eyes silent and unseeing,
their ears silent and unhearing.
In motion, they are like Heaven and Earth;
in stillness, like spirits and gods.
They act without doing,
speak without words.
Advancing, they are invincible;
retreating, they are unexhausted.
Their body knows no minor worries,
their realm knows no trifling calamities.

The tranquility of non-action
must come from simplicity and ease,
thus it is followed by its stability and ease of maintenance.

*[End of Chapter Eight: Hidden Treasures of the Daoist Canon—*Mùwēi* Scroll]*
*[Beginning of Chapter Nine: Compiled Subtleties of the Daoist Canon—*Zuǎnwēi* Scroll]*

**Chapter Nine: Compiled Subtleties of the Daoist Canon—*Zuǎnwēi* Scroll**

Bi Xuzi Chen Jingyuan compiled:

The *Dao* of **virtue/power** is easy to maintain when its roots are deep,
its troubles yet unseen are easy to plan against,
its frailty is easy to break,
its subtlety is easy to disperse.

Act upon it **before its emergence**,
For when a realm is yet at peace and quiet,
it is easy to preserve and uphold.
But when it nears collapse and peril,
then guarding it becomes difficult.

When wickedness has not yet taken form,
it is easy to plot its eradication.
But when its evil shape is already manifest,
its annihilation becomes hard to achieve.

When the seeds of misfortune are tender,
they are easy to uproot and destroy.
But when branches and trunks have grown,
how can one strike them down?

When regret and sorrow are but faint traces,
worries and anxieties are easy to dispel.
But when calamity has already revealed itself,
its disaster cannot be undone.

The original text reads: *"its frailty is easy to judge"* (or *"to disperse"*).
**Act upon it before its emergence**—this means to **modify and remove** it beforehand.

When a realm has not yet fallen into peril,
and wickedness has not yet sprouted,
if one preemptively guards against it,
then what misfortune could arise?

**Prevent disorder before it begins.**
*"Prevent"* here means **to rescue**.

When the seeds of misfortune have not yet taken root,
and calamity has not yet attached itself,
if rescue comes first,
then no great harm shall arise.

Yan Junping said:
A person not yet sick is easy to heal,
a realm not yet in danger is easy to counsel,
a sprouting affliction is easy to address,
a small, weak misfortune is easy to dispel.

How does this work?
A cart with a single wheel missing can be guided by a child.
But when it has overturned and toppled,
a thousand men cannot restore it.

A fledgling bird, not yet able to fly,
can be stopped with a mere finger’s touch.
But when it has become a wild goose,
soaring with wings above the clouds,
no net or snare can capture it.

A newborn beast, still nursing,
can be bound with a single rope.
But when it has grown into a tiger,
seizing leather and devouring cattle and horses,
swords and spears cannot threaten it,
nets and traps cannot restrain it.

Thus, a single drop of water
can swell into vast oceans,
a small serpent, if not slain,
becomes a divine dragon.
Accumulated goodness leads to auspicious fortune,
while unchecked evil eventually brings ruin.

A tree of a thousand arms begins from a tiny sprout,
a nine-tiered tower rises from a single shovelful of earth,
a journey of a thousand miles starts beneath one’s feet.

These three things illustrate
the failure to heed early warnings and subtle signs,
leading to later calamities.

Misfortune not preemptively guarded against,
evil not gradually checked,
is like planting a tree.
At first, it grows from a tiny sprout,
which can be pulled out and destroyed.
But when it has reached a girth of embrace,
its roots are deep in the shadowed cliffs,
its branches stretch toward the sunlit peaks,
a hundred feet tall, blocking the sun and moon—
how can it be felled?

This shows **evil arising from one’s own nature**.
Again, like building a tower:
it begins with a single basket of earth,
which can be kicked away and ruined.
But when it has risen to nine tiers,
soaring a hundred *rèn* high,
matching the grandeur of hills and valleys—
how can it be demolished?

This shows **evil formed through accumulated habits**.
Again, like a traveler setting out:
they begin with a single step,
which can be turned back with a mere pivot of the heel.
But when they have gone a thousand miles,
long rivers stretch endlessly,
towering mountains rise steep and vast,
paths narrow and treacherous,
roads winding and forked—
how can they return?

This shows **a journey gone too far to halt**.
The *Yì* says:
*"Treading on frost, the solid ice is near"*—this is what it means.
Nine tiers (originally *"nine layers"*), the ancient text reads *"nine stages"*.

Those who act **meet ruin**,
those who grasp **meet loss**.
To act beyond necessity brings natural decay,
to act within desire **diminishes vitality**.
Yet those who cling to power and love fame
suffer loss unto their own lives,
those who value courage and delight in conflict
invite disaster upon their realms—
all because they cannot act **before its emergence**,
cannot rescue **before disorder begins**.

Thus, the Sage acts not, and so meets no ruin;
does not grasp, and so meets no loss.

Where things fail, failure comes from action—
how could non-action bring failure?
Where intentions falter, loss comes from grasping—
how could non-grasping bring loss?

Therefore, the Sage directs their mind
not toward petty achievements,
but always toward **emptiness, tranquility, serenity, and stillness**,
and **non-action**—
naturally without action,
without grasping,
without ruin,
without loss.

Zhuangzi said:
The Emperor of the Southern Sea was named **Tao**,
the Emperor of the Northern Sea was named **Hu**,
and the Emperor of the Central Sea was **Hun Dun**.
Tao and Hu once met in the land of Hun Dun.
Hun Dun treated them with great kindness.
Soon, Tao and Hu plotted to repay Hun Dun’s virtue,
saying: *"All people have seven orifices
for seeing, hearing, eating, and breathing,
but this one has none—
let us drill some for them!"*
Each day they drilled one orifice,
and on the seventh day, Hun Dun died—
this is proof that **action brings ruin**.

The people’s undertakings
often fail when nearly complete.
*"Near"* means **close at hand**.

The common folk, though they may begin with virtuous deeds,
yet have no completion—
they act first, then grow weary.
When their undertakings are nearly finished,
they neglect caution and vigilance,
and thus meet with ruin.

**To be as diligent at the end as at the beginning**
is the way to avoid failure.
This is a repeated admonition and exhortation.

If the common folk could **preserve their initial diligence to the end**,
all their endeavors would succeed.

The *Odes* say:
*"Though all may have a beginning,
few attain the end."*

Thus, the Sage desires not to desire,
and does not prize difficult-to-obtain treasures—
treasures such as gold, jade, pearls, and rhinoceros horn.

The common folk regard **not desiring** as a burden,
while the Sage finds it a joy.
The Sage values **tranquility, moderation, and self-restraint**,
while the common folk value **greed, selfishness, and extravagance**.
Therefore, what the common folk do not desire,
the Sage does desire—
and thus regards gold and jade as discarded soil.

**To not study when one should study**
is to repeat the errors of the masses.
The common folk **strain their innate nature** to pursue external learning,
and thus lose their **inherent true nature**.
The Sage **nurtures spontaneity without studying**,
and preserves the **harmony of their innate constitution**.

Yet the common folk consider **not studying** a fault,
while the Sage regards **not studying** as true learning—
hence, *"to repeat the errors of the masses."*

**To assist the natural unfolding of all things**
without presuming to act.
Human beings are born in stillness—
this is the nature of Heaven.

The Sage teaches **not to desire and not to study**
to aid the natural unfolding of all things,
so that each may fulfill its nature
without daring to contrive artificial deviations,
lest they lose their fundamental essence.

**To uphold without failure**
is to examine the subtle, unmanifest signs of joy and disorder,
without losing the **principles of lineage and the natural support of things**—
this was the **ancient Way of excellence**.

Thus, we follow the **ancient Way of excellence as Dao**.

Those who **excelled in the ancient Way of governing**
did not seek to enlighten the people,
but rather to **delude them**.

The ancient rulers who **excelled in the Way of governing themselves and transforming the people**
cultivated **sincerity within their own hearts**,
so that the Dao **permeated all things** while their governance remained unobtrusive,
and the people fulfilled their capacities without artificial praise.

They did not **flaunt their teachings and decrees**,
lest the people’s **joy and sorrow be manipulated** and deceitful scheming arise.
Instead, they sought to **guide the people back to simplicity and naturalness**.

This is the teaching of the **ancient Way of the Sage**,
offered as an exhortation for the present.

The difficulty in governing the people lies in their **excess of cleverness**.
When the people are too clever,
deceit arises first.
Though laws and decrees exist,
there is nothing to fear—
hence, they are **difficult to govern**.

Zhuangzi said:
*"If the ruler excessively favors cleverness without the Way,*
the world will fall into great disorder.
How do we know this is so?
When the cleverness of **bows, crossbows, and snares** abounds,
the birds above are thrown into chaos.
When the cleverness of **hooks, bait, nets, and traps** abounds,
the fish below are thrown into chaos.
When the cleverness of **snares, pitfalls, and nooses** abounds,
the beasts in the marshes are thrown into chaos.
When the cleverness of **deceit, poison, and sophistry**—
such as **‘firm and white,’ ‘separating filth from purity,’ ‘same and different’**—abounds,
the people are bewildered by confusion.
Thus, whenever the world falls into great disorder,
the fault lies in the love of cleverness."*

Therefore, **to govern a nation with cleverness is to ruin it**;
**to employ deceitful ministers to govern a nation**
is to **use cunning and schemes to undermine governance**,
and to **disturb and confuse the people**—
this is the **ruin of the nation**.

The *Classics* say:
*"When wisdom arises, great deceit follows."*
And again:
*"When governance is meticulous,*
the people become cunning."*

**Not to govern with cleverness is the nation’s blessing.**
**To employ virtuous and sincere ministers to govern a nation**
is to **practice non-action and simplicity**,
and to **bring the people peace and contentment**—
this is the **blessing of the nation**.

The *Classics* say:
*"I do nothing, yet the people transform themselves.
I take no action, yet the people grow wealthy."*
And again:
*"When governance is unobtrusive,*
the people are pure and sincere."*

「Du Guangting said: The ruler is like a mirror—
when the mirror is upright, its reflection is true,
when the mirror is crooked, its reflection is distorted.
If upright, the people follow and become upright;
if crooked, the people follow and become crooked.
The purity or corruption of the people is not determined by others.

To employ ministers of clever schemes,
the use of authority and stratagem will flourish,
to employ virtuous and loyal scholars,
the customs and teachings will become pure.

When the people are transformed in purity and harmony,
the nation prospers and thrives—
this is the blessing.

Understanding these two principles,
is to grasp the eternal model.

The two principles refer to
employing cleverness and not employing cleverness.

He who employs cleverness
harms governance and plunders the people—
he is the nation’s thief,
leading to the ruin of oneself and one’s family.
Thus, not employing cleverness
is the eternal model to be followed.

He who does not employ cleverness,
practices virtuous governance and benefits the people—
he is the nation’s blessing,
leading to honor for one’s clan and glory for one’s kin.
This is also the eternal model
to be followed by those who employ cleverness.

The ancient text reads *kǎishì* (稽式), *kǎi* (稽) meaning "to examine."
It refers to examining ancient models.

Always knowing the eternal model,
is called *Xuándé* (玄德, profound virtue).
*Xuán* (玄) means deep, obscure.

A ruler should know
that blessings can be entrusted to,
and thieves can be removed,
bringing the *Qínshǒu* (黔首, common folk) to wealth and longevity—
this is profound and obscure virtue indeed.

Profound virtue is deep, distant, and contrary to things,
only then does it reach the *Dàshùn* (大顺, Great Harmony).

The *Xuándé* (玄德) of the Sage
is deep beyond knowing,
distant beyond perception.
Yet when observing their words,
they seem to oppose the natural order of things,
but when examining their essence,
they align with the people’s path—
only then do they enter the realm of *Zìrán* (自然, spontaneity),
and nothing goes against it;
this is called *Dàshùn* (大顺, Great Harmony).

Yan Junping said:
The discernment of foolishness and cleverness
is without limit,
bringing peace to the heavens and tranquility to the earth,
like the *Yīnfú* (阴福, hidden blessings) of the cosmos,
unseen by the world—
thus it is called *Xuándé* (玄德, profound virtue),
deep beyond measurement,
distant beyond reckoning,
contrary to things,
and none can overcome it.

The *Liezǐ* (《列子》) says:
Emperor Yao governed the world for fifteen years,
and did not know whether the world was governed or not,
did not know whether the ten million myriads
wished to revere him or not.
He asked his attendants,
but they did not know;
he asked the outer court,
but they did not know;
he asked the commoners,
but they did not know.

Yao then dressed plainly and wandered through the bustling streets of Kang (康).
He heard children singing:
*"Establish us, the boiling masses,
none but you are the utmost.
We do not recognize you,
yet we follow the Sage’s Way."*

Yao rejoiced and asked:
*"Who taught you these words?"*
The children replied:
*"We heard them from the nobles."*
Yao asked the nobles:
*"This is an ancient poem."*

Yao returned to his court,
summoned Shun (舜),
and passed the throne to him through abdication.
Shun did not decline and accepted it.
This is called profound virtue,
deep and distant, reaching Great Harmony.

The adept in the Dao
does not act against the few,
their virtue and measure are profound and distant,
like rivers flowing toward the sea,
thus they are likened to the sea,
the king of all rivers.

The sea becomes the king of all rivers
because it excels in humility—
thus it can be the king of all rivers.

The *Kāiyuán Yùshū* (开元御疏) says:
The sea becomes the king of all rivers
because it dwells in the lowest flow.

The *Yì* (《易》) says:
*"The Earth’s Dao transforms fullness into humility,
and flows with modesty—
the Earth’s Dao uses humility,
and all rivers converge and flow toward it.
The Sage uses humility,
and the common folk come willingly without exhaustion."*

Yan Junping said:
The sea’s kingship
is not achieved by accumulating virtue,
piling up benevolence,
or offering favors to win their devotion—
nor is it through exalted rites,
broad deference,
or adorned wisdom to please them—
nor is it by employing unusual tactics,
establishing authority,
or wielding force and displaying might to control them.
Instead, it is through purity and stillness,
dwelling in humility,
waiting in emptiness,
with no action and no desire—
that all rivers come naturally.

The rivers do not come because they hear
the sea’s beauty or are transformed by its virtue—
nor are they bound by constraints,
guided by coercion.
Yet they pierce through gold and stone,
drill into cliffs,
soak into mountains,
and rush toward the sea without return—
this is the form conforming to nature,
the essence aligning with things,
and the natural course of all phenomena.

From this, we see
that humility as the Dao is vast indeed.

A hundred harms cannot injure it,
clever schemes cannot seize it.
It is strong without battle,
mighty without coercion.
Silent and without action,
it engages in mutual exchange with all things.

Valleys are low,
thus they can reach their end without exhaustion;
the sea dwells beneath,
thus it can reign without ceasing.

Therefore, the Sage desires to be above others,
yet speaks with humility.
The Sage refers to those who embody
the humility of the lowest flow—
they who wish to stand above others
must first humble themselves in speech,
knowing fullness invites loss.
Thus, they speak in humility and gentleness,
take the names of solitude and fewness,
and their great virtue grows daily,
their vast undertakings ever firm—
naturally, they are revered and elevated by all.

To lead others,
one must place oneself behind them.
To emerge first among all things,
one must humble oneself in body,
and be gentle in words.
Those who retreat behind others
are cautious in their conduct,
following the Dao,
not presuming to lead—
thus they are allowed by others to take precedence.

Therefore, though they dwell above,
others do not feel burdened;
though they stand before,
others do not feel harmed.
The Sage, when ascending the highest treasure,
occupying the utmost honor,
wearies one body while resting all people,
sparing themselves while enriching others—
yet the people all joyfully revere them,
as though they were light in their governance.
Thus, the people follow willingly,
and who could harm them?

Therefore, the world delights in pushing them forward
without exhaustion.
The ruler who follows the Dao,
even commoners flock to them,
as though drawn by the sun,
or praised like clouds.
As lord, they are never weary—
it is their virtue of humility and sincerity that brings this.

Because they do not contend,
none under heaven can contend with them.
Humility transforms the lower,
and the lower all become humble and retreat.
Thus, the Sage always takes emptiness as their body,
and non-action as their heart—
when heart and form are void,
what can contend with them?

Lu Xisheng said:
The Dao of Heaven
replenishes humility while diminishing fullness,
the Dao of Earth
transforms fullness into flowing humility,
spirits and gods
harm fullness while blessing humility,
and human conduct
abhors fullness while loving humility.»

The virtue of humility,
lowly yet self-nurturing,
thus the rivers and seas take humility as virtue,
and all streams flow toward them.

The Sage takes humility as virtue,
and is thus first in the world’s esteem.
Yet the Sage does not seek to be first—
the world delights in pushing them forward without exhaustion.

Rivers and seas dwell in the lowest flow,
the Sage-King abides in humility,
while the common practice favors strength and arrogance,
deeming passivity as unmanly.
Thus, they are ranked below those who say,
*"The world says my Way is vast—
yet I seem unworthy."*

The world says my Way is vast—
yet I seem unworthy.
*Worthy* means *resembling*.
Laozi said:
*"All under heaven speak of my Way as vast and boundless—
shining yet not dazzling,
abundant virtue like foolishness,
without form or likeness,
as though unworthy."*

Only because it is vast does it seem unworthy.
If it were worthy, long ago it would have diminished.
Only my Way, vast and boundless,
is not trusted by the lowly,
so it is called unworthy.
If it were praised,
long ago others would have extolled it—
why wait until now?
If my Way were praised by others,
how could it truly be vast?

Zhuangzi said:
Laozi told Master Chengqi:
*"The clever, wise, divine, and sagely—
I have long since freed myself from them."*

This is how Laozi has long valued
the unworthy over the praiseworthy.

As the saying goes:
*"Wuzi’s wisdom could be matched,
but his foolishness could not."*
This is close to the truth.

I have three treasures,
to preserve and uphold them.
Though my Way is vast and boundless,
without form or likeness,
there are three practices
to rely upon and hold fast—
truly the supreme treasures for self-cultivation and governance.
*(Referring to what follows.)*

The ancient text reads:
*"Hold them fast and treasure them."*
If the world follows these three practices as precious,
clinging to them without abandon,
what cannot they achieve?

The *He Shang Gong* version reads:
*"Hold them fast and preserve them."*

First: *compassion*,
second: *frugality*,
third: *not daring to lead the world.*

The *Kaiyuan Imperial Commentary* says:
To embody *ren* and act sincerely,
to love and nurture the people—this is *compassion*.
To use sparingly and enrich others,
without depletion—this is *frugality*.
Not to initiate actions,
to harmonize without leading—
this is *not daring to lead the world.*

Because of *compassion*, one is courageous.
Because of *frugality*, one is expansive.
Because one does not dare lead the world,
one can perfect tools and extend influence.

The Sage practices *compassion* as conduct,
courageous in aiding others—
the virtuous must be fearless.
The frugal in use
must be expansive in relief—
this is *"moderate use and love for people."*
Not daring to lead the world,
one can perfect tools and extend influence.

The *Yijing* says:
*"Seeing the dragon without head is auspicious."*
This is what is meant by
*"the Great Way seems unworthy."*

Li Yue said:
From the lowly, one ascends to the high—
thus one attains lordship over the world.

Now, abandoning *compassion* for *courage*,
abandoning *frugality* for *expansiveness*,

Abandoning the rear for the front—this is death.

Yet the world today does otherwise. Those deemed "worthy" are in fact contrary to the Way.

All abandon *compassion* and *virtue*, seeking instead *courage* and *righteousness*, reckless with *qi* and light in death, to ensnare others—this is the extreme peril of overreaching.

Unable to love and practice frugality, they instead indulge in extravagance, greedily seeking broad expenditure, plundering until resources are exhausted. They must inevitably harm finances and the people, thus inviting the calamity of scarcity.

Then, they adorn themselves with cleverness to astonish the ignorant, refine their conduct to expose filth, delight in leading others, and shame being last. Advancing without humility, retreating without restraint, their actions are bold yet unchecked—this is rebellion against the sovereign.

These three ways are not the enduring arts of the Great Way; they lead only to the ruin of family and self. Hence, it is said: *this is death.*

Now, *compassion*—when wielded in war, it brings victory; when held in defense, it secures strength.

Among the Three Treasures, *compassion* is most precious, hence its partial concealment as a flaw.

When a sovereign employs *compassion*, nurturing soldiers as one’s own, they become invincible beneath heaven.

For *compassionate* armies entering enemy lands do not trample crops, dig up graves, ravage stores, or burn dwellings. Instead, people welcome them as孝子 (filial sons) behold their benevolent parents. Those who surrender come like arrows loosed into deep valleys.

Thus, why must battles be fought in open fields before victory is won, or defenses fortified with gates and drums? Heaven itself will aid them, shielding with *compassion*.

For Heaven’s *Dao* rewards the virtuous and punishes the licentious. The virtuous are naturally blessed by Heaven, facing no adversity.

The *Classics* say:
*"Heaven’s *Dao* favors none—it aids only the virtuous."*

Thus Heaven aids them because their sovereigns and generals employ *compassion* and *virtue* to protect soldiers and people.

The Great Way seems unworthy, yet *compassion* and frugality appear unmartial—hence the next step: *the virtuous warrior is unmartial.*

The *virtuous warrior*—one who embodies *compassion* and *virtue*, punishes tyranny, relieves suffering, yet does not oppress the weak with brute force.

The skilled warrior does not rage in battle.
For those who master the battlefield, though armed, use force only when unavoidable. In confronting the enemy, they do not delight in slaughter. They treat war with the solemnity of mourning rites—how could they revel in anger and war?

The skilled victor does not contend.
For the skilled victor, triumph lies in *virtue*, not conflict. Armies conquer through harmony; harmony brings victory. They do not act from self-interest but follow the *Dao* to seize opportunity.

Like Zhu Zhonglian’s whistling or Xiong Yiliang’s ball-playing—these are the ways of the skilled victor.

The skilled ruler serves beneath others.
The skilled ruler employs humility, not force; delights in guiding the people, who forget their labor. Thus, using ministers as advisors, they are like limbs to the body; employing generals, they are obeyed with reverence, their chariots led by the sovereign’s hand. They lead with *virtue* before demanding strength.

This is the *virtue* of non-contention, the *virtue* of harnessing others’ strength, the *virtue* of aligning with Heaven—ancient perfection.

Those who excel as warriors, in battle, in victory, and in ruling—ever gentle, non-contending, humble in self-cultivation—exhaust the world’s potential and fully employ all. Is this not the *virtue* of non-contention, the strength of others?

If they lead with harmony, we respond in kind—like Heaven’s boundless grace arising from no favor, or the seasons moving without command. Thus they align with Heaven, governing in its place—this is the ultimate of ancient *Dao*.

The *virtue* of non-contention is the law of warfare, hence the next step: *the principles of warfare.*

**Principles of Warfare:**
Laozi lamented that the world’s armies take delight in war, so ancient military strategies are cited to warn against this.

I dare not be the aggressor, only the defender;
I dare not advance an inch, only retreat a foot.

Warfare’s aggressor is the attacker; the defender responds. The Sage’s army acts only when forced, thus always responding—always the defender.

Advancing little, retreating much—this is the art of deep strategy against superior foes.

Though enemies come, I rely on *Dao* rather than formation, thus avoiding conflict.

Again, the laws of deploying troops: the aggressor schemes through mobilization; the defender focuses on response. Though advances are few, they edge toward annihilation; though retreats are many, they turn toward survival. Thus, one must not dare to be the aggressor but the defender, not dare to advance an inch but retreat a foot—just as courage in daring leads to slaughter, while courage in restraint brings life.

This is called:
*"Marching without marching,
Repelling without arms,
Confronting without enemies,
Wielding without weapons."*

For the purpose of warfare is to halt the enemy, valuing non-contention. Though halting them, one harbors no intent to kill.

Since there is no killing intent, my army merely upholds *righteousness* in defense—thus *"marching without marching."*

Again, if generals must raise arms to charge first, it is as if they have no arms to raise—such is their aversion to killing and reverence for *compassion.*

Even if a tyrannical aggressor stirs rebellion, I respond with *cultural virtue* and display *righteous* armies. They will retreat upon hearing *righteousness*, naturally leaving no enemy. Though the threat of retaliation remains, there is no enemy to retaliate against—hence *"confronting without enemies."*

With the enemy withdrawn, arms are sheathed. Though the ritual of bearing weapons remains, there are no weapons to wield—hence *"wielding without weapons."*

No greater calamity exists than underestimating the enemy—underestimating the enemy risks losing one’s *Treasure.*

*Treasure* refers to life and position.

Underestimating the enemy means delighting in external warfare while neglecting internal defenses. Delighting in external warfare may yield victory or defeat, but neglecting internal defenses ensures ruin.

Though the Sage rules supreme beneath heaven, considering the times of order and chaos, enemies are many.

What then is meant by this?

The *Classic of Documents* states:
*"Caress me, and I will follow; oppress me, and I will become your enemy."*

If this is so, then even a single state or kingdom is my enemy, and even a single village or household is my enemy.

Thus, the sovereign who does not neglect even the lowliest minister will win the affection of all under heaven.
The noble who does not humiliate the widowed and orphaned will win the people’s affection.
The virtuous scholar who does not neglect self-cultivation will bring divine delight and heavenly harmony.

Only then can one safeguard what is most precious.

What does it mean to say *"near to losing my Treasure"*?

I am naturally merciful and do not delight in killing. If forced to confront an enemy, it is not my true intent. Since it is not my true intent, it is already a loss. Though the loss is not yet great, it is *"near to losing."*

Thus, when armies clash, the first to strike is victorious.
*"Ant"* (抗) means *"to raise."* When two states raise their armies against each other, the side that acts first prevails.

*"Pity"* (哀) is the expression of sincere mercy from the heart, arising from the ruler’s virtue. If the ruler retains a merciful heart, does not neglect the rites of governance, and the ministers below fulfill their loyalty, exhausting their duty to serve—then where can they fail to triumph?

Warfare warns against underestimating the enemy, but its words are easily understood. Therefore, I follow with my own teachings, which are even easier to understand and even easier to practice.

The *Classic* says:
*"Act without action."*
*"Know the world without leaving your door; know the Way without looking through a window."*
*"Practice the teaching of the unsaid."*

All these are the Way of *Wuwei*—simple and effortless within its proper scope. Though the words are not elaborate and the practice requires no labor, they are easy to know and easy to do.

Yet none under heaven can know them, none can practice them.

Those who cannot know them are the lesser scholars. The lesser scholars delight in petty disputes, cling to superficial arguments, and are easily confused by *having-for-nothing* (有为). They favor attacking heterodox views, unable to purge their minds, still their thoughts, or guard the One. They remain in darkness, unaware of the Way of *Wu*—vague and unable to act.

There is a principle in speech, a master in affairs. This explains why the easy-to-know and easy-to-do is so seldom attained.

*"Principle"* (宗) is the root, the sovereign. Though the hundred schools speak in different ways, they all converge on *li* (理, principle). Those who grasp *li* forget the words, so the ultimate principle of speech is *not-speaking*.

All affairs, though diverse in their paths, ultimately unite in *achievement*. When achievement is complete, the affairs are abandoned—thus, the master of affairs is *non-action*.

This is teaching through *non-speech* and *non-action*—how could it not be easy to know and easy to do?

Yan Junping said:
The Sage’s words root in *naturalness*, ancestral in *divine clarity*. They dwell in the inversion between speech and silence, subtle and wondrous, returning to *naturalness*. Their clarity is as if unseen, their hearing as if unheard—open yet seemingly blocked, penetrating yet seemingly exhausted. Their practice begins in *naturalness*, flows into the divine, and dwells in harmony between *action* and *inaction*. Pure, still, yielding, and weak, their movements are delicate and refined, simple and retiring, returning to *Wuwei*.

Only because they are without knowledge are they unknown to others.

The lesser scholars, blind in their ignorance, delight in parsing words and lost in debates over *solid-white* and *same-different*. Hearing the Dao of *non-speech* and *non-action*, they scoff and laugh in disbelief, deeming my Way great yet calling it unworthy.

Yan Junping said:
It is not that my Way is small or unworthy of knowledge, nor that my affairs are trivial or unworthy of action. Nor is it that the world delights in ruin, hates completion, loves calamity, and shuns rest—refusing to follow me.

Human nature opposes mine, desires conflict with mine. The wise are ensnared by desires, drowning in what they hear their whole lives, their *qi* and spirit unsettled, their hearts and intentions misaligned.

Few know me, thus I am honored.

The words of the ultimate Dao have a principle and a master—only the enlightened know them, hence their rarity and sparseness.

Those who attain this Dao are honored by all things, hence it is said, *"those who follow me are honored."*

The *Kaiyuan* edition reads: *"those who follow my Way are honored."*

Yan Junping said:
Thus, their clarity is not seen, their hearing not heard. Deep words return to receive slander, great deeds retreat to earn blame. The lone-seer’s clarity is useless in the world, the lone-hearer’s wisdom brings shame among the people. Those who align with divine clarity do not conform to convention, those whose achievements match the heavens and earth do not please the masses.

Thus, the Sage wears coarse cloth while harboring jade.
*"Coarse cloth"* (褐) is the garment of the lowly. Jade is pure and moist, likened to the virtue of the gentleman.

The Sage internally treasures virtue and power—this is likened to harboring jade. Externally, he lacks ornamentation—this is likened to wearing coarse cloth.

Thus, though his inner brilliance is vast, his outer appearance seems foolish and dim. Pearls are hidden in oysters, jade nestled in stone—none under heaven can know it, hence my Way is honored.

Since common folk presumptuously claim to know the Sage’s hidden knowledge, I follow with *"knowing the unknown."*

*"Knowing the unknown"* is virtue’s height.
*"Not knowing the known"* is a flaw.

The Sage, pure in *qi* and refined in nature, internally harbors true knowledge. All things are spontaneously understood, yet though capable of knowing, he does not boast in it—this is the highest virtue.

The middle and lesser scholars, coarse in *qi* and stubborn in nature, internally brimming with cunning, yet in their affairs they are arrogant and ignorant. Though they do not truly know, they forcefully argue, embellishing their words to feign knowledge—this is the flaw of virtue.

Zhuangzi said:
*"The unknown is profound; the known is shallow. The unknown is within; the known is without. This is what is called: ‘The knower does not speak; the speaker does not know.’"*

Only because of the sickness of sickness is there no sickness.

The Sage’s true knowledge and wondrous root penetrate the dim and obscure, thus he possesses the six penetrations and four openings, transcending above and below without fixed form. Tranquil and without burden, he yet constantly suffers.

The common folk mistakenly cling to the sickness of forced knowledge, often leading them into fatal traps from which they do not return—truly a matter for lamentation.

This reveals the Sage’s compassionate heart, alone able to cure afflictions.

The common folk suffer from this sickness of forced knowledge, yet how could they harm the Sage’s virtue? Thus, true knowledge is without affliction, while forced knowledge brings sickness.

The Sage is not afflicted because he cures affliction—this is why he is not afflicted.

The Sage alone knows the virtue of ignorance and the mechanism of the Dao, dimming his sharpness and abandoning his cleverness, truly free from worldly afflictions.

Compassionate and benevolent, he can cure the afflictions of all beings because he himself is without affliction.

If he had afflictions, how could he cure the afflictions of all beings?

Zhuangzi said:
*"No one can be weighed down by flowing water, yet people are weighed down by stagnant water. Only the still can still all things; receiving its mandate from the Earth, only the pine stands alone. Rectifying the winter and summer, its verdant hues endure; receiving its mandate from Heaven, only Shun stands alone. Rectifying his own life to rectify all lives, this is first rectifying oneself before rectifying others—just as the Sage does not suffer because he cures affliction."*

Forced knowledge loses the Dao, provoking calamity. When calamity comes yet the people do not fear, then the greatest fear arrives.

If the people do not fear the fear of Heaven or the fear of the state, then the greatest fear arrives.

The greatest fear is the omen of death.

The virtuous fear Heaven’s mandate, fear great men, and fear the words of the Sage.

For man, to establish oneself, fear must be the foundation. If one does not fear even small evils, they accumulate until the greatest fear arrives—inevitable and inescapable.

Yan Junping said:
*"When the greatest fear has arrived, one begins to do good. At this time, virtue and power cannot save, Heaven and Earth cannot resolve—this is not Heaven’s fault."*

Those who are high and secure, bearing the fear yet wielding power, forget care and lose fear, seeking nothing from themselves.

Thus, those who care for their own bodies do not fear others, those who fear themselves are not controlled by others, those who are cautious in small matters do not dread great ones, and those who restrain themselves near do not regret their distance.

Do not defile where you dwell.
*Ancient reading*: "狎" (*xiá*), meaning "habitual."
*Where you dwell*: refers to one’s conduct.
The cautious and fearful must choose good neighbors, avoid corrupt companions, maintain purity in self-guarding, and hold themselves in humility—then calamity cannot touch them. Their bodies remain whole, their spirits sovereign; this is the depth of fear and caution.

Do not narrow where you dwell.
*Kaiyuan Imperial Commentary*: "狭" (*xiá*), meaning to broaden one’s dwelling, not to forcefully confine oneself. It lies in harmonizing light and accommodating all, not clashing with things.

*Ancient reading*: "The dwelling of the spirit is the heart."
Man should forget emotions and abandon desires, softening his breast to let the spirit’s chambers rest, so that the spirit may dwell in the heart—then life is preserved.

Do not corrupt where you are born.
*Ancient reading*: "驮" (*tuó*), meaning "evil."
The form given by the Dao is what is meant by "where you are born."
Man should not corrupt the evil of his Dao; he should serve diligently, revere the great, fear the Dao and fear Heaven—then he can eternally preserve his primordial blessing.

If he indulges his desires, corrupts his Dao, and despises virtue, calamity will follow swiftly.

*Ancient reading*: "The spirit born from the body."
Man lives through the spirit; "where you are born" refers to the spirit.
The spirit, clear and luminous, prefers emptiness and stillness. If man washes his heart and calms his thoughts, the spirit returns of itself.
If he indulges in desires and debases his spirit, scheming to indulge his nature, then the spirit’s *qi* scatters and dissipates, and life is lost.

Thus, he is exhorted to not corrupt the spirit he was born with, to preserve the enduring Dao.

Only because one does not resist, is one not silent.

Only those who fear the Dao and fear Heaven are singularly precise and unified. In haste or leisure, they adhere to this principle—how could they have a heart for evil or laziness? Thus, the Dao’s hidden blessings descend upon them, granting a hundred auspicious signs. This is why they mutually cherish and protect, never exposing evil.

*Kaiyuan Imperial Commentary*: "Singularly precise and unified" are words from *The Book of Documents—Great Yu’s Model* (《尚书·大禹谟》), where Shun commands Yu to take his place.

Man’s heart is fraught with peril, the Dao’s heart is subtle—peril makes it hard to rest, subtlety makes it hard to discern. Only through singular precision and unity can one steadfastly grasp its center.

Thus, the Sage knows himself without revealing himself.
The Sage first preserves within himself, then within others. Examining his own heart’s origin, he knows his own virtues and vices.
Through cleansing and purging, he ensures no dust or filth enters his gates—this is called self-knowledge.
Having understood the Dao’s profound silence, he acts with movement day and night, not boasting of his talents or abilities, nor being confused by things, fearing to deviate from principle or act improperly—like those who do not fear the greatest fear.

He loves himself but does not exalt himself.
The Sage cherishes his own body, knowing it is the Dao’s creation. He does not indulge his appetites, nor does he wallow in evil and idleness, harming the utmost principle or diminishing his form and spirit—this is called self-love.
If one instead covets rich flavors and fine clothes, indulges in beauty and music, finds comfort in his nature, and delights his ears and eyes, exalting himself while despising others—this is defiling where he dwells and failing to maintain purity and stillness. How could this be one who fears and is cautious in the minutest matters?

Thus, he abandons that and takes this.
If one abandons the arrogant self-exaltation and takes the fear of the greatest fear—obtaining the principle of revering the Dao and serving Heaven without growing weary of others—then external punishments fade, and life’s Dao is internally sufficient.

He does not do evil for the sake of doing evil, nor seek courage for the sake of killing—
*Daoist True Classic: Treasury of Virtue and Power, Chapter Nine, "Perfection of the Dao"*
*Daoist True Classic: Treasury of Virtue and Power, Chapter Ten, "Refinement of the Dao"*

**Daoist True Classic: Treasury of Virtue and Power, Chapter Eleven, "Discernment of Courage and Boldness"**

Boldness in daring leads to killing;
Courage in restraint leads to life.
Firm resolve is courage,
Swift decisiveness is boldness.
The resolute and unyielding—
Fearless of threats, unflinching in action,
Relying on their ruthless obstinacy,
They strike and slaughter without hesitation—
Thus, boldness in daring leads to killing.

Yet the virtuous Daoist,
Discerning retreat and advance,
Moderate and yielding,
Soft and harmonious,
Dare not act recklessly,
Spare life and avoid harm—
Thus, courage in restraint leads to life.

To know these two paths—
One brings benefit, the other harm—
What Heaven abhors, who truly understands?
Hence, even the Sage finds this difficult.

These two refer to daring and restraint,
To killing and to life.

The greatest virtue of Heaven and Earth is *generation*.
The Sage takes compassion as substance,
Yet does not delight in killing.

Death is the bitterest poison to humanity;
All living beings abhor it.
Boldness in decisive action is the path of killing.
Courage in mercy and benevolence is the path of life.

If one clings rigidly to this principle without change,
They have yet to grasp Heaven and Earth’s authority over life and death.
If today’s actions bring benefit or harm,
It is because the choice between killing and life remains undetermined.

When one commits evil in plain sight,
Humans may punish them.
When one commits evil in secret,
Ghosts may slay them.
Even the Sage’s mercy,
Heaven’s benevolence—
Cannot pity or save them.
Yet if pardoned and spared,
Retribution’s calamity will surely follow.

There are those who *should* be killed and are killed,
Those who *should* live and are spared,
Those who *should* be killed but must not be,
Those who *should* live but must not be,
Those whose sparing brings misfortune,
Those whose killing brings blessing—
How so?

Yet what Heaven abhors,
Who truly knows its reason?
Unless one embodies the True Transformation,
How could one know the origins of calamity and blessing,
The roots of benefit and harm?
Even the Sage’s wisdom finds boldness and daring difficult—
How much more so for those who dare to act by private will?

Thus, it remains difficult.

**Yan Junping** said:
These two functions—
Courage and boldness, equally matched—
Yet their strategies diverge;
One strikes the body, the other defies it;
They oppose Heaven’s intent, contradict Earth’s will;
They unleash their passions, indulge their desires—
One may turn a thousand chariots into captives,
Another may rise from a commoner to king.
Thus, some are born to bring fortune,
Others to bring ruin;
Some are killed to bring blessing,
Others to bring disaster.
These two paths are profound—
None can fully fathom them.

The Dao of Heaven
Conquers without striving,
Naturally balanced, it does not resist the myriad things,
Yet the myriad things thrive on their own.
How could it compete with humans over daring or restraint,
Over killing or life?

Yet those who submit to it
Do not contend with things,
Yet conquer them well—
This is the courage and boldness of the uninjured field.

Silent yet responsive,
Heaven speaks not—
Yet the seasons march, the myriad things flourish,
And the rewards for virtue, the punishments for licentiousness,
Are never mistaken.
Unsummoned, they come.
Heaven’s Dao is vast and distant,
With no spoken teachings—
It never calls the myriad things,
Yet all face the sun and shun the shade,
Spring brings birth, autumn brings harvest—
This is the natural order of yin and yang’s life and death.
Mysterious yet wise in counsel.
The *Kaiyuan* and *He Shang Gong* versions read *"tanran"* (谭然).
Yan Junping’s version reads *"mò"* (默).
Wang Bi’s version reads *"tǎn"* (坦).

The Dao of Heaven is tranquil and silent, without favor or bias,
Its justice is absolute and unerring.
To those who act with virtue, it returns blessings of fortune,
To those who act with wickedness, it returns calamities of misfortune.
In its power over human affairs—life and death’s decree,
The patterns of *xiàngwěi* and celestial movements—
It never falters, never fails.
Is this not the wisdom of the Great Planner?

The Heavenly Net is vast and wide,
Though sparse, it never lets slip.
Stretched across the boundless void, it is called the Heavenly Net.
Its breadth and expanse are called *huánghuáng*—this is *liàn*.
Its reach into darkness and light, with no escape, is called *bù shī*.
Thus, the bold are slain,
Yet the common folk do not fear death—
Hence, the saying: *"the common folk do not fear death."*

For there are two meanings to "not fearing death":
The enlightened know its natural order and do not fear it.
The foolish lose sight of its natural order and yet do not fear it.

By Heaven’s nature, humans are born in stillness,
Yet delight in life and dread death—this is human nature.
They till the fields and eat,
They weave and wear,
They rest in their dwellings,
They take joy in their labors,
They nurture the living and bury the dead—
These are the customs of the world.

When the people abide by their natural ways,
And officials uphold their proper laws,
Without wrong or excess,
They meet their natural deaths.
If the people’s lives and deaths follow their natural course,
Then why should they fear it?

But when virtue declines,
And rulers cannot refrain from action,
Their nets and laws become dense and strict,
The people barely survive.
Theft and deceit arise,
They cheat the hungry and sow chaos.
The small fear trembles within them,
The great fear paralyzes them,
And they forget death.
How can severe punishments and cruel officials
Instill terror in the common folk?
The ancient text reads: *"How can one make the people fear death?"*

If the people were to fear death,
And yet still commit crimes,
I would seize and execute them—
Who would dare?

For fear of death also has two meanings:
Those who cherish life and act with caution
Fear Heaven’s judgment and seek virtue.
Those who commit evil and crave ease
Fear punishment and feign obedience to the law.

Moreover, by nature, humans
Abandon their burdens and walk freely,
Or lie at ease, drumming their bellies in leisure.
Lost in confusion, they know not of aging’s approach—
These are the people who follow the natural order.

Now, with laws and decrees multiplying,
Every movement risks death,
Thus the people are made to fear death.
When the people fear death,
They cling to life’s fleeting comforts,
And nurture treacherous, deceitful schemes
To evade the laws.
When deceit flourishes and calamities arise,
The officials seize and slaughter,
Severing the roots of evil—
Who would dare transgress?

The natural order executes those who execute.
He Shang Gong says:
*"Those who execute"* refer to Heaven’s high and distant gaze,
Judging the sins of those below.
The Heavenly Net is vast and wide—
It speaks of Heaven’s clear discernment, nothing unseen.
Why need there be harsh punishments and strict laws?

Du Guangting says:
*"Those who execute"* are the sovereign’s agents,
Great in nurturing life and loving the people.
In the *Nan Gong Dan Lu* scrolls, rewards are given for virtue,
And in the *Bei Guan Hei Lu* records, crimes are punished.
Heaven and Earth, with all their gods,
Oversee the deeds of good and evil,
To chastise and encourage,
So that evil may be abandoned and virtue cultivated.
Thus, those with merit are granted longevity,
Those with guilt have their years cut short,
No fraction lost—
Like the *Yángguān* officials in their meticulous reckoning.
Heaven has the *Sì Sī* stars that govern fate,
Positioned between *Xū* and *Wēi*,
Overseeing merit, demerit, lifespan—
This is Heaven’s execution of judgment.
It examines crimes and blessings obliquely,
So that all may know to cultivate virtue and avoid evil.

The ruler teaches with virtue,
And moves with compassion and forgiveness.
As for those who do not act virtuously,
Heaven’s executioners will slay them themselves.
The Heavenly Net is vast and forgiving,
Though sparse, it does not let slip.
To defy Heaven and reverse the Dao—
Where can one flee punishment?

To execute in Heaven’s place
Is like a clumsy carpenter taking the master’s tools.
To execute in Heaven’s place,
To wield judgment and rely solely on punishments,
To usurp the authority of *Zàohuà* over life and death—
This is like a clumsy man replacing a skilled artisan.

To take the master’s tools,
Few are those who do not harm their hands.
To replace a master artisan with a clumsy one,
Not only will the wood be ruined and the work spoiled,
But also hands will be injured and fingers broken.
This illustrates how rulers,
By usurping *Zàohuà*’s authority over life and death,
And recklessly applying their laws and punishments,
Not only harm the common folk,
But also sever the harmonious *héqì*.

For Heaven’s Dao is high and bright,
While human understanding is narrow and dim.
To have a ruler with limited wisdom
Usurp the place of Heaven’s boundless clarity,
And mislead the people in their natural order—
This is to lose the governance of nature.

The people do not fear death,
But only hunger.
Hence, the next phrase: *"the people’s hunger."*
The people’s hunger comes from their rulers’ excessive taxes—
Thus they starve.

The people are the foundation of the state.
When governance is burdensome, taxes grow heavy,
And the people’s poverty weakens the state’s roots.
Therefore, the ruler depends on the people for sustenance,
And the people rely on the ruler for stability.
If taxes are taken in excess, both ruler and people starve.
If the people are stripped bare while the ruler grows rich,
Both suffer ruin.

Du Guangting says:
*"The laws and decrees are clearly established in antiquity.
‘Food’ refers to the ruler’s provisions,
‘Taxes’ to the state’s revenues."*

「Hence, the tax of one-tenth (*shíyī zhī shuì*) must be light in its implementation. Heavy taxes impoverish the people, while light taxes ensure their sufficiency. When the people are well-provided, the state flourishes; when they are impoverished, the state is in peril. The principle lies in moderation—laws should not be too harsh. Thus, when rulers cast penal codes with severity, they provoke resentment; when they impose burdensome levies, the land grows poor. The Marquis of Qi (*Qī Hóu*) perished through excessive demands, while the Duke of Tian (*Tián Shì*) became a hegemon through generous grants. Let the enlightened ruler take heed of these lessons.

The people are difficult to govern because their rulers act excessively—thus they are difficult to govern. Excessive action leads to burdensome governance; non-action brings simplicity. Simplicity is easy to follow, while complexity is hard to govern. When the ruler indulges in fine jade and lavish meals, the common folk delight in coarse grains and husks. Strict nets of law and harsh decrees make governance difficult. The people grow indifferent to death because their pursuit of life is so desperate—thus they become reckless with death. When governance is oppressive, taxes and levies are heavy, and the people lose their means of livelihood, they then transgress laws and break prohibitions, readily facing death. This is because their desperate search for sustenance leads them to disregard danger, even to wade through water and fire without fear or to confront blades without hesitation—hence, they are said to treat death lightly.

Only those who do not cling to life are truly wiser than those who do. Those who greedily chase life and profit are like sheep running to the slaughter, hastening their own demise. Yet there are those who outwardly forget life’s concerns: they endure hardship without humiliation, eat humble fare yet find it sweet, and though the world’s affairs may be tumultuous, they remain tranquil and at ease. This is how they differ from those who cling to life.

Clinging to life is both a blessing and a curse—it nourishes life, yet also becomes a self-inflicted burden. Those who value life excessively are driven by bodily comfort, craving for rich flavors, fine garments, and pleasing colors; they seek harmonious sounds, and if denied, they grow anxious and fearful, even to the point of blaming others and recklessly inviting death. When the people are hungry, their spirits scatter and they grow indifferent to death; when they are well-fed, their bodies and minds are whole and they cherish life—thus the next phrase: *"the people’s life is soft and frail."*

The people’s life is soft and frail, yet their death is firm and resolute. When alive, the people absorb the *yuánhé* Dao’s *qi* and embrace the *zhēnyī* essence’s vitality, their bodies and spirits whole—thus their limbs are soft and frail. But when they die, the *yuánhé* *qi* disperses, the *zhēnyī* essence is exhausted, their forms decay and their spirits vanish—thus their limbs become firm and rigid.

All things, grasses and woods alike, are soft and pliable when alive, yet dry and brittle when dead. When alive, they are nourished by the *qi* of heaven and earth flowing within, and by the *yīnyáng* fluids moistening without—thus their spring shoots are tender and can be bound, their summer leaves flourish and can be woven, a display of their softness. When they die, the *qi* of heaven and earth dissipates within, and the *yīnyáng* fluids wither without—thus their autumn fruits turn crimson and fall, their winter branches wither and can be snapped, a sign of their brittleness.

The ancient sage understood this principle well—this is the act of the ruthless. The ruthless see life and death as mere gathering and scattering of *qi*. The enlightened understand life and death as the preservation or loss of Dao. Thus, the firm and resolute are the followers of death, while the soft and frail are the followers of life.

The *Kāiyuán* commentary states: *"Grasses and woods are soft and pliable when alive, firm and brittle when dead. Thus, for those who act with firmness and rigidity, they are the followers of death; for those who act with softness and frailty, they are the followers of life."*

Yán Jūn Píng said: *"Where the yang *qi* resides, even wood can be woven and grass can be bound. Where the yang *qi* departs, water can freeze and ice can shatter. Thus, the enlightened yang *qi* is the root of life, and softness and frailty are the medicine for all things. Softness and frailty are the means of gentle growth, while the enlightened yang *qi* is what sustains them. All things follow the yang *qi* in their softness and frailty, so the firm and rigid are the true likeness of death, while the soft and pliable are the true likeness of life. Hence, when armies are strong, they cannot prevail."*

Yù Zhēn said: *"War is an instrument of violence and a tool of conflict, a realm where one encounters the enemy in battle. When armies are strong, the ruler becomes complacent while the generals grow arrogant; when generals are arrogant, the soldiers grow reckless. A complacent ruler faces arrogant generals, while arrogant generals confront reckless soldiers—defeat is inevitable before victory can even be pursued. Thus, Jié and Zhòu, despite their armies of a million each, were overthrown across the four seas; Qīn Shǐhuáng, despite his unified empire, lost all nine regions; Xiàng Yǔ rose as hegemon yet perished swiftly; Wáng Mǎng usurped the throne and was destroyed in turn. Fú Jiān met disaster at the Huái River, while Suí Yáng was shattered at the Chu Palace—these rulers all commanded armies numbering in the tens of millions, or even hundreds of millions, yet none could rely on their strength to secure victory. These are clear proofs that strong armies do not prevail."*

Moreover, conquering the enemy is not difficult; maintaining victory is the challenge. Only the ruler who follows the Dao can hold onto victory. The defeats of these past rulers all stemmed from their inability to maintain victory.

Wood is strong in its trunk and roots, yet its branches and leaves are soft and pliable, bearing fruit above. The *Kāiyuán* commentary states: *"Wood is inherently strong and sturdy, so its roots and trunk remain firm and low, while its branches and leaves, soft and weak, bear fruit above. This is because the soft and pliable are positioned above, while the strong and rigid remain below."*
