The strong and sturdy are at the base, while the soft and pliable are above. Wood’s strong trunk and roots always reside below, its soft branches and tender leaves always above. If wood behaves thus, how much more so for humans, for nations! When the vital *qi* of life persists, the people are soft and frail—this is the Dao of humility. Heaven’s Dao favors humility, hence the next phrase: *"the Dao of Heaven."*

The Dao of Heaven is like drawing a bow. The *Kāiyuán* commentary states: *"Heaven’s Dao is vast and lofty; no analogy can clarify it better than the method of drawing a bow, which reveals Heaven’s virtue in action. The high must be lowered, the low must be raised; the excessive must be diminished, the deficient must be supplemented."* The *Kāiyuán* commentary further explains: *"The bow’s purpose is to combine materials and set its form, ensuring that its slackness and tension are balanced, its high and low adjusted—this is the standard for shooting arrows."*

「Adjusting the excessive and supplementing the deficient is for determining the distance of the shot—whether the arrow will hit its mark or fall short. Thus, it achieves its target, and the discussion returns to the Dao of Heaven. The *Yì* [《易》] states: *"The Dao of Heaven is established in the interplay of yin and yang. Yang governs ascent, yin governs descent; when yang ascends to its utmost in Heaven, it descends; when yin descends to its utmost in Earth, it ascends. This is the symbol of restraining the high and elevating the low, as in drawing a bow."* The yin-yang cycles of Heaven’s Dao—seven interludes of fullness and emptiness—adjust the excess of the day and supplement the deficiency of the moon. The sun’s heat and moon’s chill, one advancing and one retreating, thus complete the year’s work.

A wise ruler should follow Heaven’s Dao: restraining the strong and aiding the weak, diminishing excess and augmenting deficiency. Hence, the emphasis on *kuī* [亏, deficiency] and *yíng* [盈, fullness], on *jiàn* [谦, humility], to ensure that all measures are balanced and applied with fairness.

Yán Jūn Píng [严君平] remarked: *"When craftsmen make a bow, they ensure neither over-tightness nor slackness, neither excessive compression nor tension. They shape it with compass and square, guide it with plumb line. If the string is too high and taut, they loosen and relax it; if too low and slack, they gather and raise it. Where there is excess, they trim and diminish; where there is deficiency, they reinforce and add. The string’s tension and the bow’s body harmonize, the upper and lower parts balance, equanimity as their core, accord as their norm—thus the bow can measure weight and the arrow can fly true."* To restrain the high and elevate the low, to diminish the great and augment the small, is the Dao of Heaven and Earth.

Heaven’s Dao supplements the deficient and diminishes the excessive, but human affairs do the opposite: diminishing the deficient to serve the excessive. The *Kāiyuán* commentary [开元御疏] states: *"This clarifies that human affairs cannot match Heaven’s Dao of augmentation and diminution, but instead favor the many at the expense of the few."* In the *Yì* [《易》], when one *kuī* [亏, diminishes] the lower and augments the upper, it is called *kuī* [损]. When one diminishes the upper and augments the lower, it is called *yì* [益], and the lower is taken as the foundation. Diminishing the lower while augmenting the upper—its Dao proceeds upward—thus in the *Tài* hexagram [泰卦], when one diminishes the lower and augments the upper, the transformation yields *kuī* [损]. Diminishing the upper while augmenting the lower—the people rejoice without bound—thus in the *Pǐ* hexagram [否卦], when one diminishes the upper and augments the lower, the transformation yields *yì* [益]. This is the method by which the Sage established the hexagrams and observed their symbols.

Who, then, could withhold excess to serve all under Heaven? Even those who claim to follow the Dao.

This is a rhetorical question, elaborating its meaning. The question asks: *"Who can align with Heaven’s Dao of descending to aid the people, sacrificing their own excess of rank and wealth to provide for the poor and needy of all under Heaven?"* The answer states: *"Only the Daoist sage, the virtuous ruler, and the wise philosopher can do so."* The term *"deliver"* [奉] emphasizes that the Sage-ruler, though elevated, never forgets the lowly—just as the humble serve the exalted without claiming superiority. Thus, the Sage acts without arrogance, achieves without clinging, and does not desire to be called virtuous.

The Sage is *yuán tōng* [圆通, omniscient and adaptable], using the Way as wisdom and adapting to circumstances as their heart. They order all things without claiming righteousness, nourish all ages without claiming benevolence, endure from ancient times without claiming longevity, and shape the myriad forms of existence without claiming cleverness. How could they boast of their own deeds, pride themselves on their virtues, or, upon completing their work, insist on retaining their position? Only by not clinging and not claiming can they lead the humble like the master of the *wéi* [韦, coarse] materials.

Heaven’s Dao augments the few and diminishes the excess, restraining the high and elevating the low—only the Daoist sage can embody this principle of humility. Hence, the next point: *"Under Heaven, nothing is as soft and weak as water."* Though water is the softest and weakest of all things, it can overcome the hardest and strongest—nothing can replace it. Among all things under Heaven, water is the most soft and weak, yet it pierces metal and stone, conquering the hard and strong with no rival.

Again, consider humanity: water can be blocked by dams, directed by channels, contained by dikes, and diverted by obstacles—high, low, perilous, or muddy, it flows in countless ways. Yet because of its soft and weak nature, it remains unaltered in its essence. Thus, softness overcomes hardness, and weakness overcomes strength—all under Heaven know this, yet few can practice it. Everyone knows that water extinguishes fire, yin subdues yang, the tongue is soft yet preserves, while the teeth are hard yet break—these are common truths. Yet people follow their own desires and cannot embody the Dao of softness and weakness. Hence, Lǎozi [老子] sighed deeply, and he cited the Sage’s words to illustrate the practice of softness and weakness.

Therefore, the Sage said: *"To bear the defilement of the state is to be called the Lord of the Soil and Grain."* The Sage’s words—whether the remnants of the *Sān Bēn* [三坟] or Lǎozi’s humble phrasing—teach that a ruler can accept the blame of the state, taking upon themselves all the faults of the people. If one person bears the guilt, the myriad cannot be burdened, then the people will look up in admiration and not scatter. Thus, they can perpetually serve the Soil and Grain as their sovereign.

The *Hóng Liè Jiě* [《鸿烈解》] recounts: *"When Jin attacked Chu, the army did not advance despite three withdrawals. The officials urged the king to strike. King Zhuang [庄王] said: 'In my father’s time, Jin did not attack Chu. Now, in my reign, Jin attacks Chu—this is my fault. How can I bring shame upon the state?' Wèi Dàfū [韦大夫] replied: 'In my father’s time, Jin did not attack Chu. Now, in my reign, Jin attacks Chu—this is my fault. I beg the king to strike.' The king wept bitterly, his tears flowing like rain, then ascended and bowed to Wèi Dàfū. When the people of Jin heard this, they said: 'The ruler and his minister both claim the fault as their own, and the king even humbles himself before his subject—we cannot attack such a state.' That night, they retreated and returned home."* This is an example of *"bearing the defilement of the state"*—the first instance of *"the Lord of the Soil and Grain."*

When founding a state, emperors and kings place the ancestral temple on the left and the Soil and Grain altar on the right. The ancestral temple honors the ancestors and aligns with Heaven; the Soil and Grain altar honors agriculture and the harvest. The *bèi* [备] and *qì* [弃] hexagrams [卦] signify the abundance that sustains the people’s livelihood—food is the foundation of life. Since food is essential to humanity, a state must first establish its Soil and Grain altar, and the ruler must serve as its guardian.

To bear the defilement of the state is to be called the *"King of All Under Heaven."* If a ruler can practice humility and softness, bearing the state’s ills without complaint, then all under Heaven will return to benevolence, and the six directions will rest in harmony—they are the *"King of All Under Heaven."* The commentary states: *"Mountains and rivers accept filth, and so too should a ruler bear defilement."*

「The *Hóngliè Jiě* [《鸿烈解》] states: During the time of Duke Jing of Song [宋景公], the wandering star [荧惑] was in the constellation of the Heart [心], and the Duke, fearful, summoned Zi Wei [子韦] and asked: *"What does the wandering star in the Heart portend?"*

Zi Wei replied: *"The wandering star is a Heavenly punishment [天罚]. The Heart is the Song realm’s celestial division [宋分野]. The calamity will soon fall upon the ruler. Though it may be transferred to the prime minister [宰相], such a deed would be inauspicious."*

The Duke said: *"The prime minister governs the state [治国家], and transferring death to him would be unpropitious."*

Zi Wei replied: *"It may be transferred to the people [民]."*

The Duke said: *"If the people die, who will serve as my subjects?"*

Zi Wei replied: *"It may be transferred to the year [岁]."*

The Duke said: *"The year is the people’s life [民之命]. If the year brings famine, the people will surely perish. As a ruler, to kill the people to save myself—who would still recognize me as their sovereign?"*

*"My fate is already sealed,"* the Duke declared, *"do not speak further."*

Zi Wei bowed twice and said: *"I dare congratulate you, my lord. Heaven is exalted yet hears the lowly, and you have spoken words befitting a ruler [君人之言]. Three times you have borne this punishment, and three times the wandering star will shift its course. Tonight it will move three stages [三舍], each stage seven *lǐ* [里], and thus your life will be extended by twenty-one years. I beg leave to lie in wait beneath your throne this very evening to observe it."*

Indeed, the star shifted three stages that night.

This is the *"bearing the state’s ills"* [受国不祥], the *"King of All Under Heaven"* [天下王].

Words are reversed in meaning.

He who practices **softness and weakness** [柔弱] may become a ruler [君主], but he who relies on **rigidity and strength** [刚强] will invite disaster.

The **Sage** [圣人] bears defilement and evil, yet eternally preserves the **Great Auspiciousness** [元吉].
The common folk delight in beauty and honor, yet ultimately meet ruin.

*"Bearing the state’s ills"* [受国不祥] aligns with the **Way** [道], yet the people’s hearts remain unfulfilled. Thus, it follows with *"harmonizing great grudges"* [和大怨].
To harmonize great grudges is to leave lingering grudges behind.

If a ruler cannot govern with **effortless humility** [无为谦弱], the people will grow greedy and contentious, leading to reckless youths who block the roads [道卫], their arms raised in defiance. Then the ruler must establish teachings and laws to restrain them—*those who kill shall die, those who wound shall be punished*.

To respond to grudges with harmony is to turn their resentment into greater resentment. It is better to have no grudges than to have grudges and yet respond with harmony.

To know only how to harmonize great grudges, yet not understand how these grudges arise in the first place—even if one responds with utmost fairness [至公], lingering grudges will remain.
This is like a carpenter’s apprentice [大匠] cutting wood; few will escape harming their hands.

But if one responds with **heartless virtue** [无心至德], it is nearly as if one were creating the world anew.
How then can one speak of goodness?

The **Sage and Worthies** [圣贤] originally used punishment and governance to harmonize grudges and evil, yet how can one speak of goodness when deceit and treachery only grow worse, and resentment and longing increase?

Thus the **Sage** holds the **left token** [左契], yet does not blame the people.

Lü Xīshēng [陆希声] said: *"In ancient times, people tied knots for agreements, yet none deceived; when they split wood for tokens, none violated them. This was because the Sage had no fixed intentions of his own—he took the people’s hearts as his own."*

The Sage’s heart and the people’s hearts were as inseparable as the **left and right tokens** [左右契].
When tokens were presented, they were united in trust, and the Sage did not blame the people. Thus, ruler and subject grew close, and grudges never took root.

Lǐ Róng [李荣] said: *"In ancient times, the Sage carved wooden tokens—rulers held the left, ministers the right—and united them as a pledge of trust. No further laws were needed, so the Sage did not blame the people. If he did not blame them, how could grudges arise?"*

Thus, the **virtuous** [有德] held tokens to ensure **harmony** [契], while the **unvirtuous** [无德] held the **right tokens** [彻].
**彻** [彻] means **permeation** [通], the **Way** [道].
**司** [司] means **to govern** [主].
The **virtuous** refers to rulers of the **Middle Ages** [中古], who had no written laws, only carved tokens and matching symbols as pledges of trust. The people followed of their own accord, so they were called **virtuous**.

The **unvirtuous** refers to rulers of the **Far Antiquity** [远古], whose virtue was so vast it had no name. All things followed their natural course—dwelling in caves, nesting in trees, each content in its place. Their rulers had no schemes or thoughts; they appeared alone at dawn, and this was not considered neglect. Their virtue was profound and their Way mysterious—no tokens were needed for governance, only to ensure **permeation** [通彻] was upheld. Thus they were called **unvirtuous**.

This is the saying of Du Guāngtíng [杜光庭].
The old saying held that **彻** [彻] meant **traces** [迹], or that it meant **establishing norms** [作轨]. But if laws are enforced to guide the people, they will bring exhaustion and decay—hence the term **unvirtuous**.

Now, **permeation** [通] is the more enduring interpretation.

Heaven’s **Way** [道] has no favorites—it aligns with those who are good.
Heaven’s **Way** has no bias—it bestows itself only upon the good, as the saying goes: *"The Heavenly net is vast and wide, though its mesh seems sparse, yet nothing escapes."* [天网恢恢，疏而不失]

Thus, those who practice **great goodness** [上善] naturally find their ways in accord.
Why would they need tokens or laws to blame the people?

This is the **Way** of the **Far Antiquity** [太古] once more.

To harmonize grudges is to leave grudges unresolved; to **cease grudges** [息怨] is to govern with **non-action** [无为].
To govern with **non-action** is to dwell in **smallness** [小] yet not covet, hence it follows with *"small state, few people"* [小国寡民].

In a **small state with few people**, even if they possess **tenfold or hundredfold tools** [什伯之器], they will not use them.
**什** [什] means **five** [伍], **伯** [伯] means **long** [长], and **器** [器] refers to **materials and tools** [材器].

A small state can defend itself; few people can sustain themselves.
This is the return to the **Far Antiquity** [太古].

Let the people have **units of five and ten** [部曲什伯], and command them to respect their ranks without encroaching upon one another.
Because the ruler governs with **non-action**, the people’s livelihoods flourish, and their materials and tools become grand and wondrous, yet unused.
This is the **ultimate governance** [至治].

Let the people **cherish life** [重死] and **not wander far** [不远徙].
If a ruler governs with **non-action**, his **virtue and transformation** [德化] will be pure.
If the people are simple and unadorned, they will not lightly court death—**rooted in their origins** [崇本], they will **abandon superficiality** [弃末].
They till the fields and weave cloth, each clinging to their ancestral homes and not straying.
Even the **Xuanhuang** [轩皇] and **Jiǔ** [几蘧] of old could not surpass this governance.

Though **boats and carriages** [舟舆] exist, they are unused.
*Wooden boats are hollowed to cross water*—this is for distant travel.
Yet this is for distant travel.
Now, for rulers who uphold the **Way**, in large states they seek only to **shelter the people** [兼畜人], in small states they seek only to **have the people serve them** [入事人].
They do not encroach upon one another, nor do they engage in trade.
Though they possess boats and **straight paths** [直], they abandon them and do not use them.

Zhuāngzǐ [庄子] said: *"In the age of **Great Virtue** [至德], mountains had no paths or tunnels, marshes had no boats or bridges. All things thrived in their natural state—this was the **Way** of their homeland."*

Though **armor and weapons** [甲兵] existed, they were never employed.
Weapons were originally set up to **punish treacherous ministers** [讨逆臣] and **ward off bandits** [御乱寇].」

「When the ruler acts with **non-action** [無為], the subjects observe their duties, the people do not stray, and the four borders remain at peace—then **armor and weapons** [甲兵] are not needed.

Let the people return to the use of **knotted cords** [結繩] for record-keeping.
The *Commentary on the Changes* [《系辞》] states: *"In the ancient times, knotted cords were used to govern affairs; later, the sages replaced them with writing and inscriptions."*
In the age of knotted cords, all were pure and simple.
Once writing was introduced, deceit and falsehood grew daily.
Now, to restore the people to their original simplicity and purity,
the ruler must make them return to the use of knotted cords.

Let their food be simple yet satisfying,
their clothing plain yet comfortable,
their dwellings humble yet secure,
and their customs joyful yet unadorned.
When the ruler above has no desires, the people below naturally become simple.
If their tastes are not indulged, the people will know contentment.
Even coarse vegetables and plain broth will fill their bellies with sweetness,
while hemp garments and deer-hide cloaks will keep them warm without finery.
Reed huts and wattle-and-daub walls will shield them from wind and rain,
and the torrid southern heat or the bitter northern cold will be endured with ease.
Thus, their customs remain unspoiled by artificiality,
and their land is free from pestilence and disease.

Neighboring states may be in sight,
and the sounds of roosters and dogs may be heard across borders—
yet the people live out their lives without ever visiting one another.
Neighboring states in sight means the territories are contiguous.
Roosters and dogs heard nearby means the people are plentiful and the lands close.
The people live until old age without ever facing enemies—
this is to say they are free from war and die of natural causes.
Their lack of interaction is like fish forgetting each other in rivers and lakes,
or people forgetting each other in the **Dao/Way** and its arts—
this is the harmony of the **Huxu and Lu tribes** [赫胥尊、卢氏] of ancient times.

When the people know contentment, their words are sincere yet unadorned,
so next comes the principle: *"Sincere speech is not beautiful."*
Sincere speech is plain and without flavor—like water.
Water’s plainness allows it to endure;
its lack of adornment is its natural quality.
Beautiful speech is sweet and overindulgent—like fine wine.
Sweetness makes it fleeting;
its lack of sincerity lies in its excess.
The **good** [善] do not argue,
while the argumentative are not **good**.
The **good** value inner virtue and noble action,
while those who do not argue return to their original simplicity.
The eloquent value persuasion,
while the unvirtuous are mired in right and wrong.
The wise are not learned in many things,
while the learned are not truly wise.
The wise understand the **Dao/Way**—
they grasp principles and roots,
seizing only what is essential.
Why need they be learned in many things?
This is called **"sufficiency"** [少則得].
The *Sutra of the Western Ascension* [《西升经》] says:
*"He who attains the One fulfills all things,
and without clinging, the spirits and gods submit."*

The learned are those who exhaustively study varied doctrines,
seeking only to prove their own views.
The unwise are those who accumulate knowledge only to be confused by it.
Zhuāngzǐ [庄子] said: *"When **virtue/power** [质] is lost to **learning** [文], and **learning** is pursued to excess, the mind drowns in partial truths."*

The sages do not hoard.
Having given to others, they grow ever more abundant;
having bestowed upon others, they grow ever wealthier.
Hoarding means storing and accumulating.
The sages’ **Dao/Way** benefits all under heaven,
yet they do not hoard **virtue/power** [德] to elevate themselves.
They accumulate yet also scatter,
nor do they store wealth to enrich themselves.
They neither delay their works externally
nor gather their wisdom internally—
both are harmonized, hence they are called **"non-hoarding"** [不積].
Zhuāngzǐ [庄子] said:
*"The **Dao/Way** of Heaven flows without accumulation,
thus all things flourish.
The **Dao/Way** of the **Sage Emperor** [帝道] flows without accumulation,
thus all under heaven is united.
The **Dao/Way** of the sages flows without accumulation,
thus all within the seas submit."*

The sages do not hoard because
they manifest **moral virtue** [道德] for the people.
The people receive their benefits, and the sages’ **virtue/power** [德] becomes ever clearer—
like a **weight of gold** [镒] illuminating others without concealing its own goodness or ill-will,
yet its brilliance never diminishes.
This is the metaphor for inner wisdom.

They distribute wealth to the poor,
and the poor, receiving their bounty, find their abundance ever greater—
like a well that endures constant drawing,
benefiting all without depletion,
yet its waters remain pure and inexhaustible.
This is the analogy for external works.

The opening chapter of the upper section
clarifies that the **Dao/Way** is the fundamental teaching,
and its body aligns perfectly with its subtlety.

The closing chapter of the upper section
expounds that **non-action** [無為] is **non-inaction** [無不為],
revealing the essence of the teaching,
and its application suits the **Dao/Way**.

Thus, the form and use are both forgotten,
and the beginning and end are seamlessly connected.

The opening chapter of the lower section
explains that **"having virtue/power" [有德] and "lacking virtue/power" [無德]** are the responses to teaching,
following the **Way**’s purification and refinement of the times.

The closing chapter of the lower section
uses the contrast of *"sincere speech"* [信言] and *"insincere speech"* [不信言]
to demonstrate the teaching’s application,
allowing things to manifest naturally as adorned or plain,
and thus conforming to the **Dao/Way**.

Therefore, by adapting to the times and allowing things to unfold naturally—
without opposing or contending—
it is to uphold their original **virtue/power** [元德]
and to align with **Creation** [造化],
to restore the **Dao/Way**’s constancy
and enter its **mysterious gate** [妙门].

The *True Classic of the Dao and Virtue* [《道德真经》] concludes the tenth scroll of the *Cherished Obscurity* [藏室慕微] section.»
